#LESSON 1|Why analysts code: your job in UK public health
::p
Welcome. This course takes you from **zero programming knowledge** to being able to do the two things every UK public health analyst is paid to do: **pull data out of databases with SQL**, and **analyse and present it with R**. Those are the tools you will meet in NHS trusts, Integrated Care Boards (ICBs), the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA), and local authority public health teams.

Why code at all, when Excel exists? Three reasons that come up in real UK health jobs:
- **Scale.** A single year of hospital admissions for one region can be millions of rows. Excel stops at 1,048,576 and slows down long before that. Databases don't.
- **Reproducibility.** A script is a written record of exactly what you did. When your director asks "how did you get this number?", you can show them. When next month's data arrives, you re-run the script instead of re-doing forty clicks.
- **Safety.** Manual copy-paste is where errors are born. A famous UK example: in October 2020, 15,841 positive COVID-19 test results were lost because an old Excel file format ran out of columns. Code with proper tools avoids whole categories of that mistake.
::h|Where you might work
::p
This course blends three settings, because most graduate analysts move between them:
- **NHS trust / ICB analytics** — waiting lists, A&amp;E performance, readmissions, service planning.
- **UKHSA / health protection** — outbreak surveillance, vaccination coverage, epidemiology.
- **Local authority public health** — population health needs, deprivation, prevention services, the Joint Strategic Needs Assessment (JSNA).

All three use SQL to query databases and, overwhelmingly, **R** for analysis — UKHSA and many NHS teams have standardised on R, and there is a thriving NHS-R community. That's why this course pairs SQL with R.
::note|info|How this course works
Every lesson has a short guide, real worked examples, at least one quiz, and an exercise with a hidden solution. Lessons 1–11 need no software at all. From lesson 11 you get a **live SQL playground** built into this page, with a synthetic health database — no installation needed. Everything here uses fictional, computer-generated data only.
::quiz
Q Roughly how many rows can one Excel worksheet hold before it simply cannot accept more?
- About 65 thousand
* About 1 million
- About 100 million
- There is no limit
! Excel's hard ceiling is 1,048,576 rows per sheet. Hospital activity data for even a modest region exceeds this quickly, which is one reason analysts use databases.
::quiz
Q Which of these is the strongest argument for scripting an analysis rather than doing it by hand in a spreadsheet?
- Scripts always run faster than Excel
- Scripts look more professional in meetings
* A script is a reproducible, checkable record of every step
- Spreadsheets cannot make charts
! Speed varies and charts exist in both. The decisive advantage is reproducibility: anyone (including future you) can re-run and audit the exact steps.
::ex
No code yet. Write down, in one sentence each, three questions a public health team in your area might ask of data (for example: "Is flu vaccination uptake lower in more deprived neighbourhoods?"). Keep them — you will be able to answer questions like these by the end of the course.
~~~text
Example answers:
1. Which GP practices have the lowest childhood MMR uptake, and is it linked to deprivation?
2. Are emergency readmissions within 30 days rising in older age groups?
3. Did A&E attendances for respiratory illness spike after the cold snap in January?
Any specific, answerable question naming a measure, a population and (ideally) a time period is a good answer.
::key
- UK public health analysis runs on SQL (to query databases) and R (to analyse and report).
- Code beats spreadsheets on scale, reproducibility and error-safety.
- You'll practise in a built-in SQL playground on synthetic data — nothing to install for the SQL half.
#LESSON 1|Files, folders and paths on Windows, Mac and Linux
::p
Before any code: you must be able to say **where a file lives**. Every file has a *path* — its address on the computer. Analysts constantly read data from paths and save results to paths, and the format differs by operating system.
::table
System|Example path|Separator|Home folder shorthand
Windows|C:\Users\asha\Documents\data\admissions.csv|Backslash \|%USERPROFILE%
macOS|/Users/asha/Documents/data/admissions.csv|Forward slash /|~
Linux|/home/asha/documents/data/admissions.csv|Forward slash /|~
::p
Two kinds of path:
- An **absolute path** starts from the root of the drive (`C:\` or `/`) and works from anywhere.
- A **relative path** starts from wherever you currently are. `data/admissions.csv` means "the admissions.csv inside the data folder, right here". Relative paths make projects portable — they still work when a colleague copies your folder.

**File extensions** (the bit after the dot) tell you the format: `.csv` is plain-text comma-separated data, `.xlsx` is Excel, `.R` is an R script, `.sql` is a SQL script, `.zip` is a compressed bundle. Windows and macOS hide extensions by default — turn that off (Windows: File Explorer ▸ View ▸ File name extensions; macOS: Finder ▸ Settings ▸ Advanced ▸ Show all filename extensions). Analysts need to see exactly what a file is.
::h|CSV: the analyst's workhorse
::p
A CSV is just text. Open one in Notepad/TextEdit and you'll see the first row is usually **column headers**, then one row per record, values separated by commas:
::code|text|admissions.csv (first 3 lines)
patient_id,admission_date,specialty
1042,2025-11-03,Respiratory
1187,2025-11-03,Cardiology
::p
Because CSV is plain text, every tool — SQL, R, Python, Excel — can read it. One trap for UK analysts: Excel loves to "help" by reformatting things. It turns `01/02/2025` into an ambiguous date and famously converted gene names like `MARCH1` into dates in genetics research. Keep master copies as CSV and do the analysis in code.
::quiz
Q Which of these is a *relative* path?
- C:\Users\sam\project\data\pop.csv
- /home/sam/project/data/pop.csv
* data/pop.csv
- D:\pop.csv
! It doesn't start from a drive root, so it's resolved relative to your current folder — which is exactly what makes shared projects portable.
::quiz
Q Your colleague on a Mac sends you a path /Users/jo/analysis/output.csv. What is the Windows-style equivalent structure?
- /Users/jo/analysis/output.csv works unchanged on Windows
* C:\Users\jo\analysis\output.csv
- C:/jo/output.csv
- Windows does not use paths
! Windows paths use a drive letter and backslashes. (Inside R you can actually use forward slashes on Windows too — a handy trick coming in Module 4.)
::ex
On your own machine, create a folder for this course containing two sub-folders, data and outputs. Then write out the absolute path to the data folder as your operating system would express it.
~~~text
Examples of correct answers:
Windows: C:\Users\<yourname>\Documents\ph-course\data
macOS:   /Users/<yourname>/Documents/ph-course/data
Linux:   /home/<yourname>/ph-course/data
The exact wording varies — what matters is: correct separator, starts at the root/drive, ends at the data folder.
::key
- A path is a file's address; Windows uses \ and a drive letter, Mac/Linux use /.
- Relative paths make projects portable; absolute paths pin an exact location.
- Show file extensions, and treat CSV as your safe, universal data format.
#LESSON 1|Meet the command line (PowerShell, Terminal, bash)
::p
The command line is a text window where you type instructions instead of clicking. You don't need to become a wizard, but every analyst needs the basics: installers, Git, and servers all speak command line. Each OS has one built in:
- **Windows:** press Start, type `PowerShell`, open **Windows PowerShell** (or Windows Terminal).
- **macOS:** press Cmd+Space, type `Terminal`.
- **Linux:** Ctrl+Alt+T opens a **bash** shell in most distributions.

The window shows a **prompt** and waits. You type a command, press Enter, it runs, and the prompt returns. Here is the same 4-step session — see where you are, list files, make a course folder, move into it — on each system:
::oscode|bash
@win
# Windows PowerShell
pwd                          # print working directory (where am I?)
ls                           # list files here (alias of Get-ChildItem)
mkdir ph-course              # make a new folder
cd ph-course                 # change directory into it
@mac
# macOS Terminal (zsh)
pwd                          # where am I?
ls                           # list files here
mkdir ph-course              # make a new folder
cd ph-course                 # move into it
@linux
# Linux (bash)
pwd                          # where am I?
ls -l                        # list files with details
mkdir ph-course              # make a new folder
cd ph-course                 # move into it
::p
Happily, PowerShell accepts the classic Unix names (`ls`, `pwd`, `cd`, `mkdir`), so the core vocabulary transfers across all three systems. A few more you'll use: `cd ..` goes *up* one level; `cp a.csv b.csv` copies a file (`copy` also works on Windows); `rm file` deletes — **carefully**, there is no recycle bin on the command line. Pressing **Tab** auto-completes names; pressing the **up arrow** recalls previous commands.
::note|warn|In NHS and government workplaces
On managed work laptops you often can't install software yourself and some terminals are restricted. That's normal — IT will install approved tools (this is why we practise on your own machine, and why the SQL playground in this page needs nothing installed).
::quiz
Q You are in /home/asha/ph-course and type cd .. — where are you now?
- /home/asha/ph-course/..
* /home/asha
- / (the root)
- Nowhere; it's an error
! Two dots mean "the parent folder", so you move up one level to /home/asha.
::quiz
Q Which command creates a new folder on all three systems as shown?
- newfolder
- create
* mkdir
- folder -n
! mkdir ("make directory") works in PowerShell, zsh and bash.
::ex
In your command line: create a folder called ph-course with sub-folders data and outputs inside it, then list the contents of ph-course to confirm. Write the commands you used.
~~~bash
mkdir ph-course
cd ph-course
mkdir data
mkdir outputs
ls          # should show: data  outputs
# (One-liner on Mac/Linux: mkdir -p ph-course/{data,outputs})
::key
- PowerShell (Windows), Terminal (macOS) and bash (Linux) share core commands: pwd, ls, cd, mkdir, cp, rm.
- Tab completes, up-arrow repeats, cd .. goes up.
- Command-line deletion is permanent — pause before rm.
#LESSON 1|Install your SQL toolkit: SQLite + DB Browser
::p
Real NHS databases mostly run **Microsoft SQL Server** (with some Oracle and, increasingly, cloud platforms). You can't install those casually — but you don't need to, because SQL the *language* is what matters, and a tiny free engine called **SQLite** speaks it. SQLite stores a whole database in one file and runs on anything. Pair it with **DB Browser for SQLite**, a free point-and-click app for opening databases and running queries.
::p
This page's built-in playground means you can do every SQL lesson **without installing anything**. But installing the real thing on your own computer is good practice for the workplace:
::oscode|bash
@win
# Option A (no command line): download the installer from
#   https://sqlitebrowser.org  ->  "DB Browser for SQLite - Standard installer for 64-bit Windows"
# Run it, accept defaults.

# Option B (PowerShell, if you have winget):
winget install -e --id DBBrowserForSQLite.DBBrowserForSQLite
# Verify: open "DB Browser for SQLite" from the Start menu
@mac
# Option A: download the .dmg from https://sqlitebrowser.org
# and drag "DB Browser for SQLite" into Applications.

# Option B (Terminal, if you use Homebrew):
brew install --cask db-browser-for-sqlite
# macOS also ships the sqlite3 command-line tool already:
sqlite3 --version
@linux
# Debian/Ubuntu:
sudo apt update
sudo apt install sqlitebrowser sqlite3

# Fedora:
sudo dnf install sqlitebrowser sqlite

# Verify:
sqlite3 --version
::p
Once installed, open DB Browser and click **New Database** ▸ save as `practice.db` in your ph-course folder ▸ Cancel the table wizard ▸ open the **Execute SQL** tab. That tab is where you'll paste queries from this course. The playground here and DB Browser both run SQLite, so everything transfers exactly.
::note|info|Dialect heads-up
SQL has small dialect differences, like British vs American spelling. This course teaches standard SQL that runs in SQLite, and Lesson 40 gives you a translation guide to SQL Server (T-SQL), which you'll meet in the NHS.
::quiz
Q Why is SQLite a sensible learning tool for someone heading into NHS analytics?
- The NHS runs entirely on SQLite
* The SQL language skills transfer, and SQLite needs no server or licence
- SQLite is faster than SQL Server for national datasets
- It is the only database that runs on Windows
! NHS platforms are mostly SQL Server, but ~95% of the SQL you write is identical. SQLite gives you that language in a single free file.
::quiz
Q In DB Browser for SQLite, where do you type and run queries?
- The Browse Data tab
- The Database Structure tab
* The Execute SQL tab
- The Edit Pragmas tab
! Execute SQL is the query window; Browse Data is for eyeballing tables.
::ex
Install DB Browser for SQLite (or confirm sqlite3 runs in your terminal). Create practice.db inside your ph-course folder. Then answer: what is the full path to that file on your machine?
~~~text
Example answers:
Windows: C:\Users\<you>\Documents\ph-course\practice.db
macOS:   /Users/<you>/Documents/ph-course/practice.db
Linux:   /home/<you>/ph-course/practice.db
If installation is blocked (e.g. a locked-down machine), that's fine — the in-page playground covers every SQL lesson.
::key
- SQLite = the SQL language in one free file; DB Browser = the friendly app around it.
- NHS reality is mostly SQL Server; the language transfers with minor dialect tweaks (Lesson 40).
- The in-page playground means no install is ever required to progress.
#LESSON 1|Install your analysis toolkit: R + RStudio
::p
**R** is the language; **RStudio Desktop** is the free app you'll write it in (made by Posit). Install R first, then RStudio. Both are free and run on all three systems.
::oscode|bash
@win
# 1) R: go to https://cran.r-project.org -> "Download R for Windows"
#    -> "base" -> "Download R 4.x.x for Windows". Run installer, accept defaults.
# 2) RStudio: https://posit.co/download/rstudio-desktop -> Windows installer.
# 3) Later, some packages need build tools; if prompted, install "Rtools"
#    from CRAN (same site, "Rtools" link).

# Or with winget in PowerShell:
winget install -e --id RProject.R
winget install -e --id Posit.RStudio
@mac
# 1) R from https://cran.r-project.org -> "Download R for macOS"
#    (pick the .pkg matching your chip: arm64 for Apple Silicon, x86_64 for Intel).
# 2) RStudio from https://posit.co/download/rstudio-desktop (.dmg).
# 3) If a package ever asks for developer tools, run:
xcode-select --install

# Or with Homebrew:
brew install --cask r rstudio
@linux
# Debian/Ubuntu (CRAN's build is more current than the distro's):
sudo apt update
sudo apt install --no-install-recommends r-base r-base-dev

# RStudio: download the .deb from https://posit.co/download/rstudio-desktop
sudo apt install ./rstudio-*-amd64.deb

# r-base-dev matters: many R packages compile from source on Linux.
::p
Open RStudio. You'll see four panes: the **Console** (bottom-left) runs code immediately; the **Source** editor (top-left) holds scripts you save; **Environment** (top-right) lists your data objects; and **Files/Plots/Help** (bottom-right). Type this in the Console and press Enter:
::code|r|Your first R commands
2 + 2
round(37.68492, 1)          # a function with two arguments
"Public health"             # text is written in quotes
::p
If you see `4`, `37.7`, and the text echoed back, everything works. One setting to change immediately: **Tools ▸ Global Options ▸ General ▸ untick "Restore .RData at startup" and set "Save workspace" to Never**. This forces every session to start clean, which is exactly what reproducible analysis needs.
::note|info|Can't install at work?
Many NHS organisations provide R/RStudio through an internal software centre, or host **Posit Workbench** in a browser. If your own laptop is the blocker, webR (R in the browser) exists for small experiments — but for this course a normal install is strongly recommended for the R modules.
::quiz
Q What is the correct install order and relationship?
- RStudio first; R is included inside it
* R first (the language), then RStudio (the editor that uses it)
- Either alone is enough; they are alternatives
- R is only for Linux; RStudio is only for Windows
! RStudio is an IDE — a workbench. It needs the R engine installed underneath.
::quiz
Q Why switch off "Restore .RData at startup" in RStudio?
- It makes R run faster on old laptops
* Clean sessions make results depend only on your script, not leftover objects
- It is required by NHS information governance
- It enables dark mode
! Reproducibility: if your script works in a fresh session, it will work for colleagues and for future you.
::ex
Install R and RStudio. In the Console, calculate how many days there are in 52 weeks, and use round() to round 365.25 / 7 to two decimal places. Paste both commands.
~~~r
52 * 7            # 364
round(365.25 / 7, 2)   # 52.18
::key
- Install R (engine) then RStudio (workbench); Linux users also want r-base-dev.
- Console = run now; Source = saved scripts; keep sessions clean (never save workspace).
- Functions look like name(argument1, argument2) — you just used round().
#LESSON 1|How data flows through UK health services
::p
Analysts add value by knowing **where numbers come from**. A simplified map of the UK (mainly English) health data landscape:
- **Primary care (GP practices):** every consultation, prescription and vaccination is coded (using **SNOMED CT** terms) in systems like EMIS and SystmOne. Extracts flow to national programmes and local shared-care records.
- **Secondary care (hospitals):** each admission generates a coded record — diagnoses in **ICD-10**, procedures in **OPCS-4**. Trusts submit these to the national **Secondary Uses Service (SUS)**, and cleaned versions become **Hospital Episode Statistics (HES)**. A&amp;E flows via the **Emergency Care Data Set (ECDS)**.
- **NHS England** holds national collections (SUS/HES, waiting lists, workforce) — it absorbed NHS Digital in 2023.
- **UKHSA** runs infectious disease surveillance: lab notifications via its **Second Generation Surveillance System (SGSS)**, outbreak data, immunisation coverage.
- **ONS** (Office for National Statistics) provides the denominators — population estimates, life expectancy — plus births, deaths and the Census.
- **Local authorities** hold public health services data (health visiting, sexual health, drug &amp; alcohol treatment) and use all the above for JSNAs.

The glue between these is a set of identifiers: the **NHS number** (a 10-digit unique patient identifier), **ODS codes** for organisations (every GP practice and trust has one), and geography codes such as **LSOA** (Lower layer Super Output Area — small neighbourhoods of ~1,500 people) which link health records to ONS population and deprivation data.
::table
Dataset|What it covers|Typical analyst use
SUS / HES|Hospital admissions & outpatients|Readmissions, activity trends, case-mix
ECDS|A&E attendances|Winter pressures, attendance patterns
SGSS (UKHSA)|Lab-confirmed infections|Outbreak detection, surveillance reports
GP extracts|Primary care events|Vaccination uptake, disease registers
ONS mid-year estimates|Population by age/sex/area|Denominators for every rate you compute
IMD|Deprivation rank for each LSOA|Health inequality analysis
::p
Public-facing versions of much of this live on UKHSA/DHSC's **Fingertips** platform (fingertips.phe.org.uk) — hundreds of indicators by local authority. Later you'll pull Fingertips data straight into R with one function call.
::quiz
Q A hospital diagnosis of pneumonia will be recorded in an admissions dataset using which coding system?
- SNOMED CT
* ICD-10
- OPCS-4
- ODS
! Hospital diagnoses use ICD-10 (pneumonia is J18.9 when unspecified); OPCS-4 codes procedures; SNOMED CT dominates primary care; ODS codes identify organisations.
::quiz
Q You need a denominator to turn a count of flu admissions into a rate per 100,000 residents. Which source provides it?
- SUS
- ECDS
* ONS mid-year population estimates
- SGSS
! Health datasets give numerators (events). ONS population estimates by age, sex and area are the standard denominator.
::ex
Match each question to the most likely primary data source (SUS/HES, ECDS, SGSS, GP extract, ONS): (a) How many measles cases were lab-confirmed last quarter? (b) What proportion of 5-year-olds have had two MMR doses? (c) Did emergency admissions for COPD rise last winter? (d) How many people aged 80+ live in each LSOA?
~~~text
(a) SGSS — lab-confirmed infections sit in UKHSA surveillance.
(b) GP extract — childhood vaccinations are recorded in primary care.
(c) SUS/HES — emergency admissions are hospital activity.
(d) ONS — small-area population estimates.
::key
- Numerators come from health systems (SUS/HES, ECDS, SGSS, GP data); denominators come from ONS.
- ICD-10 = hospital diagnoses; OPCS-4 = procedures; SNOMED CT = primary care; NHS number links a patient across systems.
- LSOAs link health data to population and deprivation — the backbone of inequalities analysis.
#LESSON 1|Information governance: the rules you must never break
::p
Health data is among the most sensitive information the state holds, and the UK regulates it hard. Getting this wrong isn't a style point — it can end careers and harm patients' trust. The legal frame: **UK GDPR** and the **Data Protection Act 2018**, plus the **common law duty of confidentiality**. In practice, four working concepts:
- **Personal data / PID** (person-identifiable data): anything that can identify a living person — name, address, date of birth, NHS number, or combinations that single someone out.
- **Pseudonymised data:** identifiers replaced by codes (e.g. NHS number swapped for a study ID). Safer, but legally still personal data, because someone holds the key.
- **Anonymised / aggregate data:** counts and rates that can't identify anyone. This is what leaves the building in reports.
- **The Caldicott Principles:** eight rules (justify the purpose; use the minimum necessary; access on a need-to-know basis…; the eighth: inform patients how data is used). Every NHS organisation has a **Caldicott Guardian** who adjudicates.
::h|Small numbers: the analyst's daily judgement
::p
Even aggregate tables can identify people. If your table says "2 cases of HIV in Little Bexton ward", locals might work out who. Standard UK practice is **statistical disclosure control**: suppress small counts (commonly anything from 1 to 7 is shown as `*` or "&lt;5" — thresholds vary by organisation; HES conventionally suppresses 1–7), and sometimes round others so the suppressed cell can't be back-calculated from totals. When in doubt: suppress, and ask your information governance (IG) team.
::note|warn|Three personal rules to adopt today
1) Never move patient-level data off approved systems — no emailing extracts, no USB sticks, no personal laptops, and (as Module 8 covers) **never pasting it into public AI tools**. 2) Never look up anyone you know — access is audited. 3) Publish aggregates, not people: apply small-number suppression before anything leaves your team.
::quiz
Q A dataset where NHS numbers have been replaced with random study IDs (and a key exists elsewhere) is best described as…
- Anonymised — GDPR no longer applies
* Pseudonymised — still personal data under UK GDPR
- Synthetic
- Public data
! Because re-identification is possible via the key, UK GDPR still treats it as personal data. Only truly irreversible aggregation/anonymisation escapes that.
::quiz
Q Your output table includes a cell showing 3 teenage pregnancies in one small village. Best practice is to…
- Publish it; 3 is a real number
- Delete the whole table
* Suppress the small count (e.g. show "<5") and check totals can't reveal it
- Round it up to 10
! Small-number suppression protects individuals in small areas. Also check row/column totals don't let readers reconstruct the suppressed value.
::ex
You've produced a ward-level table of drug-related deaths for a council report. Counts range from 0 to 14; four wards have counts of 1 or 2. List the steps you'd take before this table leaves your team.
~~~text
1. Apply the organisation's suppression rule (e.g. replace 1–4, or 1–7, with "<5"/"*").
2. Check totals and complementary cells can't be used to back-calculate suppressed values
   (secondary suppression if needed).
3. Confirm the output contains no names, dates of birth, or other identifiers.
4. Follow local sign-off: senior analyst review and, if unsure, the IG team/Caldicott Guardian.
5. Record what you did so the method is reproducible next time.
::key
- UK GDPR + DPA 2018 + confidentiality law govern all health data; Caldicott Principles operationalise them.
- Pseudonymised ≠ anonymised; only irreversible aggregation truly de-identifies.
- Suppress small numbers before publishing; never move PID outside approved systems.
#LESSON 1|Thinking like an analyst: from vague question to defensible answer
::p
The hardest part of analysis isn't code — it's turning "Are we doing OK on childhood jabs?" into something precise. Professionals use a repeatable chain: **Question → Data → Method → Output → Check**.

**1. Sharpen the question.** Force out the measure, population, place, time and comparison. Vague: "How's MMR uptake?" Sharp: "What percentage of children turning 5 in 2025/26 had received two MMR doses, by GP practice in our ICB, compared with the 95% WHO target?" A useful skeleton for indicators: numerator, denominator, time period, breakdown.

**2. Find the data — and its caveats.** Which system records it? How complete is it? Coding changes, service disruption (COVID years!), and lag all shape what you can claim.

**3. Choose the method.** Often just counting and dividing — carefully. Sometimes age-standardisation, confidence intervals, or a model (Module 6).

**4. Produce the output for the audience.** A director wants one chart and three sentences; a fellow analyst wants your code.

**5. Check.** Sanity-check magnitudes (does 40,000 A&amp;E attendances a month sound right for your trust's size?), reconcile against published figures, and get a second pair of eyes. In NHS analytics this is often formalised as **QA by a colleague** before release.
::note|info|Financial years — a very British trap
The NHS and most UK public services run on **financial years: 1 April to 31 March**, written 2025/26. School-age cohorts, GP payments and most performance data follow it. Always confirm whether "the year" means calendar or financial — mixing them is one of the commonest real-world errors, and you'll write code for it in Lessons 21 and 54.
::quiz
Q Which restatement of "Are A&E waits getting worse?" is analysis-ready?
- Is A&E slower than it used to be?
* What % of A&E attendances at our trust were admitted, transferred or discharged within 4 hours, each month for the last 3 financial years?
- How do patients feel about A&E waits?
- Is the NHS in crisis?
! It names the measure (4-hour standard %), the population (our trust's attendances), the time frame and the comparison (monthly trend).
::quiz
Q A report says "uptake in 2024" without qualification. As a UK analyst your first clarifying question is…
- Which font was used
* Calendar year 2024 or financial year 2024/25?
- Was it Excel or R
- Who is the Caldicott Guardian
! Calendar vs financial year changes the cohort and the answer. Establish it before comparing anything.
::ex
Sharpen this request from a councillor into an analysis-ready question: "Is smoking still a problem in poor areas?" Identify measure, population, geography, time and comparison.
~~~text
One good version:
"What was the smoking prevalence (% of adults 18+ who currently smoke, from the
Annual Population Survey) in 2024/25, for each electoral ward / LSOA-derived area
in the borough, compared between the most and least deprived IMD quintiles,
and how has the gap changed over the last five years?"
Measure: adult smoking prevalence.  Population: adults 18+.  Geography: wards or
IMD quintiles within the borough.  Time: 2024/25 plus 5-year trend.
Comparison: most vs least deprived.
::key
- Question → Data → Method → Output → Check. Write the sharp question down before touching code.
- Every indicator = numerator + denominator + period + breakdown.
- UK health data runs April–March; always state which "year" you mean.
#LESSON 1|Why databases beat spreadsheets (and what a database is)
::p
A **relational database** stores data in **tables** (rows and columns), with defined **relationships** between them. Instead of one giant sheet where the patient's name is repeated on every admission row, a database keeps a `patients` table (one row per person) and an `admissions` table (one row per admission) joined by `patient_id`. This design — **normalisation** — means each fact is stored once, so it can't drift out of sync.
::table
Concept|Spreadsheet world|Database world
A dataset|Worksheet|Table
One record|Row|Row
One field|Column (A, B, C…)|Named, typed column
Identity|Whatever's in column A|Primary key (unique per row)
Linking|VLOOKUP per cell|JOIN on keys, once, declaratively
Rules|Hope|Constraints (types, NOT NULL, uniqueness)
::p
Three database superpowers matter for health data:
- **Types and constraints.** A column declared as DATE can't quietly hold "N/A" or "see notes"; a primary key can't duplicate. The database refuses bad data at the door.
- **Concurrency and audit.** Fifty analysts can query the warehouse at once without emailing `final_v7_REAL.xlsx` around.
- **Scale.** Millions of rows are routine.

The language for asking questions of relational databases is **SQL — Structured Query Language** (say "sequel" or spell it out; both are fine). It's *declarative*: you describe **what** you want ("emergency admissions for over-65s in March, counted by specialty") and the engine works out how to fetch it. SQL has been an ISO standard since 1987, which is why skills you learn on SQLite transfer to the NHS's SQL Server, to Oracle, and to modern cloud warehouses.
::quiz
Q In a well-designed health database, a patient's date of birth is stored…
- On every admission row, for convenience
* Once, in the patients table, linked to admissions by patient_id
- In a separate file per year
- In the admissions table only
! One fact, one place. Repeating it per admission invites contradictions (three different DOBs for one person is a classic data-quality find).
::quiz
Q SQL is described as "declarative". That means…
- You must specify the exact loop the computer runs
* You state what result you want; the engine decides how to compute it
- It only works on Microsoft products
- Queries must be written in capital letters
! You declare the result's shape; the query optimiser picks the retrieval strategy. (Capitals for keywords are a style convention, not a rule.)
::ex
A team tracks flu vaccinations in one spreadsheet with columns: patient name, DOB, GP practice, practice phone number, vaccination date, batch. Sketch a better two- or three-table database design and say which columns go where.
~~~text
patients:        patient_id (primary key), name, dob, practice_code
gp_practices:    practice_code (primary key), practice_name, phone
vaccinations:    vaccination_id (primary key), patient_id, vaccination_date, batch
Links: vaccinations.patient_id -> patients.patient_id;
       patients.practice_code -> gp_practices.practice_code.
Each fact now lives once: change a practice phone number in one row, not thousands.
::key
- Relational databases = typed tables linked by keys; each fact stored once.
- Constraints stop bad data at entry; spreadsheets only hope.
- SQL is the standard, declarative language for querying them — and it transfers everywhere.
#LESSON 1|Anatomy of a table: rows, columns, types and keys
::p
Time to read a real table definition. Throughout this course you'll use a synthetic database for the fictional county of **Barsetshire** — three local authorities (Barchester, Millford, Kingsbay), twelve GP practices, ~600 patients. Here is its `patients` table:
::code|sql|The patients table (definition)
CREATE TABLE patients (
  patient_id     INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,   -- unique per person
  sex            TEXT,                  -- 'F' or 'M'
  birth_date     TEXT,                  -- ISO format 'YYYY-MM-DD'
  ethnicity      TEXT,                  -- ONS high-level groups
  lsoa_code      TEXT,                  -- links to lsoa_imd
  practice_code  TEXT                   -- links to gp_practices
);
::p
Reading it: each **column** has a name and a **type**. SQLite keeps types simple — `INTEGER` (whole numbers), `REAL` (decimals), `TEXT` (strings, including dates stored as ISO `YYYY-MM-DD`). Bigger systems add richer types (`DATE`, `DECIMAL(10,2)`, `VARCHAR(50)`), but the idea is identical. The **PRIMARY KEY** is the column guaranteed unique and non-empty — the row's identity. Columns that point at another table's key (`lsoa_code`, `practice_code`) are **foreign keys**: the joins you’ll write in Lesson 25 run along them.

One row of this table might be: `(1042, 'F', '1958-03-14', 'White', 'E01000123', 'P81004')` — a woman born March 1958, living in LSOA E01000123, registered at practice P81004. Notice what's *not* here: no name, no address. This mirrors how analysts usually receive data — pseudonymised, with `patient_id` standing in for identity.
::note|info|Why store dates as YYYY-MM-DD?
ISO 8601 dates sort correctly as plain text ('2025-03-01' < '2025-11-30'), are unambiguous internationally (no 03/04 confusion), and every tool parses them. UK analysts constantly convert *from* DD/MM/YYYY *into* ISO — you'll learn the code for that in both SQL and R.
::quiz
Q Which column in patients is a foreign key?
- patient_id
- birth_date
* practice_code
- sex
! practice_code points to the primary key of gp_practices — that's what makes it foreign. patient_id is this table's own primary key.
::quiz
Q Why would '02/03/2025' be a risky way to store a date?
* It's ambiguous — 2 March or 3 February depending on convention — and sorts wrongly as text
- Slashes are illegal in databases
- It uses too much storage
- Dates cannot be stored as text
! DD/MM vs MM/DD ambiguity has caused real reporting errors; and as text, '02/03/2025' sorts before '01/04/2024'. ISO YYYY-MM-DD fixes both.
::ex
Design a CREATE TABLE statement for lab_results holding: a unique result id, the patient it belongs to, the test name, a numeric value, the units, and the date taken. Choose sensible types and mark the keys.
~~~sql
CREATE TABLE lab_results (
  result_id   INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,
  patient_id  INTEGER,          -- foreign key -> patients.patient_id
  test        TEXT,
  value       REAL,
  units       TEXT,
  date_taken  TEXT              -- ISO 'YYYY-MM-DD'
);
-- (This is, in fact, almost exactly the table in your playground.)
::key
- A table = named, typed columns; a primary key gives each row identity; foreign keys create relationships.
- SQLite types: INTEGER, REAL, TEXT — dates as ISO text.
- Analyst-facing data is usually pseudonymised: IDs, not names.
#LESSON 1|Your first query — and how to use the playground
::p
Everything is in place. This page contains a **live SQL engine** with the Barsetshire database loaded: eight tables of synthetic patients, admissions, vaccinations, lab results, deaths, GP practices, deprivation scores and population. Open it any time from the menu (**▶ SQL playground**) or with the **Run ▶** button on SQL examples like the one below.
::p
The playground has: a query box, a **Run** button, table-name chips that show each table's columns, and a **Reset database** button if you ever break something (you genuinely can't break anything important — it rebuilds in a second). Your first query:
::code|sqltry|Run your first query
SELECT patient_id, sex, birth_date
FROM patients
LIMIT 5;
::p
Read it aloud: *"Select these three columns from the patients table; give me just 5 rows."* Press Run and five synthetic patients appear. Three habits to start now:
- **Keywords in CAPITALS** (`SELECT`, `FROM`, `LIMIT`) and names in lower case — pure convention, but it makes queries readable, and readability is a professional skill.
- **End statements with a semicolon.** Optional for single statements in some tools, required in scripts.
- **LIMIT while exploring.** Real tables have millions of rows; asking for all of them by accident is the classic new-analyst move. Peek first.

Also try the chips: tap `admissions` to see its columns, then adapt the query yourself (`SELECT * FROM admissions LIMIT 5;` — the `*` means "all columns").
::note|study|Study rhythm for this course
Each lesson takes 20–40 minutes. A sustainable pace is one or two lessons a day — the course is a marathon of 105 lessons, and skills compound only if you type the code yourself. Answer every quiz, attempt every exercise *before* revealing the solution, and re-run yesterday's queries from memory as a warm-up.
::quiz
Q In SELECT patient_id, sex FROM patients LIMIT 5; what does LIMIT 5 do?
- Shows only 5 columns
* Returns at most 5 rows
- Deletes all but 5 rows
- Sorts the first 5 values
! LIMIT caps the number of rows returned. It never changes the stored data — SELECT is read-only.
::quiz
Q What does the asterisk mean in SELECT * FROM admissions?
- All rows
* All columns
- A multiplication
- The primary key only
! * = every column. Handy for a quick look; in production queries you'll name columns explicitly.
::ex
In the playground: return the first 10 rows of the gp_practices table, all columns. Then modify it to show only practice_name and la_name. Write both queries.
~~~sql
SELECT * FROM gp_practices LIMIT 10;

SELECT practice_name, la_name
FROM gp_practices
LIMIT 10;
::key
- SELECT columns FROM table LIMIT n — your exploration pattern for every new table.
- SELECT never modifies data; you cannot break the playground (and there's a Reset anyway).
- Type everything yourself; the muscle memory is the point.
#LESSON 2|SELECT and FROM: choosing your columns
::p
Every query you will ever write starts the same way: `SELECT` (which columns) `FROM` (which table). The database returns a result set — itself a little table — and changes nothing on disk. You can also *compute* new columns on the fly and rename things with `AS`:
::code|sqltry|Selecting, computing, renaming
-- Comments start with two dashes: the engine ignores them.
SELECT
  patient_id,
  birth_date,
  sex            AS patient_sex,          -- rename a column
  'Barsetshire'  AS county                -- a constant column
FROM patients
LIMIT 8;
::p
Points to absorb:
- **Column order in your SELECT controls output order.** The table's own order is irrelevant.
- **AS creates an alias** — the result column's display name. If an alias needs a space, wrap it in double quotes: `AS "Patient sex"` (works, but analysts usually stick to snake_case names and prettify later in R).
- **Comments** (`-- like this`) are how professionals leave notes for colleagues and future selves. A query without comments is a query someone will misread.
- Layout is free-form: SQL ignores line breaks and extra spaces. One clause per line, indented, is the readable house style you'll see in NHS code.

Try editing the query in the playground: add `ethnicity`, remove `sex`, change the alias. Errors are welcome — read the message, fix, re-run. A typo like `SELCT` produces `near "SELCT": syntax error`, which tells you exactly where to look.
::quiz
Q What does SELECT birth_date AS dob FROM patients do to the stored table?
- Renames the column permanently
- Deletes the other columns
* Nothing — it only labels the output column dob for this query
- Creates a new table called dob
! SELECT is read-only. AS affects only the result set you're looking at.
::quiz
Q Which line is a valid SQL comment?
- // fetch patients
- # fetch patients
* -- fetch patients
- &lt;!-- fetch patients --&gt;
! Standard SQL comments are two dashes (SQLite and SQL Server also accept /* block comments */). The others belong to different languages.
::ex
Write a query returning, from admissions: the admission_id renamed to spell_id, the specialty, and a constant column data_source containing the text 'SUS'. Limit to 6 rows.
~~~sql
SELECT
  admission_id AS spell_id,
  specialty,
  'SUS'        AS data_source
FROM admissions
LIMIT 6;
::key
- SELECT columns FROM table; output order follows your SELECT list.
- AS = alias for readability; -- = comment; layout is for humans.
- SELECT never alters data — experiment freely.
#LESSON 2|WHERE: filtering rows with conditions
::p
`WHERE` keeps only the rows that satisfy a condition — it is how "all admissions" becomes "emergency admissions to Respiratory since April". The comparison operators: `=`, `<>` (not equal; `!=` also works), `<`, `<=`, `>`, `>=`. Text values go in **single quotes** and, in most engines, are case-sensitive — `'emergency'` will not match `'Emergency'`.
::code|sqltry|Filtering admissions
SELECT admission_id, patient_id, admission_date, specialty
FROM admissions
WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'
  AND admission_date >= '2025-04-01'
  AND specialty      IN ('Respiratory', 'General Medicine')
LIMIT 10;
::p
Combine conditions with `AND` (both must hold), `OR` (either), `NOT` (negate). Two power tools:
- `IN (list)` — cleaner than chained ORs, exactly as above.
- `BETWEEN a AND b` — inclusive range: `value BETWEEN 48 and 58` includes both ends. For dates, `admission_date BETWEEN '2025-04-01' AND '2026-03-31'` covers financial year 2025/26 (thanks to ISO date text sorting correctly).

**The precedence trap:** `AND` binds tighter than `OR`. `WHERE specialty = 'Cardiology' OR specialty = 'Respiratory' AND admission_method = 'Emergency'` means Cardiology-anything OR emergency-Respiratory — probably not what you meant. **When mixing AND with OR, always add parentheses:**
::code|sql|Parentheses make intent explicit
WHERE (specialty = 'Cardiology' OR specialty = 'Respiratory')
  AND admission_method = 'Emergency'
::quiz
Q Which WHERE clause returns admissions in financial year 2024/25 only?
- WHERE admission_date BETWEEN '2024-01-01' AND '2024-12-31'
* WHERE admission_date BETWEEN '2024-04-01' AND '2025-03-31'
- WHERE admission_date = 2024/25
- WHERE year = '2024/25'
! UK financial years run 1 April to 31 March. ISO-formatted text dates make BETWEEN safe here.
::quiz
Q WHERE sex = 'F' OR sex = 'M' AND imd_decile = 1 — without parentheses this returns…
- Only decile-1 patients of either sex
* All females, plus decile-1 males
- All males, plus decile-1 females
- A syntax error
! AND evaluates first, so the condition is: sex='F' OR (sex='M' AND decile=1). Parentheses would fix the ambiguity.
::ex
From lab_results, return rows where the test is 'HbA1c' and the value is 48 or above (the diabetes diagnostic threshold in mmol/mol), taken on or after 1 April 2025. Show patient_id, value and date_taken, limited to 10 rows.
~~~sql
SELECT patient_id, value, date_taken
FROM lab_results
WHERE test = 'HbA1c'
  AND value >= 48
  AND date_taken >= '2025-04-01'
LIMIT 10;
::key
- WHERE filters rows; text in 'single quotes'; <> means not-equal.
- IN and BETWEEN (inclusive) tidy up common patterns.
- Mixing AND/OR without parentheses is the classic silent logic bug.
#LESSON 2|ORDER BY and LIMIT: sorting and topping
::p
Result sets have **no guaranteed order** unless you ask for one — an engine may return rows in any sequence it finds convenient, and that sequence can change between runs. If order matters (it usually does in reports), say so with `ORDER BY`:
::code|sqltry|Largest practices first
SELECT practice_name, la_name, list_size
FROM gp_practices
ORDER BY list_size DESC,      -- biggest first
         practice_name ASC    -- tie-break alphabetically
LIMIT 5;
::p
- `ASC` (ascending) is the default; `DESC` flips it.
- Multiple sort keys act as tie-breakers, left to right.
- You can order by a column you didn't select, or by an alias you created in the SELECT.
- `ORDER BY` + `LIMIT` = "top N": the query above is *the five largest practices*. Add `OFFSET 5` to skip the first five (rows 6–10) — occasionally useful for paging.

**Dialect note you'll need in the NHS:** SQL Server has no `LIMIT`. Its idiom is `SELECT TOP 5 ... ORDER BY ...`, or the standard `OFFSET 0 ROWS FETCH NEXT 5 ROWS ONLY` after `ORDER BY`. Same idea, different spelling — Lesson 40 collects all of these.

Sorting text follows character order: for ISO dates that's chronological (the whole reason we store them that way), but beware numbers stored as text: `'10'` sorts before `'2'`. If a "numeric" column sorts oddly, its type is text — a data-quality clue.
::quiz
Q Without an ORDER BY, the row order of a SELECT is…
- Always the order rows were inserted
- Always primary-key order
* Unspecified — the engine may return any order
- Alphabetical by the first column
! Order is only guaranteed when you specify it. Never rely on "it came out sorted last time".
::quiz
Q Which query gives the 3 most recent admissions?
- SELECT * FROM admissions LIMIT 3;
* SELECT * FROM admissions ORDER BY admission_date DESC LIMIT 3;
- SELECT TOP admissions BY date;
- SELECT * FROM admissions ORDER BY admission_date ASC LIMIT 3;
! Sort newest-first (DESC), then cap at 3. ASC would give the three oldest; LIMIT alone gives three arbitrary rows.
::ex
List the 10 oldest patients: patient_id, sex, birth_date, sorted so the earliest birth date comes first, with ties broken by patient_id.
~~~sql
SELECT patient_id, sex, birth_date
FROM patients
ORDER BY birth_date ASC, patient_id ASC
LIMIT 10;
::key
- No ORDER BY = no promised order; state your sort in anything that gets reported.
- ORDER BY col DESC LIMIT n = "top n"; SQL Server spells it TOP n.
- Text-sorting quirks (('10' < '2')) reveal columns with the wrong type.
#LESSON 2|DISTINCT: seeing and counting unique values
::p
"What specialties do we actually have in this data?" is a first-day-with-a-new-dataset question, and `DISTINCT` answers it — it collapses duplicate rows in the output:
::code|sqltry|What values exist?
SELECT DISTINCT specialty
FROM admissions
ORDER BY specialty;
::p
Used on **several columns**, DISTINCT returns unique *combinations*: `SELECT DISTINCT specialty, admission_method FROM admissions` lists each specialty–method pairing that occurs. This is your profiling tool: run it on any code-like column (ethnicity, vaccine, admission_method) the moment you meet a new table, because it exposes the messy truth — stray values like `'emergency '` with a trailing space, `'RESP'` vs `'Respiratory'`, or mysterious codes needing a lookup.

DISTINCT also pairs with COUNT (formally taught in Lesson 22) for the single most common analyst question — *how many people, not how many rows*:
::code|sqltry|Rows vs people
SELECT
  COUNT(*)                    AS admission_rows,
  COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id)  AS distinct_patients
FROM admissions;
::p
Those two numbers differ because some patients are admitted more than once. Confusing them is one of the most consequential real-world errors in health analysis: "10,000 admissions" is activity; "10,000 patients" is people. Commissioners fund the first; epidemiology counts the second.
::quiz
Q A vaccination table has 3 rows for one child (two MMR doses and a flu jab). SELECT DISTINCT patient_id FROM vaccinations returns how many rows for this child?
* 1
- 2
- 3
- 0
! DISTINCT collapses duplicates: one unique patient_id, however many events.
::quiz
Q Your trust reports "42,000 A&E attendances" and a colleague says "42,000 patients used A&E". Why might the colleague be wrong?
- Attendances are always fewer than patients
* Some people attend more than once, so distinct patients ≤ attendances
- A&E does not record patients
- The two numbers are always equal
! Frequent attenders mean the same person appears in many rows. COUNT(*) counts events; COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id) counts people.
::ex
Profile the vaccinations table: (1) list the distinct vaccine names; (2) list the distinct vaccine + dose_number combinations, ordered by vaccine then dose.
~~~sql
SELECT DISTINCT vaccine
FROM vaccinations
ORDER BY vaccine;

SELECT DISTINCT vaccine, dose_number
FROM vaccinations
ORDER BY vaccine, dose_number;
::key
- DISTINCT reveals the actual values (and value combinations) a column contains — run it on every new categorical column.
- COUNT(*) = rows/events; COUNT(DISTINCT id) = people. Know which your question needs.
- Odd values surfaced by DISTINCT are data-quality leads, not annoyances.
#LESSON 2|Working with text: LIKE, case, trimming and joining strings
::p
Health data is full of text — codes, names, free-ish labels — and text is where mess lives. Core tools:

**Pattern matching with LIKE.** `%` matches any run of characters, `_` matches exactly one:
::code|sqltry|ICD-10 chapter hunting
-- All respiratory-chapter diagnoses (ICD-10 codes starting with J)
SELECT DISTINCT primary_diagnosis
FROM admissions
WHERE primary_diagnosis LIKE 'J%'
ORDER BY primary_diagnosis;
::p
`LIKE 'J1_'` would match J10–J19 style three-character codes only. In SQLite, LIKE ignores case for plain letters; in SQL Server it depends on the database's collation — so professionals often normalise case explicitly rather than rely on settings:

**Case and whitespace.** `UPPER(x)` / `LOWER(x)` standardise case; `TRIM(x)` strips leading/trailing spaces (`LTRIM`/`RTRIM` for one side). The defensive comparison pattern worth memorising: `WHERE UPPER(TRIM(specialty)) = 'RESPIRATORY'` — it survives `' respiratory '` and `'RESPIRATORY'` alike.

**Slicing and joining.** `SUBSTR(text, start, length)` extracts characters (start counts from 1): `SUBSTR(primary_diagnosis, 1, 1)` gives the ICD-10 chapter letter. Concatenation is `||` in standard SQL/SQLite: `'Practice ' || practice_code`. (SQL Server uses `+` or `CONCAT()`; another one for the Lesson 40 phrasebook.) `LENGTH(x)` returns character count — great for validation, e.g. NHS numbers must be 10 digits.
::code|sqltry|Building labels
SELECT
  practice_code,
  UPPER(la_name)                                   AS la_upper,
  practice_name || ' (' || practice_code || ')'    AS label
FROM gp_practices
LIMIT 6;
::quiz
Q WHERE lsoa_code LIKE 'E01%' matches…
- Codes ending in E01
* Codes beginning E01, of any length
- Exactly the code E01
- Codes containing exactly one E
! % is a wildcard for any (possibly empty) sequence of characters after E01 — this pattern selects English LSOA codes.
::quiz
Q Which expression reliably matches 'Elective', 'ELECTIVE' and ' elective ' all at once?
- admission_method = 'Elective'
- admission_method LIKE 'Elective'
* UPPER(TRIM(admission_method)) = 'ELECTIVE'
- TRIM(admission_method)
! Normalise both case and whitespace before comparing. It's the standard defence against inconsistently entered categories.
::ex
From admissions, extract the ICD-10 chapter letter of each primary_diagnosis as a column called chapter, and count rows per chapter using SELECT chapter, COUNT(*) ... GROUP BY chapter (a preview of Lesson 23). Order by the count, largest first.
~~~sql
SELECT
  SUBSTR(primary_diagnosis, 1, 1) AS chapter,
  COUNT(*)                        AS n
FROM admissions
GROUP BY SUBSTR(primary_diagnosis, 1, 1)
ORDER BY n DESC;
::key
- LIKE with % and _ finds patterns; leading-letter patterns navigate ICD-10 chapters.
- UPPER/LOWER + TRIM = defensive comparisons against messy text.
- SUBSTR slices, || concatenates (SQL Server: + / CONCAT), LENGTH validates.
#LESSON 2|Numbers: arithmetic, rounding and the integer-division trap
::p
SQL does arithmetic in the SELECT list with `+ - * /` and `%` (remainder in many engines). You'll use it constantly to build rates and percentages. But there is one trap that has produced wrong numbers in real dashboards: **integer division**. In many databases (SQL Server prominently; SQLite included), dividing an integer by an integer discards the remainder:
::code|sqltry|The trap, demonstrated
SELECT
  7 / 2                          AS int_division,    -- 3, not 3.5!
  7 / 2.0                        AS with_decimal,    -- 3.5
  CAST(7 AS REAL) / 2            AS with_cast,       -- 3.5
  100.0 * 37 / 120               AS pct_correct,     -- 30.83...
  ROUND(100.0 * 37 / 120, 1)     AS pct_rounded      -- 30.8
FROM patients LIMIT 1;   -- (any one-row source works for a demo)
::p
The professional habit: when computing any proportion, **make one operand a decimal** — either write `100.0` / multiply by `1.0`, or `CAST(numerator AS REAL)`. `CAST(x AS type)` is the general conversion tool (`REAL`, `INTEGER`, `TEXT` in SQLite; `FLOAT`, `INT`, `DECIMAL(10,2)`, `VARCHAR` in SQL Server).

`ROUND(x, n)` rounds to n decimal places — apply it **only at the final display step**, never mid-calculation, or rounding errors accumulate. Also useful: `ABS(x)` for absolute values (e.g. difference between two readings). One more subtlety for later modules: rounding rules at .5 differ slightly between engines and R (R uses "round half to even"), which is why reconciliations sometimes differ by a penny — knowing this saves you an afternoon someday.
::quiz
Q In SQLite/SQL Server, SELECT 45 / 60 returns…
* 0
- 0.75
- 75
- An error
! Integer ÷ integer = integer: the .75 is truncated to 0. Write 45 / 60.0 or CAST one side to REAL to get 0.75.
::quiz
Q Which expression correctly computes a percentage of emergency admissions as a decimal like 61.3?
- emergency / total * 100
- ROUND(emergency / total, 1) * 100
* ROUND(100.0 * emergency / total, 1)
- CAST(emergency AS TEXT) / total
! The 100.0 forces decimal arithmetic before the division; ROUND then trims for display. The first two fall into integer division if both columns are integers.
::ex
Each GP practice has a list_size. Write a query showing practice_name, list_size, and list_size as a percentage of 100000 (a notional ICB population), rounded to 2 decimal places, ordered largest first, top 5.
~~~sql
SELECT
  practice_name,
  list_size,
  ROUND(100.0 * list_size / 100000, 2) AS pct_of_icb
FROM gp_practices
ORDER BY pct_of_icb DESC
LIMIT 5;
::key
- Integer ÷ integer truncates: force decimals with 100.0, 1.0 * , or CAST(... AS REAL).
- ROUND(x, n) at the display step only; ABS for magnitudes; CAST converts types.
- Every rate and percentage you ever publish passes through this lesson.
#LESSON 2|NULL: the meaning of missing
::p
`NULL` is SQL's marker for **no value** — not zero, not an empty string, but *unknown/absent*. In health data NULLs are everywhere and they *mean* things: a NULL discharge_date can mean the patient is still in a bed; a NULL date_of_death means (as far as this system knows) the person is alive. Treating NULL casually produces silently wrong counts, so learn its three rules:

**1. NULL compares as unknown.** `discharge_date = NULL` is never true — not even for NULL rows — because nothing equals unknown. The correct tests are `IS NULL` and `IS NOT NULL`:
::code|sqltry|Who is still in hospital?
SELECT admission_id, patient_id, admission_date, specialty
FROM admissions
WHERE discharge_date IS NULL
ORDER BY admission_date;
::p
**2. NULL propagates through calculations.** `value + NULL` is NULL; `'text' || NULL` is NULL. One missing component poisons the expression.

**3. NULL is invisible to both sides of a filter.** `WHERE ethnicity <> 'White'` excludes NULL ethnicities too — rows that are neither equal nor not-equal simply drop out. If you mean "non-White *or unknown*", say `WHERE ethnicity <> 'White' OR ethnicity IS NULL`.

The repair tool is `COALESCE(a, b, c, …)` — it returns the first non-NULL argument. `COALESCE(ethnicity, 'Not recorded')` gives reports a tidy label; `COALESCE(discharge_date, date('now'))` treats current inpatients as "discharged today" for a length-of-stay-so-far calculation. (SQL Server also offers `ISNULL(a,b)`; COALESCE is the standard and takes any number of arguments.)
::quiz
Q Which query correctly finds patients with no recorded ethnicity?
- WHERE ethnicity = NULL
- WHERE ethnicity = 'NULL'
* WHERE ethnicity IS NULL
- WHERE ethnicity == none
! Equality with NULL is never true; IS NULL is the dedicated test. 'NULL' in quotes is just a four-letter word.
::quiz
Q A table has 100 rows: 60 with ethnicity 'White', 30 'Asian', 10 NULL. How many rows does WHERE ethnicity <> 'White' return?
- 40
* 30
- 10
- 100
! The 10 NULL rows satisfy neither = nor <> — they vanish from both filters. This silent exclusion is the classic NULL bug.
::ex
Write a query listing admission_id, admission_date, and a column discharge_shown that displays the discharge_date, or the text 'Still in hospital' where it is missing. Hint: COALESCE mixes types happily in SQLite.
~~~sql
SELECT
  admission_id,
  admission_date,
  COALESCE(discharge_date, 'Still in hospital') AS discharge_shown
FROM admissions
ORDER BY admission_date DESC
LIMIT 12;
::key
- NULL = unknown: test with IS NULL / IS NOT NULL, never = NULL.
- NULL poisons arithmetic and escapes both sides of <> filters — count your NULLs before filtering.
- COALESCE substitutes a fallback; in health data, first ask what the NULL means clinically.
#LESSON 2|CASE WHEN: categorising on the fly
::p
`CASE WHEN` is SQL's if-then-else, and it is the tool behind every age band, flag and RAG rating you'll ever build. Conditions are checked **top to bottom**; the first match wins; `ELSE` catches everything left (omit it and non-matches become NULL).
::code|sqltry|Flagging and banding
SELECT
  patient_id,
  test,
  value,
  CASE
    WHEN value >= 48             THEN 'Diabetes range'
    WHEN value BETWEEN 42 AND 47 THEN 'Pre-diabetes'
    WHEN value IS NULL           THEN 'No result'
    ELSE 'Normal'
  END AS hba1c_band
FROM lab_results
WHERE test = 'HbA1c'
LIMIT 12;
::p
Because evaluation stops at the first true branch, **order your conditions from most to least specific** — a `WHEN value >= 0` placed first would swallow everything. CASE expressions can live anywhere an expression can: in ORDER BY (custom sort orders), inside aggregates (Lesson 22's conditional counting trick), and in GROUP BY.

The other daily use is banding ages. You don't have an `age` column yet (that's Lesson 21), but the pattern is universal:
::code|sql|The age-band pattern (memorise the shape)
CASE
  WHEN age < 5                 THEN '0-4'
  WHEN age BETWEEN 5  AND 17   THEN '5-17'
  WHEN age BETWEEN 18 AND 39   THEN '18-39'
  WHEN age BETWEEN 40 AND 64   THEN '40-64'
  WHEN age BETWEEN 65 AND 79   THEN '65-79'
  ELSE '80+'
END AS age_band
::p
These bands match the `la_population` table in your playground — a deliberate design so numerators and denominators can meet (the essence of rate calculation, coming in Lesson 37).
::quiz
Q In a CASE expression, what happens if two WHEN conditions are both true for a row?
- The engine raises an error
- Both labels are returned
* The first true condition (top-down) wins
- The last true condition wins
! Evaluation is strictly top to bottom and stops at the first match — which is why condition order matters.
::quiz
Q A CASE with no ELSE, where no WHEN matches, returns…
- 0
- An empty string
* NULL
- The first WHEN's value
! No match + no ELSE = NULL. Adding an explicit ELSE 'Other' (or similar) is usually kinder to whoever reads the output.
::ex
From admissions, create a column los_flag: 'Same day' when discharge_date equals admission_date, 'Still in' when discharge_date IS NULL, otherwise 'Overnight+'. Show admission_id, both dates and the flag for 12 rows.
~~~sql
SELECT
  admission_id,
  admission_date,
  discharge_date,
  CASE
    WHEN discharge_date IS NULL              THEN 'Still in'
    WHEN discharge_date = admission_date     THEN 'Same day'
    ELSE 'Overnight+'
  END AS los_flag
FROM admissions
LIMIT 12;
::key
- CASE WHEN … THEN … ELSE … END: first true branch wins; missing ELSE yields NULL.
- Order conditions most-specific-first; put IS NULL checks in explicitly.
- Age banding via CASE is the analyst's bread and butter — align bands with your population table.
#LESSON 2|Dates part 1: storing, comparing and extracting
::p
Dates power almost every health question — trends, waits, ages, financial years — and they're where SQL dialects differ most. The universal foundation: store and write dates as **ISO 8601 text**, `YYYY-MM-DD`. Comparisons and BETWEEN then just work, as you've already seen.

SQLite's date toolkit (used by your playground) is three functions:
- `date('now')` — today's date. `datetime('now')` adds the time (UTC).
- `date(d, modifier…)` — date arithmetic via text modifiers: `date('2026-03-31', '-1 year')`, `date(admission_date, '+30 day')`, `date('now', 'start of month')`.
- `strftime(format, d)` — extract parts: `'%Y'` year, `'%m'` month, `'%d'` day, `'%Y-%m'` a month key for grouping.
::code|sqltry|Extracting and shifting
SELECT
  admission_date,
  strftime('%Y',    admission_date)        AS yr,
  strftime('%Y-%m', admission_date)        AS yr_month,
  date(admission_date, '+30 day')          AS follow_up_due
FROM admissions
LIMIT 6;
::p
Subtracting dates: `julianday(a) - julianday(b)` gives the difference in days — the basis of length of stay:
::code|sqltry|Length of stay in days
SELECT
  admission_id,
  admission_date,
  discharge_date,
  CAST(julianday(discharge_date) - julianday(admission_date) AS INTEGER) AS los_days
FROM admissions
WHERE discharge_date IS NOT NULL
ORDER BY los_days DESC
LIMIT 8;
::p
**Dialect phrasebook (preview):** SQL Server writes today as `GETDATE()` or `CAST(GETDATE() AS DATE)`, differences as `DATEDIFF(DAY, a, b)`, shifts as `DATEADD(DAY, 30, a)`, and parts as `YEAR(d)` / `MONTH(d)` / `FORMAT(d,'yyyy-MM')`. Same concepts, different names — the full table waits in Lesson 40.
::quiz
Q In SQLite, which expression gives the number of days between admission and discharge?
- discharge_date - admission_date
- DATEDIFF(discharge_date, admission_date)
* julianday(discharge_date) - julianday(admission_date)
- AGE(discharge_date, admission_date)
! Subtracting ISO text directly doesn't work in SQLite; julianday converts each date to a day-number first. DATEDIFF is SQL Server's spelling.
::quiz
Q strftime('%Y-%m', '2025-12-07') returns…
- 2025
- 12-2025
* 2025-12
- December 2025
! %Y-%m formats year and month — the standard "month key" you'll GROUP BY for monthly trend charts.
::ex
Return the 10 most recent lab results with: date_taken, the year-month key, and the date exactly one year after date_taken (call it review_due).
~~~sql
SELECT
  date_taken,
  strftime('%Y-%m', date_taken)   AS yr_month,
  date(date_taken, '+1 year')     AS review_due
FROM lab_results
ORDER BY date_taken DESC
LIMIT 10;
::key
- ISO YYYY-MM-DD text makes comparisons, BETWEEN and sorting safe everywhere.
- SQLite: date()/datetime() shift, strftime() extracts, julianday differences count days.
- SQL Server says GETDATE, DATEDIFF, DATEADD, YEAR/MONTH — same ideas (Lesson 40 has the full phrasebook).
#LESSON 2|Dates part 2: ages, financial years and month spines
::p
Three date calculations come up so often in UK health work that they deserve their own lesson.

**1. Age at an event.** Age is the person's completed years at a reference date. A precise, portable formula: take the year difference, then subtract 1 if the birthday hasn't happened yet that year. In SQLite a compact trick compares the MM-DD parts as text:
::code|sqltry|Age at admission, exactly
SELECT
  a.admission_id,
  p.birth_date,
  a.admission_date,
  (strftime('%Y', a.admission_date) - strftime('%Y', p.birth_date))
    - (strftime('%m-%d', a.admission_date) < strftime('%m-%d', p.birth_date))
    AS age_at_admission
FROM admissions a
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id   -- joins arrive properly in Lesson 25
LIMIT 8;
::p
That `(condition)` evaluates to 1 when true, 0 when false — subtracting it handles the not-yet-birthday case. An approximation like `(julianday(d1)-julianday(d0))/365.25` is fine for grouping but can misplace people near band boundaries; use the exact form for anything published.

**2. Financial year.** April–March, labelled `2025/26`. The rule: if the month is January–March, the FY started the *previous* calendar year.
::code|sqltry|Deriving financial year
SELECT
  admission_date,
  CASE
    WHEN CAST(strftime('%m', admission_date) AS INTEGER) >= 4
      THEN strftime('%Y', admission_date) || '/' ||
           SUBSTR(CAST(strftime('%Y', admission_date) + 1 AS TEXT), 3, 2)
    ELSE (strftime('%Y', admission_date) - 1) || '/' ||
           SUBSTR(strftime('%Y', admission_date), 3, 2)
  END AS financial_year
FROM admissions
LIMIT 8;
::p
**3. Month keys for trends.** `strftime('%Y-%m', d)` gives a groupable, sortable month. One caution for real reports: months with zero events simply *don't appear* in grouped output — a gap, not a zero. The fix (a "date spine" you join onto) is built in Lesson 32.
::quiz
Q A patient born 1960-07-15 is admitted on 2025-07-01. Their age at admission is…
- 65
* 64
- 64.96
- 66
! The 65th birthday (15 July) hasn't happened yet on 1 July, so completed age is 64. This is exactly the case the exact formula handles and the /365.25 shortcut can fumble.
::quiz
Q An admission on 2026-02-10 belongs to which financial year?
- 2026/27
* 2025/26
- 2025
- 2026
! February is before April, so it falls in the FY that began the previous April: 2025/26.
::ex
Write a query showing each vaccination's date_given and its financial year, then count vaccinations per financial year (GROUP BY sneak preview) ordered by year.
~~~sql
SELECT
  CASE
    WHEN CAST(strftime('%m', date_given) AS INTEGER) >= 4
      THEN strftime('%Y', date_given) || '/' ||
           SUBSTR(CAST(strftime('%Y', date_given) + 1 AS TEXT), 3, 2)
    ELSE (strftime('%Y', date_given) - 1) || '/' ||
           SUBSTR(strftime('%Y', date_given), 3, 2)
  END      AS financial_year,
  COUNT(*) AS vaccinations
FROM vaccinations
GROUP BY financial_year
ORDER BY financial_year;
::key
- Exact age = year difference minus (birthday not yet reached); publish nothing with the 365.25 shortcut.
- FY rule: months Jan–Mar belong to the FY that began the previous April.
- Month keys via strftime('%Y-%m'); remember grouped output omits zero months.
#LESSON 2|Aggregates: COUNT, SUM, AVG, MIN, MAX
::p
Aggregate functions collapse many rows into one summary value — the moment SQL turns from *fetching* data into *analysing* it.
::code|sqltry|One-row summary of admissions
SELECT
  COUNT(*)                                        AS total_admissions,
  COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id)                      AS patients_admitted,
  MIN(admission_date)                             AS first_date,
  MAX(admission_date)                             AS last_date,
  ROUND(AVG(julianday(discharge_date)
          - julianday(admission_date)), 1)        AS mean_los_days
FROM admissions;
::p
Rules with teeth:
- `COUNT(*)` counts **rows**. `COUNT(column)` counts **non-NULL values** in that column — so `COUNT(discharge_date)` is completed discharges, and `COUNT(*) - COUNT(discharge_date)` is current inpatients. That NULL-skipping applies to all aggregates: `AVG` averages only the values that exist.
- `SUM` adds; on a column of 1/0 flags it becomes a conditional counter.
- The **conditional aggregation** idiom — CASE inside an aggregate — builds several filtered counts in one pass:
::code|sqltry|Several counts in one query
SELECT
  COUNT(*)                                                     AS all_adm,
  SUM(CASE WHEN admission_method = 'Emergency' THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) AS emergency,
  SUM(CASE WHEN admission_method = 'Elective'  THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) AS elective,
  ROUND(100.0 * SUM(CASE WHEN admission_method = 'Emergency' THEN 1 ELSE 0 END)
        / COUNT(*), 1)                                         AS pct_emergency
FROM admissions;
::p
That pattern — numerator built with CASE, denominator with COUNT(*), joined by `100.0 *` — is the skeleton of half the performance indicators in the NHS.
::quiz
Q In a table of 500 admissions where 40 have NULL discharge_date, COUNT(discharge_date) returns…
- 500
- 40
* 460
- NULL
! COUNT(column) skips NULLs: 500 − 40 = 460 completed discharges. COUNT(*) would give 500.
::quiz
Q AVG(value) over the values 10, 20, NULL is…
- 10
* 15
- 7.5 because NULL counts as 0
- NULL
! Aggregates ignore NULLs entirely: (10+20)/2 = 15. If you wanted missing to count as zero, you'd COALESCE(value,0) first — a deliberate analytical choice.
::ex
For lab_results where test = 'Systolic BP': in one query return the number of readings, number of distinct patients, mean value (1 dp), minimum and maximum, and the count of readings of 140 or higher (call it high_bp_readings).
~~~sql
SELECT
  COUNT(*)                         AS readings,
  COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id)       AS patients,
  ROUND(AVG(value), 1)             AS mean_sbp,
  MIN(value)                       AS min_sbp,
  MAX(value)                       AS max_sbp,
  SUM(CASE WHEN value >= 140 THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) AS high_bp_readings
FROM lab_results
WHERE test = 'Systolic BP';
::key
- COUNT(*) = rows; COUNT(col) = non-NULLs; all aggregates skip NULLs.
- SUM(CASE WHEN … THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) = filtered counting inside one query.
- Numerator ÷ denominator × 100.0 — the indicator skeleton you'll reuse forever.
#LESSON 2|GROUP BY: summaries for every category
::p
`GROUP BY` splits rows into groups and runs your aggregates **once per group** — turning "how many admissions?" into "how many admissions *per specialty, per month, per practice*?". It is the single most-used clause in health analytics.
::code|sqltry|Admissions by specialty and method
SELECT
  specialty,
  admission_method,
  COUNT(*)                                   AS admissions,
  ROUND(AVG(julianday(discharge_date)
          - julianday(admission_date)), 1)   AS mean_los
FROM admissions
GROUP BY specialty, admission_method
ORDER BY admissions DESC;
::p
The iron rule: **every column in your SELECT must be either inside an aggregate or listed in GROUP BY.** SQLite is permissive and will quietly pick an arbitrary row's value for an unaggregated column — a silent wrong answer. SQL Server refuses to run such a query at all, which is friendlier. Write to the strict rule always.

Monthly trend tables fall straight out by grouping on an expression:
::code|sqltry|The monthly trend pattern
SELECT
  strftime('%Y-%m', admission_date) AS yr_month,
  COUNT(*)                          AS admissions,
  COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id)        AS patients
FROM admissions
GROUP BY strftime('%Y-%m', admission_date)
ORDER BY yr_month;
::p
Reading grouped output critically is a skill: small groups (a specialty with 3 admissions) produce unstable averages; and remember from Lesson 21 that missing months are absent, not zero. Both caveats belong in your commentary when this becomes a report.
::quiz
Q Which query is correctly formed?
- SELECT specialty, admission_date, COUNT(*) FROM admissions GROUP BY specialty;
* SELECT specialty, COUNT(*) FROM admissions GROUP BY specialty;
- SELECT specialty, COUNT(*) FROM admissions;
- SELECT COUNT(*) GROUP BY specialty;
! Every selected column must be grouped or aggregated. Option 1 selects admission_date without either; option 3 mixes an aggregate with a bare column and no GROUP BY.
::quiz
Q GROUP BY strftime('%Y-%m', admission_date) produces one output row per…
- Admission
- Year
* Calendar month that has at least one admission
- Patient
! One row per distinct month key present in the data — which is why zero-activity months vanish (date spines fix that later).
::ex
Produce a table of vaccinations given, by vaccine and dose_number: count of doses and count of distinct children, ordered by vaccine then dose_number.
~~~sql
SELECT
  vaccine,
  dose_number,
  COUNT(*)                   AS doses_given,
  COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id) AS children
FROM vaccinations
GROUP BY vaccine, dose_number
ORDER BY vaccine, dose_number;
::key
- GROUP BY = one summary row per category (or category combination).
- Strict rule: select only grouped columns or aggregates — SQLite won't warn you, SQL Server will.
- Group on expressions (month keys) for trends; distrust tiny groups.
#LESSON 2|HAVING: filtering the groups themselves
::p
`WHERE` filters **rows before grouping**; `HAVING` filters **groups after aggregation**. You need HAVING the moment your condition involves an aggregate — you cannot write `WHERE COUNT(*) > 5`, because when WHERE runs, no counting has happened yet.
::code|sqltry|Frequent attenders: 3+ emergency admissions
SELECT
  patient_id,
  COUNT(*) AS emergency_admissions
FROM admissions
WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'     -- row filter first
GROUP BY patient_id
HAVING COUNT(*) >= 3                     -- then group filter
ORDER BY emergency_admissions DESC;
::p
That query is a real public-health workhorse: identifying **frequent A&E attenders / repeat emergency admissions** for case-management programmes. Note the division of labour — WHERE cut the data to emergencies (efficient: less to group), HAVING kept only the patients whose *aggregate* crossed the threshold.

The full logical order of a query is worth memorising, because it explains most beginner errors:
::table
Step|Clause|What happens
1|FROM (+ JOINs)|Assemble the source rows
2|WHERE|Filter rows
3|GROUP BY|Form groups
4|HAVING|Filter groups
5|SELECT|Compute output columns
6|ORDER BY|Sort results
7|LIMIT|Cap the output
::p
Because SELECT runs *after* WHERE, a column alias made in SELECT can't be used in WHERE (engines vary on HAVING/ORDER BY; SQLite allows aliases in both). When in doubt, repeat the expression.
::quiz
Q "Practices with more than 200 registered flu vaccinations" needs which construction?
- WHERE COUNT(*) > 200
* GROUP BY practice … HAVING COUNT(*) > 200
- HAVING before GROUP BY
- ORDER BY COUNT(*) > 200
! The condition tests an aggregate, so it must run after grouping — that's precisely HAVING's job.
::quiz
Q Why keep admission_method = 'Emergency' in WHERE rather than HAVING?
- HAVING cannot reference text columns
- It changes the answer
* It's a row-level condition, so filtering early is correct and more efficient
- WHERE is deprecated
! Row conditions belong in WHERE (smaller groups, faster, clearer intent). HAVING is reserved for conditions on aggregates. Putting a row condition in HAVING sometimes runs but muddles meaning.
::ex
Find LSOAs (lsoa_code in patients) with at least 15 registered patients: return lsoa_code and the patient count, largest first.
~~~sql
SELECT
  lsoa_code,
  COUNT(*) AS patients
FROM patients
GROUP BY lsoa_code
HAVING COUNT(*) >= 15
ORDER BY patients DESC;
::key
- WHERE = rows before grouping; HAVING = groups after aggregation.
- Frequent-attender lists are GROUP BY patient + HAVING COUNT ≥ threshold.
- Learn the logical clause order — it predicts what the engine will accept.
#LESSON 2|JOIN part 1: INNER JOIN, keys and aliases
::p
So far every query has used one table, but the questions that matter span several: *admissions* by *deprivation decile* needs admissions → patients → lsoa_imd. `JOIN` stitches tables together by matching key values. An **INNER JOIN** keeps only rows that find a match on both sides.
::code|sqltry|Admissions with patient details attached
SELECT
  a.admission_id,
  a.admission_date,
  a.specialty,
  p.sex,
  p.birth_date,
  p.practice_code
FROM admissions a
INNER JOIN patients p
        ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id
LIMIT 8;
::p
Anatomy:
- `admissions a` gives the table a short **alias**; thereafter every column is written `alias.column`. With multiple tables this isn't optional politeness — `patient_id` exists in both, and an unqualified reference is ambiguous.
- `ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id` is the **join condition**: match rows where the keys are equal. Joining on the wrong columns, or forgetting ON entirely (which produces every-row-with-every-row chaos), are the classic failures.
- Conceptually the engine finds, for each admission, the patient row with the same id, and glues the columns side by side.

Joined results feed straight into everything from earlier lessons — filter them, band them, group them:
::code|sqltry|Emergency admissions by sex
SELECT
  p.sex,
  COUNT(*) AS emergency_admissions
FROM admissions a
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id    -- INNER is the default
WHERE a.admission_method = 'Emergency'
GROUP BY p.sex;
::quiz
Q In FROM admissions a JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id, what are a and p?
- Column names
- Temporary copies of the tables
* Aliases — short local names for each table
- Keywords required by SQL
! Aliases keep multi-table queries readable and make column references unambiguous. Any short names work; initials are conventional.
::quiz
Q An INNER JOIN between admissions and patients drops which rows?
- Admissions with NULL discharge dates
* Any admission whose patient_id has no match in patients (and any patient with no admissions)
- Duplicate admissions
- Nothing; INNER JOIN keeps everything
! INNER keeps matched pairs only. Unmatched rows on either side silently vanish — sometimes fine, sometimes a data-quality alarm (Lesson 26 handles keeping them).
::ex
Join lab_results to patients and count HbA1c tests by patient sex. Return sex and the test count.
~~~sql
SELECT
  p.sex,
  COUNT(*) AS hba1c_tests
FROM lab_results l
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = l.patient_id
WHERE l.test = 'HbA1c'
GROUP BY p.sex;
::key
- INNER JOIN … ON key = key: keeps matching pairs, drops non-matches on both sides.
- Alias every table; qualify every column as alias.column.
- Join first, then filter/band/group exactly as before — the clauses all still apply.
#LESSON 2|JOIN part 2: LEFT JOIN and finding what's missing
::p
INNER JOIN answers "who matches?" — but public health constantly asks the opposite: **who's missing?** Which children have *no* MMR record? Which practices had *no* admissions? A `LEFT JOIN` keeps **every row of the left table**, matched or not; where the right side has no match, its columns come back as NULL. That NULL is the fingerprint of absence, and filtering on it is the standard "find the missing" pattern:
::code|sqltry|Patients with no vaccination record at all
SELECT
  p.patient_id,
  p.birth_date,
  v.vaccination_id          -- NULL when no match exists
FROM patients p
LEFT JOIN vaccinations v
       ON v.patient_id = p.patient_id
WHERE v.vaccination_id IS NULL
LIMIT 10;
::p
Read it as: keep all patients; attach vaccinations where they exist; then keep only the rows where nothing attached. (This is called an **anti-join**, and R has a function named exactly that — Lesson 51.)

A second essential use: **preserving zeros in grouped counts.** Count admissions per practice with an INNER JOIN and practices with none disappear; LEFT JOIN keeps them, and counting a right-side column (which is NULL for non-matches) turns absence into 0:
::code|sqltry|Every practice, including the quiet ones
SELECT
  g.practice_code,
  g.practice_name,
  COUNT(a.admission_id) AS admissions      -- COUNT(col) skips NULLs -> 0
FROM gp_practices g
LEFT JOIN patients   p ON p.practice_code = g.practice_code
LEFT JOIN admissions a ON a.patient_id    = p.patient_id
GROUP BY g.practice_code, g.practice_name
ORDER BY admissions ASC;
::p
Note `COUNT(a.admission_id)`, not `COUNT(*)`: the latter would count the practice's placeholder row itself and report 1 instead of 0. A `RIGHT JOIN` exists (keep all right-side rows) but analysts almost always just reorder the tables and use LEFT; `FULL OUTER JOIN` (keep both sides) is rarer and absent from SQLite.
::quiz
