Q After patients LEFT JOIN vaccinations, a patient with no vaccinations appears…
- Not at all
- Once per vaccine type with zeros
* Once, with the vaccination columns NULL
- As an error
! LEFT keeps every left row; unmatched right columns are NULL — which is exactly what WHERE … IS NULL then detects.
::quiz
Q In the zero-preserving count, why COUNT(a.admission_id) instead of COUNT(*)?
- They are identical
- COUNT(*) is slower
* COUNT(*) counts the unmatched placeholder row as 1; COUNT(a.admission_id) skips its NULL and yields a true 0
- admission_id is the primary key
! The NULL-skipping behaviour of COUNT(column) — from Lesson 22 — is what turns "no match" into the honest zero.
::ex
List patients who have a death record's underlying_cause of 'C34' available — no, better: list all patients aged in the data who have NO row in deaths (i.e. presumed alive), returning patient_id and birth_date, 10 rows.
~~~sql
SELECT
  p.patient_id,
  p.birth_date
FROM patients p
LEFT JOIN deaths d ON d.patient_id = p.patient_id
WHERE d.patient_id IS NULL
LIMIT 10;
::key
- LEFT JOIN keeps all left rows; unmatched right columns are NULL.
- WHERE right_key IS NULL = the anti-join: who's missing a record.
- LEFT JOIN + COUNT(right_column) preserves true zeros in grouped counts.
#LESSON 2|JOIN part 3: chains, fan-out and the duplication trap
::p
Real questions chain several joins. "Emergency admissions per IMD decile" walks admissions → patients → lsoa_imd:
::code|sqltry|Admissions by deprivation decile
SELECT
  d.imd_decile,
  COUNT(*) AS emergency_admissions
FROM admissions a
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id
JOIN lsoa_imd d ON d.lsoa_code  = p.lsoa_code
WHERE a.admission_method = 'Emergency'
GROUP BY d.imd_decile
ORDER BY d.imd_decile;
::p
Deciles run 1 (most deprived) to 10 (least). If a health-service dataset is behaving as the literature predicts, emergency activity tilts toward low deciles — you now have the tool to check.

**The trap: join fan-out.** Joins match *every* pair of matching rows. Join patients (1 row each) to admissions (many rows each) and each patient row is **duplicated once per admission** — correct for counting admissions, catastrophic if you then count "patients" with COUNT(*) or SUM a per-patient value like list size. Symptoms: totals mysteriously bigger than the source table; the fix: know each relationship's shape —
::table
Relationship|Example|Effect of joining
One-to-one|patients ↔ deaths|Row count unchanged
One-to-many|patients → admissions|Left rows repeat per match
Many-to-many|admissions ↔ lab_results via patient|Multiplicative blow-up — almost never join directly
::p
Defensive habits: check `COUNT(*)` before and after each join; count people with `COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id)` after any one-to-many join; and never join two "many" tables to each other on patient_id — aggregate one side first (subqueries and CTEs, next lessons, exist for exactly this).
::quiz
Q patients has 600 rows; admissions has 900. SELECT COUNT(*) FROM patients p JOIN admissions a ON a.patient_id = p.patient_id returns at most…
- 600
* 900
- 1500
- 540000
! One output row per matching admission: patients fan out to their admissions. (Exactly 900 if every admission's patient exists — worth verifying!)
::quiz
Q After joining patients to admissions, the safe way to report "number of patients admitted" is…
- COUNT(*)
- COUNT(admission_id)
* COUNT(DISTINCT p.patient_id)
- SUM(1)
! Fan-out duplicates patients across their admissions; DISTINCT collapses them back to people.
::ex
Produce emergency admissions per local authority: chain admissions → patients → lsoa_imd, group by la_name, and report both admissions and distinct patients.
~~~sql
SELECT
  d.la_name,
  COUNT(*)                     AS emergency_admissions,
  COUNT(DISTINCT p.patient_id) AS patients
FROM admissions a
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id
JOIN lsoa_imd d ON d.lsoa_code  = p.lsoa_code
WHERE a.admission_method = 'Emergency'
GROUP BY d.la_name
ORDER BY emergency_admissions DESC;
::key
- Chain joins to walk relationships: event → person → place is the health-data spine.
- One-to-many joins duplicate the "one" side — count people with COUNT(DISTINCT id).
- Never join two many-sided tables directly on patient_id; aggregate one first.
#LESSON 2|UNION and friends: stacking result sets
::p
Joins combine tables **side by side**; set operators combine them **top to bottom**. You'll reach for them when similar data arrives split — this year's file and last year's, or two systems recording the same kind of event.

- `UNION ALL` stacks two SELECTs, keeping everything (fast, honest about duplicates).
- `UNION` stacks and **removes duplicate rows** (slower; a deduplication step in disguise).
- `INTERSECT` keeps rows appearing in both results; `EXCEPT` keeps rows in the first but not the second. (SQL Server spells EXCEPT the same; Oracle calls it MINUS.)

Rules: each SELECT must have the **same number of columns with compatible types**; column names come from the first SELECT; a single ORDER BY applies at the very end.
::code|sqltry|One event list from two tables
SELECT patient_id, date_given  AS event_date, 'Vaccination: ' || vaccine AS event
FROM vaccinations
UNION ALL
SELECT patient_id, date_taken  AS event_date, 'Lab test: '    || test    AS event
FROM lab_results
ORDER BY patient_id, event_date
LIMIT 15;
::p
That "long event list" shape — one row per event, a type label column — is a genuinely useful analytical structure (R calls it *tidy data*; Lesson 52).

INTERSECT and EXCEPT shine at cohort logic and QA:
::code|sqltry|Who had COVID-19 vaccination but no flu jab?
SELECT patient_id FROM vaccinations WHERE vaccine = 'COVID-19'
EXCEPT
SELECT patient_id FROM vaccinations WHERE vaccine = 'Flu';
::quiz
Q The difference between UNION and UNION ALL is…
- UNION ALL requires identical column names
* UNION removes duplicate rows; UNION ALL keeps every row
- UNION works across databases; UNION ALL doesn't
- No difference
! ALL means "all rows, duplicates included". Default UNION deduplicates — occasionally what you want, often a surprise and always slower.
::quiz
Q You stack admissions from two hospital systems with UNION (not ALL) and the total is less than the two counts added. Most likely explanation?
- The database lost rows
* Identical rows existed in both sources and were deduplicated
- UNION always halves the data
- A join fanned out
! UNION's dedupe removed rows appearing (identically) in both inputs — which itself is a finding: are those true duplicates or coincidences?
::ex
Using INTERSECT, find patient_ids that appear in both the admissions table and the deaths table (people who were admitted at some point and later have a death record).
~~~sql
SELECT patient_id FROM admissions
INTERSECT
SELECT patient_id FROM deaths;
::key
- UNION ALL stacks; UNION stacks + dedupes; INTERSECT = in both; EXCEPT = in first only.
- Column counts/types must align; the first SELECT names the output.
- Stacked "event list" tables are an analytical asset, not a compromise.
#LESSON 2|Subqueries: queries inside queries
::p
A subquery is a SELECT nested inside another statement, in brackets. Three flavours cover nearly all use:

**1. Scalar subquery** — returns one value, used like a number:
::code|sqltry|Compare each practice to the average
SELECT
  practice_name,
  list_size,
  list_size - (SELECT AVG(list_size) FROM gp_practices) AS diff_from_mean
FROM gp_practices
ORDER BY diff_from_mean DESC;
::p
**2. IN-list subquery** — returns one column, used as a filter set. "Admissions of patients living in the most deprived decile":
::code|sqltry|Filter by membership of a computed set
SELECT a.admission_id, a.admission_date, a.specialty
FROM admissions a
WHERE a.patient_id IN (
  SELECT p.patient_id
  FROM patients p
  JOIN lsoa_imd d ON d.lsoa_code = p.lsoa_code
  WHERE d.imd_decile = 1
)
LIMIT 10;
::p
`NOT IN` inverts it — but carries a nasty edge: if the subquery returns any NULL, `NOT IN` matches *nothing at all* (NULL logic from Lesson 18 striking again). The LEFT JOIN … IS NULL anti-join, or `NOT EXISTS`, avoids this and is what experienced analysts write.

**3. Subquery in FROM (a derived table)** — treat a query's result as a table and query *that*. "Average number of admissions per admitted patient":
::code|sqltry|Aggregate of an aggregate
SELECT ROUND(AVG(n), 2) AS mean_admissions_per_patient
FROM (
  SELECT patient_id, COUNT(*) AS n
  FROM admissions
  GROUP BY patient_id
) AS per_patient;
::p
Two levels of aggregation need two steps — inner counts per patient, outer averages the counts. Nested brackets get unreadable fast, though; the next lesson's CTEs express the same idea legibly, and are what you should default to.
::quiz
Q A scalar subquery must return…
- One column, any rows
* Exactly one value (one row, one column)
- At least two columns
- Only integers
! It stands where a single value would: one row, one column. More rows = an error (or, in permissive engines, silent surprise).
::quiz
Q Why do professionals avoid NOT IN (SELECT patient_id FROM …) when the subquery column can be NULL?
- It is slower than everything else
* A single NULL in the list makes NOT IN return no rows at all
- NOT IN is not valid SQL
- It deletes rows
! x NOT IN (…, NULL) evaluates to unknown for every x, so the filter passes nothing. NOT EXISTS or an anti-join are NULL-safe.
::ex
Using an IN subquery, list the vaccination records (patient_id, vaccine, dose_number, date_given) of patients registered at the single largest practice by list_size. (Hint: the inner query finds that practice_code; you may nest two levels or use LIMIT 1.)
~~~sql
SELECT v.patient_id, v.vaccine, v.dose_number, v.date_given
FROM vaccinations v
WHERE v.patient_id IN (
  SELECT p.patient_id
  FROM patients p
  WHERE p.practice_code = (
    SELECT practice_code
    FROM gp_practices
    ORDER BY list_size DESC
    LIMIT 1
  )
)
ORDER BY v.patient_id, v.date_given;
::key
- Scalar (one value), IN-list (filter set), derived table (query a query) — the three subquery shapes.
- NOT IN + NULLs = empty results; prefer NOT EXISTS or anti-joins.
- Aggregating an aggregate requires nesting — which CTEs (next) make readable.
#LESSON 3|CTEs: readable, professional query pipelines
::p
A **Common Table Expression** (CTE) names a subquery up front with `WITH`, so the main query reads like a pipeline of steps. Everything a derived table can do, a CTE does more legibly — and legible SQL is what NHS teams QA, share and maintain. The Lesson 29 example, rewritten:
::code|sqltry|Same logic, readable shape
WITH per_patient AS (
  SELECT patient_id, COUNT(*) AS n
  FROM admissions
  GROUP BY patient_id
)
SELECT ROUND(AVG(n), 2) AS mean_admissions_per_patient
FROM per_patient;
::p
Chain several with commas — each step can use the ones before it. A realistic mini-pipeline: emergency admissions, add patient deprivation, then summarise:
::code|sqltry|A three-step pipeline
WITH emerg AS (
  SELECT patient_id, admission_date
  FROM admissions
  WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'
),
with_imd AS (
  SELECT e.patient_id, e.admission_date, d.imd_decile
  FROM emerg e
  JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = e.patient_id
  JOIN lsoa_imd d ON d.lsoa_code  = p.lsoa_code
)
SELECT
  CASE WHEN imd_decile <= 2 THEN 'Most deprived 20%'
       ELSE 'Rest' END        AS grp,
  COUNT(*)                    AS admissions,
  COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id)  AS patients
FROM with_imd
GROUP BY grp;
::p
Style points that mark out professional code: one CTE per logical step; name CTEs after what they *contain* (`emerg`, `with_imd`, `latest_hba1c`), not `t1`, `t2`; keep each step small enough to test alone — you can highlight just the inner SELECT and run it. CTEs exist in every serious engine (SQL Server, PostgreSQL, SQLite alike), so this habit transfers wholesale into NHS work.
::quiz
Q What is the main practical advantage of a CTE over a nested subquery?
- CTEs run faster in every engine
* The query reads as named, testable steps instead of inside-out nesting
- CTEs can modify data; subqueries cannot
- Subqueries are deprecated
! Performance is usually similar; the win is readability and QA-ability. In audit-heavy NHS work, that is worth a lot.
::quiz
Q In WITH a AS (…), b AS (…) SELECT … — which is true?
- b cannot reference a
* b may reference a, and the final SELECT may use both
- Only one CTE is allowed per query
- CTEs persist in the database after the query ends
! CTEs chain top-to-bottom within one statement and vanish when it finishes (persisting results is Lesson 41).
::ex
Using two CTEs, find the mean HbA1c per patient (first CTE: each patient's average value across their HbA1c tests), then return how many patients have a personal mean of 48+ versus below 48 (second step: CASE + GROUP BY).
~~~sql
WITH patient_means AS (
  SELECT patient_id, AVG(value) AS mean_hba1c
  FROM lab_results
  WHERE test = 'HbA1c'
  GROUP BY patient_id
)
SELECT
  CASE WHEN mean_hba1c >= 48 THEN '48 and over' ELSE 'Under 48' END AS band,
  COUNT(*) AS patients
FROM patient_means
GROUP BY band;
::key
- WITH name AS (SELECT …) turns nesting into named steps — the professional default.
- Chain CTEs with commas; later steps can use earlier ones.
- Name steps for their content; test steps in isolation.
#LESSON 3|Window functions 1: ROW_NUMBER and "latest record per patient"
::p
Aggregates collapse rows; **window functions** compute across related rows *while keeping every row*. The syntax is `function() OVER (PARTITION BY … ORDER BY …)`: PARTITION BY says who is compared with whom; ORDER BY says in what order.

The single most useful pattern in health data: **the latest record per patient**. Patients have many HbA1c results; clinical questions usually want the most recent one each:
::code|sqltry|Latest HbA1c per patient
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT
    patient_id,
    value,
    date_taken,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
      PARTITION BY patient_id
      ORDER BY date_taken DESC
    ) AS rn
  FROM lab_results
  WHERE test = 'HbA1c'
)
SELECT patient_id, value, date_taken
FROM ranked
WHERE rn = 1
ORDER BY value DESC
LIMIT 10;
::p
Read it: within each patient (PARTITION), sort their tests newest-first (ORDER BY … DESC), number them 1, 2, 3… (ROW_NUMBER), keep number 1. Because window results can't sit directly in WHERE, the CTE-then-filter shape is standard.

Cousins to know: `RANK()` gives ties the same number and skips (1,1,3); `DENSE_RANK()` gives ties the same number without skipping (1,1,2). For "latest per patient", ROW_NUMBER with a decisive ORDER BY (add a tiebreak like `result_id DESC`) is what you want — RANK could return two "latest" rows on a tied date.
::quiz
Q PARTITION BY patient_id in a window function means…
- Group rows and collapse each patient to one row
* Restart the calculation for each patient, keeping all rows
- Split the table into physical files
- Sort by patient_id
! Windows never collapse rows: each patient's rows form a separate frame for the calculation, and every input row survives to the output.
::quiz
Q Two tests share a patient's latest date. Which choice guarantees exactly one "latest" row?
- RANK() … WHERE rnk = 1
- DENSE_RANK() … WHERE rnk = 1
* ROW_NUMBER() with a tiebreaker in ORDER BY … WHERE rn = 1
- MAX(date_taken)
! RANK/DENSE_RANK both label ties 1, returning two rows. ROW_NUMBER always numbers uniquely — make the order deterministic with a tiebreak column.
::ex
Find each patient's FIRST ever admission (earliest admission_date; tiebreak on admission_id), returning patient_id, admission_date and specialty, 10 rows.
~~~sql
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT
    patient_id, admission_date, specialty,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
      PARTITION BY patient_id
      ORDER BY admission_date ASC, admission_id ASC
    ) AS rn
  FROM admissions
)
SELECT patient_id, admission_date, specialty
FROM ranked
WHERE rn = 1
LIMIT 10;
::key
- function() OVER (PARTITION BY who ORDER BY sequence) — compute per group, keep all rows.
- ROW_NUMBER + WHERE rn = 1 = latest/first record per patient — memorise it.
- Add a tiebreaker to ORDER BY; use RANK/DENSE_RANK only when ties should share.
#LESSON 3|Window functions 2: running totals, moving averages and date spines
::p
Surveillance charts live on two calculations: **cumulative totals** and **moving averages**. Both are windows with an ORDER BY and (for the average) a *frame* saying how many rows to include.
::code|sqltry|Monthly counts, cumulative and smoothed
WITH monthly AS (
  SELECT strftime('%Y-%m', admission_date) AS yr_month,
         COUNT(*)                          AS n
  FROM admissions
  WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'
  GROUP BY yr_month
)
SELECT
  yr_month,
  n,
  SUM(n) OVER (ORDER BY yr_month)                          AS cumulative,
  ROUND(AVG(n) OVER (ORDER BY yr_month
        ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW), 1)      AS moving_avg_3m
FROM monthly
ORDER BY yr_month;
::p
`ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW` = this row plus the two before: a 3-month moving average. During COVID, the 7-day moving average of daily cases used exactly this shape with days instead of months.

**But there's a flaw:** if a month had zero emergencies, it's missing from `monthly`, and the "3-month" average silently spans 4 calendar months. The professional fix is a **date spine** — generate every period, then LEFT JOIN the counts on:
::code|sqltry|A month spine with zeros preserved
WITH RECURSIVE spine(month_start) AS (
  SELECT '2024-04-01'
  UNION ALL
  SELECT date(month_start, '+1 month') FROM spine
  WHERE month_start < '2026-03-01'
),
monthly AS (
  SELECT strftime('%Y-%m', admission_date) AS yr_month, COUNT(*) AS n
  FROM admissions
  GROUP BY yr_month
)
SELECT strftime('%Y-%m', s.month_start) AS yr_month,
       COALESCE(m.n, 0)                 AS admissions
FROM spine s
LEFT JOIN monthly m ON m.yr_month = strftime('%Y-%m', s.month_start)
ORDER BY yr_month;
::p
The recursive CTE builds rows by repeatedly adding a month until the stop condition. (SQL Server: same idea, or join to a pre-built calendar/`Tally` table, which most NHS warehouses keep.) Spine first, then windows, and your trends are honest.
::quiz
Q ROWS BETWEEN 6 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW computes, on daily data ordered by date…
- A 6-day average excluding today
* A 7-day window: today plus the previous six rows
- The whole series to date
- Six days into the future
! Current row + 6 preceding = 7 rows — the classic 7-day moving average, provided no days are missing (hence the spine).
::quiz
Q Why does a moving average over grouped month counts need a date spine?
- Window functions require one
* Missing zero-count months make the frame span the wrong calendar period
- SQLite cannot average otherwise
- To make the query faster
! ROWS frames count rows, not calendar time. Absent months shift the window silently; a spine restores one-row-per-month with zeros.
::ex
Build a monthly count of 'Flu' vaccinations with a cumulative total column, using a CTE for the monthly counts (spine optional here). Order by month.
~~~sql
WITH monthly AS (
  SELECT strftime('%Y-%m', date_given) AS yr_month, COUNT(*) AS n
  FROM vaccinations
  WHERE vaccine = 'Flu'
  GROUP BY yr_month
)
SELECT yr_month, n,
       SUM(n) OVER (ORDER BY yr_month) AS cumulative
FROM monthly
ORDER BY yr_month;
::key
- SUM(...) OVER (ORDER BY t) = running total; AVG with a ROWS frame = moving average.
- Frames count rows, so zero periods must exist: build a date spine (recursive CTE or calendar table).
- Spine + LEFT JOIN + COALESCE(n,0) is the honest-trend pattern.
#LESSON 3|Window functions 3: LAG, LEAD and gaps between events
::p
`LAG(col)` fetches a value from the **previous row** in the window; `LEAD(col)` from the next. Partition by patient, order by date, and you can measure the gap between one event and the last — the mechanic behind readmission intervals, repeat-test intervals and dose spacing.
::code|sqltry|Days since the patient's previous admission
SELECT
  patient_id,
  admission_id,
  admission_date,
  LAG(discharge_date) OVER (
    PARTITION BY patient_id
    ORDER BY admission_date, admission_id
  ) AS prev_discharge,
  CAST(julianday(admission_date) -
       julianday(LAG(discharge_date) OVER (
         PARTITION BY patient_id
         ORDER BY admission_date, admission_id)) AS INTEGER) AS days_since_discharge
FROM admissions
ORDER BY patient_id, admission_date
LIMIT 15;
::p
Notes: the first admission per patient has no previous row, so LAG returns **NULL** — correct and meaningful (nothing to compare). `LAG(col, 2)` looks two rows back; `LAG(col, 1, 0)` supplies a default instead of NULL. Repeating the window is legal but noisy — in engines that allow it you can name a window (`WINDOW w AS (…)`), or compute LAG in one CTE and difference it in the next, which is the portable, readable route:
::code|sql|Cleaner: LAG in one step, arithmetic in the next
WITH ordered AS (
  SELECT patient_id, admission_id, admission_date, discharge_date,
         LAG(discharge_date) OVER (
           PARTITION BY patient_id
           ORDER BY admission_date, admission_id) AS prev_discharge
  FROM admissions
)
SELECT *,
       CAST(julianday(admission_date) - julianday(prev_discharge) AS INTEGER)
         AS days_since_discharge
FROM ordered;
::p
Lesson 38 turns exactly this into the NHS's emergency-readmissions-within-30-days indicator.
::quiz
Q For each patient's first admission, LAG(discharge_date) OVER (PARTITION BY patient_id ORDER BY admission_date) returns…
- The last admission's discharge date
- 0
* NULL — there is no previous row in the partition
- An error
! No prior row means no value: NULL, which downstream logic should treat as "not a readmission candidate".
::quiz
Q You want each admission paired with the NEXT admission's date for the same patient. Which function?
- LAG(admission_date)
* LEAD(admission_date)
- ROW_NUMBER()
- MAX(admission_date)
! LEAD looks forward in the ordered partition; LAG looks back.
::ex
For lab_results where test = 'HbA1c', show each patient's tests with the days since their previous HbA1c (NULL for the first). Return patient_id, date_taken, value, days_since_prev; order by patient then date; 15 rows.
~~~sql
WITH ordered AS (
  SELECT patient_id, date_taken, value,
         LAG(date_taken) OVER (
           PARTITION BY patient_id
           ORDER BY date_taken) AS prev_date
  FROM lab_results
  WHERE test = 'HbA1c'
)
SELECT patient_id, date_taken, value,
       CAST(julianday(date_taken) - julianday(prev_date) AS INTEGER) AS days_since_prev
FROM ordered
ORDER BY patient_id, date_taken
LIMIT 15;
::key
- LAG/LEAD read neighbouring rows within a partition's order — gaps and intervals fall out by subtraction.
- First-row LAG is NULL by design; LAG(col, n, default) customises.
- Compute the window in a CTE, do arithmetic in the next step: portable and readable.
#LESSON 3|Deduplication: deciding what "one record" means
::p
Real extracts contain duplicates: interface hiccups double-send rows, patients register twice, the same test posts from two systems. Before counting anything, an analyst asks: **what should be unique here?** One row per admission? Per patient per day? Per patient overall? That decision is analytical, not technical — then SQL enforces it.

**Step 1 — measure the problem:**
::code|sqltry|Are there exact duplicate vaccination events?
SELECT patient_id, vaccine, dose_number, date_given, COUNT(*) AS copies
FROM vaccinations
GROUP BY patient_id, vaccine, dose_number, date_given
HAVING COUNT(*) > 1;
::p
**Step 2 — deduplicate by rule.** For *exact* duplicates, `SELECT DISTINCT` is enough. For *near*-duplicates (same event, different rows — e.g. two rows for one admission with different discharge dates), you need a **keep rule**: usually "the most recent / most complete record wins", which is Lesson 31's ROW_NUMBER pattern with the rule encoded in ORDER BY:
::code|sql|Keep one row per patient-vaccine-dose: latest date wins
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT *,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
      PARTITION BY patient_id, vaccine, dose_number
      ORDER BY date_given DESC, vaccination_id DESC
    ) AS rn
  FROM vaccinations
)
SELECT * FROM ranked WHERE rn = 1;
::p
**Step 3 — document it.** "Deduplicated to one row per patient/vaccine/dose, keeping the latest date_given" is a sentence that belongs in your methods note; different keep rules give different uptake figures, and a QA reviewer must be able to see which you chose. Golden rule: dedupe in queries and views, **never** by deleting from source tables — sources stay raw; rules live in code.
::quiz
Q "One row per patient per financial year" as a uniqueness rule means PARTITION BY…
- patient_id
* patient_id and the derived financial year
- financial year only
- admission_id
! The partition is exactly the combination that should be unique — the ROW_NUMBER then ranks candidates within it.
::quiz
Q Why prefer deduplicating in a query/view rather than deleting duplicate rows from the source table?
- DELETE is not real SQL
* The raw source stays intact and the rule is visible, reversible and re-runnable
- Views are faster than tables
- Duplicates are always meaningful
! Raw data is evidence. Encoding the rule in code keeps it auditable and lets you change your mind without loss.
::ex
Some patients have multiple Systolic BP results on the same date. Produce one row per patient per date_taken, keeping the HIGHEST value (a "worst reading" rule). Return patient_id, date_taken, value; 12 rows.
~~~sql
WITH ranked AS (
  SELECT patient_id, date_taken, value,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
      PARTITION BY patient_id, date_taken
      ORDER BY value DESC, result_id DESC
    ) AS rn
  FROM lab_results
  WHERE test = 'Systolic BP'
)
SELECT patient_id, date_taken, value
FROM ranked
WHERE rn = 1
ORDER BY patient_id, date_taken
LIMIT 12;
::key
- First define what should be unique; GROUP BY … HAVING COUNT(*)>1 measures violations.
- DISTINCT for exact copies; ROW_NUMBER with a keep rule for near-duplicates.
- Dedupe in code (views/CTEs), never by deleting source rows — and write the rule down.
#LESSON 3|Cohorts: defining exactly who is in your analysis
::p
A **cohort** is the precisely defined group your analysis is about: "adults 65+ registered in Barchester on 1 April 2025", "children turning 5 in 2025/26", "patients with an emergency respiratory admission during winter". Every serious piece of health analysis starts by building the cohort as its own step — usually a CTE ending in **one row per person** — and everything downstream joins to it.

Two distinctions to get right:
- **Prevalent vs incident.** Prevalent = everyone *with* the condition/state in a window (all diabetics being managed). Incident = *new* occurrences in the window (first-ever diagnoses). Mixing them muddles trends: prevalence can rise while incidence falls.
- **The index date.** Each cohort member gets an anchor date (first qualifying event, or a fixed census date) from which "within 30 days", "age at index" and follow-up are measured.
::code|sqltry|Cohort: patients with any HbA1c ≥ 48, with index date
WITH qualifying AS (
  SELECT patient_id, date_taken, value
  FROM lab_results
  WHERE test = 'HbA1c' AND value >= 48
),
cohort AS (
  SELECT patient_id,
         MIN(date_taken) AS index_date,       -- first qualifying result
         COUNT(*)        AS qualifying_results
  FROM qualifying
  GROUP BY patient_id
)
SELECT c.patient_id, c.index_date, c.qualifying_results,
       p.sex, p.birth_date, p.practice_code
FROM cohort c
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = c.patient_id
ORDER BY c.index_date
LIMIT 12;
::p
Checklist for any cohort definition (write the answers down before coding): inclusion criteria; exclusion criteria (e.g. died before index? not registered locally?); the time window; the index date rule; and whether a person can appear once or many times. In real work these sentences go in the methods section — analysts who skip them produce numbers nobody can reproduce.
::quiz
Q "New type 2 diabetes diagnoses in 2025/26" is a question about…
- Prevalence
* Incidence
- Mortality
- Screening coverage
! New occurrences in a period = incidence. "How many people currently have diabetes" would be prevalence.
::quiz
Q In the example, MIN(date_taken) per patient implements which cohort concept?
- Exclusion criteria
- Prevalence
* The index date — the first qualifying event anchoring follow-up
- Deduplication of exact copies
! Choosing the first qualifying event as the anchor is the standard incident-cohort index rule.
::ex
Build a cohort of frequent emergency attenders: patients with 3 or more Emergency admissions overall. One row per patient with patient_id, first emergency date (index_date), emergency count; joined to patients for sex. Order by count descending.
~~~sql
WITH emerg AS (
  SELECT patient_id, admission_date
  FROM admissions
  WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'
),
cohort AS (
  SELECT patient_id,
         MIN(admission_date) AS index_date,
         COUNT(*)            AS emergency_count
  FROM emerg
  GROUP BY patient_id
  HAVING COUNT(*) >= 3
)
SELECT c.*, p.sex
FROM cohort c
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = c.patient_id
ORDER BY c.emergency_count DESC;
::key
- A cohort CTE (one row per person, with an index date) anchors every serious analysis.
- Distinguish incident (new) from prevalent (existing); state inclusions, exclusions, window.
- Downstream steps join to the cohort — never re-derive membership ad hoc.
#LESSON 3|Length of stay, spells and pathway thinking
::p
Hospital data has structure inside it. In SUS/HES, a **provider spell** is one continuous stay in one hospital; inside it, **consultant episodes** track handovers between teams; transfers between hospitals chain spells into a **continuous inpatient (CIP) spell**. Your playground's `admissions` table is one row per spell — the common analyst-facing shape — but knowing the layers matters when numbers from different systems disagree ("their 'admissions' are episodes, ours are spells").

**Length of stay (LOS)** = discharge − admission in days; same-day cases are 0. LOS is the workhorse of capacity analysis, and it is famously **skewed**: most stays are short, a few are very long, so mean > median and the mean swings with a handful of outliers. Report the median (and percentiles) alongside any mean.
::code|sqltry|LOS by specialty: mean vs median-ish view
WITH los AS (
  SELECT specialty,
         CAST(julianday(discharge_date) - julianday(admission_date) AS INTEGER) AS days
  FROM admissions
  WHERE discharge_date IS NOT NULL
)
SELECT specialty,
       COUNT(*)                 AS spells,
       ROUND(AVG(days), 1)      AS mean_los,
       MAX(days)                AS longest,
       SUM(CASE WHEN days = 0 THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) AS same_day,
       SUM(CASE WHEN days >= 7 THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) AS week_plus
FROM los
GROUP BY specialty
ORDER BY mean_los DESC;
::p
(SQLite lacks a built-in MEDIAN; you can get it with window tricks, and in R it's one function — a good example of using each tool where it's strong.) Two data-quality realities to check whenever LOS looks odd: **negative LOS** (discharge before admission — data error) and **NULL discharges** (still in, or never closed — Lesson 39 makes these routine checks). Pathway thinking generalises: A&E attendance → admission → discharge → readmission is a sequence of linked events per patient, and the window functions you now own (ROW_NUMBER, LAG/LEAD) are precisely the tools that walk it.
::quiz
Q Mean LOS is 4.1 days, median is 1 day. The best interpretation is…
- The data is wrong
* Stays are right-skewed: most short, a few very long pulling the mean up
- Median must be recalculated
- Patients stay 4 days typically
! Classic skew. The median says a typical stay is short; the mean is dragged by long-stay outliers. Report both.
::quiz
Q In SUS/HES terms, one continuous stay in one hospital that contains three consultant handovers is…
- Three spells
* One provider spell containing three episodes
- One episode containing three spells
- A CIP spell only
! Episodes nest inside spells; chained inter-hospital spells form a CIP spell. Counting episodes as admissions triples the number.
::ex
Find suspicious records: any admissions where discharge_date is BEFORE admission_date (negative LOS), and separately count how many spells are still open (NULL discharge). Two short queries.
~~~sql
SELECT admission_id, patient_id, admission_date, discharge_date
FROM admissions
WHERE discharge_date IS NOT NULL
  AND discharge_date < admission_date;

SELECT COUNT(*) AS open_spells
FROM admissions
WHERE discharge_date IS NULL;
::key
- Episodes nest in spells nest in CIP spells — know which layer a count refers to.
- LOS is right-skewed: medians and percentiles beside means, always.
- Negative LOS and unclosed spells are standing data-quality checks.
#LESSON 3|Rates: joining numerators to populations
::p
Counts mislead: 120 emergency admissions in Barchester vs 80 in Kingsbay says little until you know how many people live in each. A **crude rate** = events ÷ population, scaled per 1,000 or 100,000. Your playground carries `la_population` (population by LA, sex and age band) precisely so numerators can meet denominators:
::code|sqltry|Crude emergency admission rate per 1,000, by LA
WITH numerator AS (
  SELECT d.la_name, COUNT(*) AS admissions
  FROM admissions a
  JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id
  JOIN lsoa_imd d ON d.lsoa_code  = p.lsoa_code
  WHERE a.admission_method = 'Emergency'
  GROUP BY d.la_name
),
denominator AS (
  SELECT la_name, SUM(population) AS pop
  FROM la_population
  GROUP BY la_name
)
SELECT n.la_name,
       n.admissions,
       d.pop,
       ROUND(1000.0 * n.admissions / d.pop, 1) AS rate_per_1000
FROM numerator n
JOIN denominator d ON d.la_name = n.la_name
ORDER BY rate_per_1000 DESC;
::p
The pattern — numerator CTE, denominator CTE, join, `1000.0 *` (decimal!, Lesson 17's trap) — is the same for vaccination coverage, GP consultation rates, mortality rates, everything. Three professional cautions:
- **Match the definitions.** Numerator "residents of Barchester" must divide by *residents* of Barchester, not patients *treated* there — resident-based vs provider-based counting is a real and common mismatch.
- **Match the period.** Two years of admissions over a one-year population doubles your rate; divide by person-years or use one year of events.
- **Crude rates hide age.** An older LA will "look worse" on almost anything. Comparing places fairly needs **age-standardisation** — the method is Lesson 70, in R, where it's far more pleasant.
::quiz
Q Barchester: 120 emergency admissions, population 40,000. The crude rate per 1,000 is…
- 0.3
* 3.0
- 30
- 333
! 1000 × 120 / 40000 = 3.0 per 1,000. (Per 100,000 it would be 300.)
::quiz
Q An LA with a much older population shows a higher crude COPD admission rate than a young city. Before concluding its services are worse, you should…
- Re-run the query
- Suppress the number
* Age-standardise, since age structure alone can explain crude differences
- Use counts instead of rates
! Crude rates confound age with everything else. Standardisation (Lesson 70) compares like with like.
::ex
Compute the death rate per 1,000 population by LA: numerator from deaths joined via patients to lsoa_imd; denominator from la_population. Round to 2 dp.
~~~sql
WITH numerator AS (
  SELECT l.la_name, COUNT(*) AS deaths
  FROM deaths d
  JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = d.patient_id
  JOIN lsoa_imd l ON l.lsoa_code  = p.lsoa_code
  GROUP BY l.la_name
),
denominator AS (
  SELECT la_name, SUM(population) AS pop
  FROM la_population
  GROUP BY la_name
)
SELECT n.la_name, n.deaths, d.pop,
       ROUND(1000.0 * n.deaths / d.pop, 2) AS death_rate_per_1000
FROM numerator n
JOIN denominator d ON d.la_name = n.la_name
ORDER BY death_rate_per_1000 DESC;
::key
- Rate = numerator ÷ matching denominator × scale; build each as a CTE and join.
- Match residence vs provider, and period vs period — mismatches are silent errors.
- Crude rates confound age: standardise before comparing areas (Lesson 70).
#LESSON 3|The classic metric: emergency readmissions within 30 days
::p
"Emergency readmissions within 30 days of discharge" is a national NHS indicator — a marker of care quality and system pressure — and it exercises everything you've learned: ordering events per patient, LAG, date arithmetic, cohort discipline. The logic: for each admission, find the patient's **previous discharge**; if this admission is an **Emergency** and the gap is **0–30 days**, it counts as a readmission.
::code|sqltry|30-day emergency readmissions
WITH ordered AS (
  SELECT
    patient_id,
    admission_id,
    admission_date,
    discharge_date,
    admission_method,
    LAG(discharge_date) OVER (
      PARTITION BY patient_id
      ORDER BY admission_date, admission_id
    ) AS prev_discharge
  FROM admissions
),
flagged AS (
  SELECT *,
    CASE
      WHEN admission_method = 'Emergency'
       AND prev_discharge IS NOT NULL
       AND julianday(admission_date) - julianday(prev_discharge)
           BETWEEN 0 AND 30
      THEN 1 ELSE 0
    END AS readmission_30d
  FROM ordered
)
SELECT
  COUNT(*)                                        AS admissions,
  SUM(readmission_30d)                            AS readmissions_30d,
  ROUND(100.0 * SUM(readmission_30d) / COUNT(*), 1) AS pct
FROM flagged;
::p
Definition details separate a defensible figure from a wrong one — the published indicator makes choices you should recognise: gaps measured **discharge → next admission**; day 0 (same-day bounce-back) counts; the *index* denominator excludes stays ending in death (you can LEFT JOIN `deaths` to exclude); planned/elective returns don't count as events (our Emergency filter handles this); and specialties like obstetrics are often excluded. When your number differs from a published one, the explanation is almost always one of these choices — which is why you **write the definition down**.
::quiz
Q Why measure the gap from the previous DISCHARGE rather than the previous admission?
- Admission dates are unreliable
* The indicator asks how soon after leaving hospital the patient bounced back
- Discharge dates are always NULL
- It makes the SQL shorter
! "Within 30 days of discharge" is the clinical concept: the risk window starts when the patient leaves.
::quiz
Q A patient dies during their index admission. For the standard indicator, that admission should be…
- Counted as a readmission
- Counted in the denominator
* Excluded from the denominator — they cannot be readmitted
- Duplicated
! Deaths in hospital can't generate a readmission, so including them deflates the rate. Exclude via a join to the deaths table.
::ex
Extend the query: report the 30-day emergency readmission percentage BY SPECIALTY of the index (previous) admission — hint: also LAG the specialty. Order by percentage descending.
~~~sql
WITH ordered AS (
  SELECT patient_id, admission_id, admission_date, admission_method,
         LAG(discharge_date) OVER (PARTITION BY patient_id
                                   ORDER BY admission_date, admission_id) AS prev_discharge,
         LAG(specialty)      OVER (PARTITION BY patient_id
                                   ORDER BY admission_date, admission_id) AS prev_specialty
  FROM admissions
),
flagged AS (
  SELECT prev_specialty,
    CASE WHEN admission_method = 'Emergency'
          AND prev_discharge IS NOT NULL
          AND julianday(admission_date) - julianday(prev_discharge) BETWEEN 0 AND 30
         THEN 1 ELSE 0 END AS readmit
  FROM ordered
  WHERE prev_discharge IS NOT NULL
)
SELECT prev_specialty AS index_specialty,
       COUNT(*)       AS discharges_followed,
       SUM(readmit)   AS readmissions_30d,
       ROUND(100.0 * SUM(readmit) / COUNT(*), 1) AS pct
FROM flagged
GROUP BY prev_specialty
ORDER BY pct DESC;
::key
- Readmissions = LAG(discharge) per patient + Emergency filter + 0–30 day gap.
- The definition's small choices (day 0, deaths, exclusions) move the number — record them.
- This one query pattern demonstrates cohorts, windows and date logic together: interview gold.
#LESSON 3|Data quality checks: trust nothing, verify everything
::p
Professional analysts run a **DQ pass** on every new extract before analysing it. Not because data teams are careless — because pipelines break, coding practices drift, and *your* conclusions inherit every flaw. A reusable six-check battery, each a small query:

**1. Row counts & date coverage** — is the volume plausible, and does the data span the period you think?
::code|sqltry|Coverage check
SELECT COUNT(*) AS rows_,
       MIN(admission_date) AS earliest,
       MAX(admission_date) AS latest
FROM admissions;
::p
**2. Completeness** — how NULL is each key column?
::code|sqltry|Missingness profile
SELECT
  COUNT(*)                                          AS rows_,
  SUM(CASE WHEN discharge_date IS NULL THEN 1 ELSE 0 END) AS null_discharge,
  SUM(CASE WHEN ethnicity IS NULL THEN 1 ELSE 0 END)      AS null_ethnicity_via_join
FROM admissions a
LEFT JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id;
::p
**3. Validity** — values in legal ranges (ages 0–110, HbA1c not 999, dates not in the future). **4. Consistency** — cross-field logic: discharge ≥ admission; death date after last discharge. **5. Referential integrity** — orphans: events whose patient doesn't exist (the anti-join from Lesson 26):
::code|sqltry|Orphaned admissions
SELECT COUNT(*) AS orphan_admissions
FROM admissions a
LEFT JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id
WHERE p.patient_id IS NULL;
::p
**6. Distribution sniff** — GROUP BY each categorical and eyeball: unexpected categories, one category at 95%, sudden monthly cliffs (a feed that died). Save your battery as a script and re-run it every refresh; in Module 7 you'll automate it. The habit to internalise: **a DQ finding is a finding** — "ethnicity is 38% missing and worse in deprived LSOAs" changes what your inequality analysis can claim.
::quiz
Q An admissions extract shows max(admission_date) three months before today. The most likely story is…
- Admissions stopped happening
* The feed lags or broke — investigate coverage before analysing trends
- SQL cannot read recent dates
- Someone deleted rows
! A data cliff is a pipeline symptom. Recent "declines" in activity are the classic artefact of an incomplete latest period.
::quiz
Q Orphaned admissions (patient_id with no patients row) most directly threaten which analyses?
- Row counts of admissions
* Anything joining to patient attributes — those events silently drop out of INNER JOINs
- ORDER BY queries
- Nothing; orphans are harmless
! Every demographic breakdown loses the orphans without warning. Measure them, then decide how to handle.
::ex
Write two consistency checks: (a) lab_results with implausible Systolic BP (below 60 or above 260); (b) deaths whose date_of_death is earlier than the patient's latest admission_date (event after death).
~~~sql
SELECT * FROM lab_results
WHERE test = 'Systolic BP'
  AND (value < 60 OR value > 260);

SELECT d.patient_id, d.date_of_death, MAX(a.admission_date) AS latest_admission
FROM deaths d
JOIN admissions a ON a.patient_id = d.patient_id
GROUP BY d.patient_id, d.date_of_death
HAVING MAX(a.admission_date) > d.date_of_death;
::key
- Six checks every extract: volume/coverage, completeness, validity, consistency, orphans, distributions.
- Save the battery; re-run on every refresh; automate later.
- DQ findings are analytical findings — they bound what you can claim.
#LESSON 3|The T-SQL phrasebook: SQL Server for NHS analysts
::p
Walk into an NHS analytics team and the database is very likely **Microsoft SQL Server**, queried through **SSMS** (SQL Server Management Studio) or Azure Data Studio, in its dialect **T-SQL**. Everything you've learned transfers; here is the translation table for the parts that differ:
::table
Task|SQLite (this course)|SQL Server (T-SQL)
First n rows|LIMIT 10|SELECT TOP 10 … (or OFFSET/FETCH)
Today|date('now')|CAST(GETDATE() AS DATE)
Date difference|julianday(a) − julianday(b)|DATEDIFF(DAY, b, a)
Date shift|date(d, '+30 day')|DATEADD(DAY, 30, d)
Year / month part|strftime('%Y', d)|YEAR(d) / MONTH(d)
Month key|strftime('%Y-%m', d)|FORMAT(d, 'yyyy-MM')
Concatenate|a ¦¦ b|a + b or CONCAT(a, b)
Integer to text|CAST(x AS TEXT)|CAST(x AS VARCHAR(20))
NULL fallback|COALESCE(a, b)|COALESCE(a, b) (or ISNULL)
Temp result|CTE / temp table|CTE / #temp_table
::p
Everything else — SELECT/WHERE/GROUP BY/HAVING, JOINs, CASE, CTEs, ROW_NUMBER/LAG/LEAD, UNION — is identical or near-identical. A few workplace-specific notes:
- **Schemas & brackets:** tables are addressed as `database.schema.table` (e.g. `Warehouse.dbo.Admissions`); square brackets `[Like This]` quote awkward names.
- **TOP needs ORDER BY** to be meaningful, exactly like LIMIT.
- **Case sensitivity** of text comparisons depends on collation (often insensitive) — keep the defensive UPPER(TRIM(…)) habit.
- **Permissions:** you'll usually have read-only access to warehouse schemas and write access to a sandbox — which is the correct shape; analysts create in sandboxes, never in source.
- **GETDATE() vs GETUTCDATE():** local vs UTC time — matters for overnight jobs.
::code|sql|The readmissions core, translated to T-SQL
WITH ordered AS (
  SELECT patient_id, admission_id, admission_date, admission_method,
         LAG(discharge_date) OVER (PARTITION BY patient_id
                                   ORDER BY admission_date, admission_id) AS prev_discharge
  FROM Warehouse.dbo.Admissions
)
SELECT COUNT(*)                                            AS admissions,
       SUM(CASE WHEN admission_method = 'Emergency'
                 AND DATEDIFF(DAY, prev_discharge, admission_date)
                     BETWEEN 0 AND 30
                THEN 1 ELSE 0 END)                         AS readmissions_30d
FROM ordered;
::quiz
Q The T-SQL equivalent of SQLite's LIMIT 20 is…
- LIMIT 20
- FIRST 20
* SELECT TOP 20 … (with ORDER BY for a meaningful "top")
- ROWNUM 20
! TOP is the T-SQL idiom (OFFSET/FETCH is the standard alternative). ROWNUM is Oracle's.
::quiz
Q DATEDIFF(DAY, prev_discharge, admission_date) corresponds to which SQLite expression?
- julianday(prev_discharge) − julianday(admission_date)
* julianday(admission_date) − julianday(prev_discharge)
- date(admission_date, '-30 day')
- strftime('%d', admission_date)
! DATEDIFF(unit, start, end) = end − start. Argument order trips people crossing dialects — check signs with a known example.
::ex
Translate to T-SQL: SELECT patient_id, date(date_given, '+1 year') AS due, strftime('%Y-%m', date_given) AS ym FROM vaccinations LIMIT 5;
~~~sql
SELECT TOP 5
       patient_id,
       DATEADD(YEAR, 1, date_given)   AS due,
       FORMAT(date_given, 'yyyy-MM')  AS ym
FROM vaccinations;
::key
- T-SQL differences are vocabulary, not concepts: TOP, GETDATE, DATEDIFF, DATEADD, FORMAT, +/CONCAT.
- Address tables as database.schema.table; expect read-only warehouse + writable sandbox.
- Keep one known-answer example to sanity-check argument order when switching dialects.
#LESSON 3|Views, temp tables and saving results
::p
So far your queries vanish on completion. Three ways to keep logic or results around — each with a proper use:

**Views** store the *query*, not the data. Query a view like a table and it re-runs its definition live — one blessed definition of "emergency admissions, cleaned" that the whole team shares, always current:
::code|sqltry|Create and use a view
CREATE VIEW IF NOT EXISTS v_emergency AS
SELECT a.admission_id, a.patient_id, a.admission_date,
       a.specialty, p.sex, p.lsoa_code
FROM admissions a
JOIN patients p ON p.patient_id = a.patient_id
WHERE a.admission_method = 'Emergency';

SELECT specialty, COUNT(*) AS n
FROM v_emergency
GROUP BY specialty
ORDER BY n DESC;
::p
**Tables from queries** store the *result* — a frozen snapshot. `CREATE TABLE cohort_2025 AS SELECT …` (T-SQL spelling: `SELECT … INTO cohort_2025 FROM …`). Use for expensive results you'll reuse, or cohorts that must not shift while you analyse them. Snapshots go **stale by design** — name them with dates (`cohort_20250401`) and record the creation query.

**Temp tables** live only for your session — `CREATE TEMP TABLE t AS SELECT …` (SQLite), `#t` (SQL Server) — ideal for multi-step working that's too big for one statement but not worth keeping.

Rules of the road in shared systems: create views/tables **only in your sandbox schema**; prefix or folder them by project; never `DROP` anything you didn't create; and remember `DROP TABLE` / `DELETE` are immediate and irreversible — in the playground you can experiment freely (Reset database restores everything), which is precisely what it's for.
::quiz
Q Analysis code queries v_emergency every morning. Overnight, new admissions arrive. The view…
* Reflects them automatically — it re-runs its definition each time
- Shows yesterday's data until refreshed
- Must be dropped and recreated daily
- Locks the source table
! Views store logic, not rows: always-current by construction. A CREATE TABLE AS snapshot is the one that freezes.
::quiz
Q You need this quarter's cohort to stay IDENTICAL for six weeks of analysis while source data keeps flowing. Best tool?
- A view
* A snapshot table (CREATE TABLE … AS), dated and documented
- A temp table
- Re-running the cohort query each time
! Stability requires freezing rows. Views drift with the source; temp tables die with the session; re-running invites silent cohort drift.
::ex
In the playground: create a view v_los containing admission_id, specialty and length of stay in days (completed spells only); then query it for mean LOS by specialty. Finally, tidy up with DROP VIEW v_los;
~~~sql
CREATE VIEW v_los AS
SELECT admission_id, specialty,
       CAST(julianday(discharge_date) - julianday(admission_date) AS INTEGER) AS los_days
FROM admissions
WHERE discharge_date IS NOT NULL;

SELECT specialty, ROUND(AVG(los_days), 1) AS mean_los
FROM v_los
GROUP BY specialty;

DROP VIEW v_los;
::key
- Views = stored logic, always current; snapshot tables = frozen results, dated; temp tables = session scratch.
- Choose by question: shared definition → view; stable cohort → snapshot; working steps → temp.
- Create only in your sandbox; drop only what you made.
#LESSON 4|Welcome to R: console, scripts and projects
::p
SQL fetched and shaped data *inside* the database. **R** is where analysis lives: statistics, charts, reports — and it's the de facto standard across UKHSA and a large slice of NHS analytics (the NHS-R community exists for a reason). Open RStudio (installed in Lesson 5) and get oriented:
- **Console** (bottom-left): type, Enter, instant result. For experiments.
- **Source** (top-left): script files (`.R`) you save and run — your real work. Run the current line with **Ctrl+Enter** (Cmd+Enter on Mac); this shortcut is 90% of your keystrokes.
- **Environment** (top-right): the objects (datasets, values) currently in memory.
- **Files / Plots / Packages / Help** (bottom-right): `?mean` opens help for any function.
::code|r|Type these in the Console
1200 / 30                 # arithmetic
#> [1] 40
sqrt(144)                 # functions: name(arguments)
#> [1] 12
round(3.14159, digits = 2) # named arguments read better
#> [1] 3.14
# Anything after a hash is a comment - R ignores it
::p
Lines beginning `#>` show what R prints back — a convention this course uses throughout so you can check yourself.

**Work in Projects from day one.** File ▸ New Project ▸ New Directory ▸ name it `ph-course-r`. A project pins R's working directory to that folder, so relative paths like `data/admissions.csv` behave identically on your machine and a colleague's — the R answer to the portability problem from Lesson 2. Inside it, create `data/`, `R/` (scripts) and `outputs/` folders. And begin the habit that defines professionals: **everything in a script**. The console is for trying; the script is the record. If it only happened in the console, it didn't happen.
::note|info|One tool, every OS
Unlike installation, the R language itself is identical on Windows, macOS and Linux — every lesson from here runs unchanged on all three. The rare OS-specific moments (paths, database drivers, scheduling) are flagged when they arrive in Module 7.
::quiz
Q Ctrl+Enter (Cmd+Enter) in the Source pane does what?
- Saves the file
* Runs the current line or selection in the Console
- Opens a new script
- Comments the line
! It sends code from your script to the console — the core loop of writing R: edit line, run line, look, repeat.
::quiz
Q Why start every piece of work as an RStudio Project?
- Projects run faster
* The working directory is pinned, so relative paths work for anyone who opens the project
- R requires it
- Projects enable colour themes
! Portability and reproducibility: data/file.csv resolves the same way on every machine that opens the .Rproj.
::ex
Create the ph-course-r project with data/, R/ and outputs/ folders. Make a script R/lesson42.R containing three commands: 52 * 7; round(365.25/12, 1); and a comment with today's date. Run each with Ctrl+Enter.
~~~r
# lesson42.R - first script, 2026-XX-XX
52 * 7
#> [1] 364
round(365.25 / 12, 1)
#> [1] 30.4
::key
- Console experiments; Source scripts record; Ctrl/Cmd+Enter runs the current line.
- Always work inside an RStudio Project with data/, R/, outputs/ folders.
- #> lines in this course show expected output — verify as you go.
#LESSON 4|Objects and vectors: R's building blocks
::p
R stores things in named **objects** using the assignment arrow `<-` (type it with Alt+minus in RStudio; `=` mostly works but `<-` is universal R style):
::code|r|Assignment and vectors
la_name  <- "Barchester"
patients <- 640
rate     <- 1000 * 120 / 40000

ages <- c(67, 45, 81, 23, 59)     # c() combines values into a vector
ages
#> [1] 67 45 81 23 59
length(ages)
#> [1] 5
ages[1]        # square brackets extract - R counts from 1
#> [1] 67
ages[c(2, 4)]
#> [1] 45 23
::p
The **vector** — an ordered collection of values, all the same type — is R's atom; even `patients` above is a vector of length 1. The types you'll meet daily:
- **numeric** — `c(67, 45, 3.2)`
- **character** — text in quotes: `c("Emergency", "Elective")`
- **logical** — `TRUE` / `FALSE` (capitals, unquoted)
- **Date** — coming in Lesson 54; and **factor** (categories) in Lesson 55.

`class(x)` reveals a vector's type, and type explains behaviour: `"7" + 1` is an error because `"7"` is text. A whole column of a health extract arriving as character when it should be numeric (thanks to one stray `"N/A"`) is a rite of passage — now you'll recognise it.

Naming style: **snake_case**, descriptive, lowercase — `mean_los`, `emergency_admissions`, `imd_decile`. R is case-sensitive: `Ages` and `ages` are different objects, and the error `object 'Ages' not found` is R telling you exactly that.
::quiz
Q After x <- c(10, 20, 30), what does x[2] return?
- 10
* 20
- c(10, 20)
- An error
! R indexes from 1 (unlike Python's 0). Position 2 holds 20.
::quiz
Q class(c("48", "51", "N/A")) is…
- numeric
* character
- logical
- factor
! Quotes make text, and one non-number would force the whole vector to character anyway — the classic reason an imported column won't do arithmetic.
::ex
Create a vector monthly_admissions with values 112, 98, 130, 121, 145 (May-September). Extract: (a) the third value; (b) the first and last by position; (c) the vector's length; (d) its class.
~~~r
monthly_admissions <- c(112, 98, 130, 121, 145)
monthly_admissions[3]
#> [1] 130
monthly_admissions[c(1, 5)]
#> [1] 112 145
length(monthly_admissions)
#> [1] 5
class(monthly_admissions)
#> [1] "numeric"
::key
- name <- value assigns; c() builds vectors; [ ] extracts, counting from 1.
- Types (numeric, character, logical) drive behaviour; class() diagnoses.
- snake_case names; R is case-sensitive.
#LESSON 4|Vectorised thinking, logical tests and NA
::p
The idea that makes R feel magical after SQL: operations apply to **whole vectors at once** — no loops required:
::code|r|Vectorised arithmetic
admissions <- c(120, 80, 95)
population <- c(40000, 26000, 31000)

rate_per_1000 <- 1000 * admissions / population   # element by element
round(rate_per_1000, 1)
#> [1] 3.0 3.1 3.1
::p
Comparisons are vectorised too, yielding **logical vectors** — and logicals have a superpower: `sum()` treats TRUE as 1, so counting-things-meeting-a-condition is one line:
::code|r|Logical vectors do the counting
hba1c <- c(41, 52, 48, 39, 66, 47)
hba1c >= 48
#> [1] FALSE  TRUE  TRUE FALSE  TRUE FALSE
sum(hba1c >= 48)        # how many at/over threshold
#> [1] 3
mean(hba1c >= 48)       # what proportion
#> [1] 0.5
hba1c[hba1c >= 48]      # filter: keep where TRUE
#> [1] 52 48 66
::p
Combine conditions with `&` (and), `|` (or), `!` (not) — single symbols, unlike SQL's words.

**NA** is R's NULL-cousin: missing. Like SQL's NULL it poisons calculations — `mean(c(41, NA, 52))` is `NA` — but R makes you opt in to skipping: `mean(x, na.rm = TRUE)`. Test for missingness with `is.na(x)` (never `x == NA`, which — exactly like SQL — returns NA). `sum(is.na(x))` counts the missing, your first data-quality reflex in R.
::code|r|Handling NA deliberately
values <- c(41, NA, 52, 48, NA)
mean(values)
#> [1] NA
mean(values, na.rm = TRUE)
#> [1] 47
sum(is.na(values))
#> [1] 2
::quiz
Q sum(los_days > 7) computes…
- Total days over a week
* How many stays exceeded 7 days
- An error: sum needs numbers
- The longest stay
! The comparison makes TRUE/FALSE per stay; sum counts the TRUEs. mean() of the same gives the proportion.
::quiz
Q mean(c(10, 20, NA)) returns NA because…
- R cannot average three values
- NA equals zero
* Missing propagates: the true mean is unknowable unless you explicitly exclude NA
- The vector is character
! Same philosophy as SQL NULL, but R demands the explicit choice: na.rm = TRUE says "ignore missing, and I know I'm doing it".
::ex
Given sbp <- c(128, 152, NA, 141, 119, 160, NA, 135): (a) count missing readings; (b) count readings of 140+ (excluding NA - hint: na.rm works in sum too); (c) proportion of non-missing readings that are 140+; (d) the mean of readings under 140.
~~~r
sbp <- c(128, 152, NA, 141, 119, 160, NA, 135)
sum(is.na(sbp))
#> [1] 2
sum(sbp >= 140, na.rm = TRUE)
#> [1] 3
mean(sbp >= 140, na.rm = TRUE)
#> [1] 0.5
mean(sbp[sbp < 140], na.rm = TRUE)
#> [1] 127.3333
::key
- Operations and comparisons act on whole vectors; sum()/mean() of logicals count and proportion.
- & | ! combine conditions; [logical] filters.
- NA propagates until you opt out with na.rm = TRUE; is.na() tests; never == NA.
#LESSON 4|Data frames and tibbles: tables arrive in R
::p
A **data frame** is R's table: columns are vectors (each one type), rows are records — your SQL result sets reborn. The tidyverse's refined version is the **tibble**; we'll use tibbles throughout (they print politely and complain early about mistakes). Build one by hand with `tibble()`, or row-wise with `tribble()`:
::code|r|Your first tibble
library(tibble)     # after Lesson 46 you'll load the whole tidyverse

practices <- tribble(
  ~practice_code, ~practice_name,        ~la_name,    ~list_size,
  "P81001",       "Riverside Surgery",   "Barchester", 8200,
  "P81002",       "High Street Practice","Barchester", 5400,
  "P81003",       "Millford Health Ctr", "Millford",   11250,
  "P81004",       "Bay Medical Group",   "Kingsbay",   9100
)
practices
#> # A tibble: 4 x 4
#>   practice_code practice_name         la_name    list_size
#>   <chr>         <chr>                 <chr>          <dbl>
#> 1 P81001        Riverside Surgery     Barchester      8200
#> ...
::p
The printout is information-dense: dimensions (`4 x 4`), column types (`<chr>` character, `<dbl>` numeric/double), then rows. First-look tools for any table:
::code|r|Inspecting a data frame
nrow(practices);  ncol(practices)
names(practices)
dplyr::glimpse(practices)     # every column, type, first values - use constantly
summary(practices$list_size) # $ pulls one column out as a vector
#>  Min. 1st Qu. Median  Mean 3rd Qu. Max.
#>  5400    7500   8650  8488    9638 11250
::p
`glimpse()` is the R twin of your SQL habit of DISTINCT-and-LIMIT on new tables: run it on everything you import, every time. The `$` operator extracts a single column as a vector — so all of Lesson 44 applies: `sum(practices$list_size)`, `practices$list_size >= 9000`. (Base R's `practices[rows, cols]` bracket indexing exists and you'll read it in older code, but dplyr — next lessons — is how modern health teams write.)
::quiz
Q In a tibble printout, <chr> and <dbl> under the column names tell you…
- Column widths
* Each column's type: character and double (numeric)
- Compression settings
- Primary keys
! Types at a glance — the fastest way to spot the "numbers arrived as text" problem before it bites.
::quiz
Q practices$list_size returns…
- A one-column tibble
* A plain numeric vector of the list sizes
- The first row
- An error
! $ extracts the column as a vector, ready for sum(), mean(), comparisons — the bridge between tables and Lesson 44's vector skills.
::ex
Build a tibble las with columns la_name (Barchester, Millford, Kingsbay) and population (40000, 26000, 31000). Then: glimpse it; extract the population column; compute total population; and a logical showing which LAs exceed 30,000.
~~~r
library(tibble)
las <- tribble(
  ~la_name,     ~population,
  "Barchester",  40000,
  "Millford",    26000,
  "Kingsbay",    31000
)
dplyr::glimpse(las)
las$population
#> [1] 40000 26000 31000
sum(las$population)
#> [1] 97000
las$population > 30000
#> [1]  TRUE FALSE  TRUE
::key
- Tibbles = typed columns (vectors) + rows; the printout shows dimensions and types.
- glimpse() every new table; $ extracts a column as a vector.
- tribble() builds small tables readably — handy for lookups and examples.
#LESSON 4|Packages: installing R's superpowers (and the tidyverse)
::p
Base R is capable; **packages** make it formidable. A package is a bundle of functions others wrote, published on **CRAN** (the quality-controlled central archive). Two verbs, one crucial difference:
::code|r|Install once, load every session
install.packages("tidyverse")   # downloads to your library - ONCE per machine
library(tidyverse)              # loads it - EVERY session/script that uses it
::p
The **tidyverse** is the modern-R toolkit this course uses — dplyr (manipulation), ggplot2 (charts), readr (import), tidyr, stringr, forcats, lubridate and friends, sharing one coherent grammar. NHS-R and UKHSA code is overwhelmingly tidyverse-shaped. Installation is the one moment R touches your operating system, so:
::oscode|r
@win
# Windows: install.packages("tidyverse") usually "just works"
# (CRAN ships pre-built binaries).
# If a package ever says it needs compilation "from source",
# install Rtools from https://cran.r-project.org/bin/windows/Rtools/
# then restart RStudio and retry.
install.packages("tidyverse")
@mac
# macOS: binaries also provided; occasionally compilation tools are wanted.
# If prompted about developer tools, run once in Terminal:
#   xcode-select --install
install.packages("tidyverse")
@linux
# Linux compiles most packages from source, so system libraries matter.
# Debian/Ubuntu, before installing heavy packages:
#   sudo apt install r-base-dev libcurl4-openssl-dev libssl-dev libxml2-dev
install.packages("tidyverse")   # then this compiles happily (takes a while)
::p
Etiquette and gotchas: `library()` calls sit **at the top of every script**, so a reader instantly sees dependencies; never bury an `install.packages()` inside a script that others run (it changes *their* machine — leave it commented, or use dependency tooling later). If two packages export the same function name (dplyr and base both have `filter`), R uses the most recently loaded — write `dplyr::filter()` to be explicit when it matters. `packageVersion("dplyr")` tells you what you're running; versions matter for reproducibility, a Module 7 theme. On locked-down NHS machines, packages may come from an internal mirror or need an IT request — the incantations are identical.
::quiz
Q install.packages() vs library() — the right mental model is…
- Both must run every session
* Install downloads once per machine; library loads once per session
- library installs; install.packages loads
- Only one is ever needed
! Installing puts the package on disk; loading attaches it to the running session. Scripts assume installed, and declare loads at the top.
::quiz
Q A script for colleagues should contain install.packages("tidyverse")…
- At the top, always
- After library()
* Not as live code — installing changes other people's machines without asking
- Inside a loop
! Declare dependencies with library() and document installs (comment/README); don't silently modify a colleague's library.
::ex
Install the tidyverse (watch your OS note above), then write a script header that loads it, prints your dplyr version, and confirms filter comes from dplyr by calling dplyr::filter on a tiny tibble.
~~~r
library(tidyverse)

packageVersion("dplyr")
#> [1] '1.1.x'  (any recent version is fine)

dplyr::filter(tibble(x = 1:5), x > 3)
#> # A tibble: 2 x 1
#>       x
#> 1     4
#> 2     5
::key
- CRAN packages: install.packages() once per machine, library() per session, declared at the top.
- The tidyverse is the UK-health-analytics dialect of R; OS build tools (Rtools/xcode/dev libs) fix "from source" errors.
- package::function() disambiguates name clashes; packageVersion() records what ran.
#LESSON 4|Importing data: read_csv and friends
::p
Analysis begins when data enters R. The tidyverse importer is `readr::read_csv()` — fast, predictable, and honest about what it did:
::code|r|Importing a CSV
library(tidyverse)

admissions <- read_csv("data/admissions.csv")
#> Rows: 900 Columns: 7
#> -- Column specification ---------------------------
#> chr  (4): discharge_date, admission_method, specialty, primary_diagnosis
#> dbl  (2): admission_id, patient_id
#> date (1): admission_date
glimpse(admissions)
::p
**Get a real file to practise on:** open this page's SQL playground, run `SELECT * FROM admissions;`, and press **Download CSV** — save it into your project's `data/` folder. (Any query's results export the same way, so you can manufacture practice files all course long.)

Read the import message like a QA report: row count plausible? Types right? A date column arriving as `chr` means the format wasn't ISO — the **UK trap**, since British DD/MM/YYYY files confuse default parsers. Fixes:
::code|r|UK dates and messy values
# Tell readr the format explicitly:
admissions <- read_csv("data/admissions.csv",
                       col_types = cols(admission_date = col_date("%d/%m/%Y")))

# Declare the strings that mean "missing":
labs <- read_csv("data/labs.csv", na = c("", "NA", "N/A", "NULL", "Unknown"))
::p
Other arrivals you'll meet: **Excel** via `readxl::read_excel("file.xlsx", sheet = "Data", skip = 2)` (skip letterhead rows); **semicolon-separated European CSVs** via `read_csv2()`; **fixed messy headers** — `janitor::clean_names()` converts `"Admission Date "` into `admission_date` and is worth running on almost every import. Two habits that mark professionals: **never edit the raw file** (fix in code, so the fix reruns next month), and **glimpse + summary immediately after every import** — Lesson 39's DQ instinct, now in R.
::quiz
Q read_csv reports a date-of-birth column as <chr>. Most likely cause and fix?
- The file is corrupt; re-download
* Non-ISO (e.g. DD/MM/YYYY) dates; specify col_date("%d/%m/%Y") or parse with lubridate
- Dates cannot be imported
- Use Excel instead
! Character-typed dates almost always mean an unrecognised format. Declare it at import (or dmy() later - Lesson 54).
::quiz
Q Why fix a broken header in code (clean_names()) rather than editing the CSV in Excel?
- Excel cannot edit CSVs
* The raw file stays pristine and the fix re-applies automatically to next month's file
- clean_names is faster to type
- Headers do not matter
! Reproducibility again: raw data immutable, transformations scripted. Next refresh costs zero clicks.
::ex
Export patients from the playground (SELECT * FROM patients;) to data/patients.csv. Import it with read_csv, glimpse it, and report: rows, columns, the type of birth_date, and how many NAs ethnicity has (sum(is.na(...))).
~~~r
library(tidyverse)
patients <- read_csv("data/patients.csv")
glimpse(patients)
#> Rows: ~600, Columns: 6
#> birth_date imports as <date> (ISO format - one benefit of Lesson 10's advice)
sum(is.na(patients$ethnicity))
#> [1] (a smallish number - some ethnicities are unrecorded, as in real data)
::key
- read_csv + immediate glimpse/summary; read the import message as a QA report.
- UK DD/MM/YYYY needs col_date("%d/%m/%Y") (or lubridate::dmy); declare na strings.
- readxl for Excel, clean_names() for headers; never hand-edit raw files.
#LESSON 4|dplyr part 1: filter rows, arrange order
::p
**dplyr** gives five verbs that cover most manipulation; each takes a data frame first and returns a new one, original untouched. First pair — and their SQL twins:

**filter() = WHERE.** Conditions use Lesson 44's comparisons; commas mean AND:
::code|r|filter in action
library(tidyverse)
admissions <- read_csv("data/admissions.csv")   # from Lesson 47

emergency_resp <- filter(admissions,
                         admission_method == "Emergency",
                         specialty %in% c("Respiratory", "General Medicine"),
                         admission_date >= as.Date("2025-04-01"))
nrow(emergency_resp)
::p
Watch the details: **`==` for equality** (single `=` inside filter is an argument-assignment error you'll make exactly once); `%in%` is SQL's IN; OR is `|` with parentheses to taste; and **NA behaves like SQL NULL** — `filter(x > 5)` silently drops NA rows, so the "keep unknowns too" version is `filter(x > 5 | is.na(x))`.

**arrange() = ORDER BY.** Ascending default; `desc()` flips:
::code|r|arrange in action
arrange(admissions, desc(admission_date), patient_id)   # newest first
::p
The "top n" idiom gets its own verb: `slice_max(admissions, order_by = admission_date, n = 5)` (and `slice_min`, `slice_head`, `slice_sample` for a random peek). Everything composes: filter, then arrange, then slice — though chaining reads far better with the pipe, arriving in Lesson 50.
::quiz
Q filter(labs, value >= 48) on a column containing NAs will…
- Keep the NA rows
* Drop the NA rows silently, exactly like SQL's WHERE
- Error
- Convert NA to 0
! Comparisons with NA are NA, and filter keeps only TRUE. Add | is.na(value) when unknowns should stay — a deliberate choice either way.
::quiz
Q The dplyr equivalent of SQL's WHERE la IN ('Barchester','Millford') is…
- filter(df, la == c("Barchester","Millford"))
* filter(df, la %in% c("Barchester","Millford"))
- filter(df, la = "Barchester" OR "Millford")
- arrange(df, la)
! %in% tests membership. == against a vector recycles element-by-element - a subtle wrong answer, not an error, which makes it worse.
::ex
From admissions: keep Elective admissions in financial year 2024/25 (dates 2024-04-01 to 2025-03-31 inclusive), arrange by specialty then newest admission_date first, and show the top 5 longest… no - keep it to filter/arrange: show the first 5 rows via slice_head(n = 5).
~~~r
fy2425_elective <- filter(admissions,
                          admission_method == "Elective",
                          admission_date >= as.Date("2024-04-01"),
                          admission_date <= as.Date("2025-03-31"))
result <- arrange(fy2425_elective, specialty, desc(admission_date))
slice_head(result, n = 5)
::key
- filter() = WHERE (== , %in%, & | !, commas = AND); NA rows drop unless invited.
- arrange() = ORDER BY, with desc(); slice_max/min = top-n.
- Verbs return new data frames; originals are never modified in place.
#LESSON 4|dplyr part 2: select, mutate and rename columns
::p
**select() chooses columns** — SQL's SELECT list, with power tools:
::code|r|Selecting columns
select(admissions, admission_id, admission_date, specialty)  # name them
select(admissions, -primary_diagnosis)                       # everything except
select(admissions, patient_id, starts_with("admission"))     # helpers:
# starts_with, ends_with, contains, where(is.numeric)...
::p
**mutate() creates or transforms columns** — SQL's computed expressions, vectorised over the whole table:
::code|r|Creating columns
admissions2 <- mutate(admissions,
  los_days   = as.numeric(discharge_date - admission_date),  # Date maths just works
  is_emerg   = admission_method == "Emergency",              # logical flag
  los_band   = case_when(
    is.na(discharge_date) ~ "Still in",
    los_days == 0         ~ "Same day",
    los_days <= 7         ~ "1-7 days",
    .default              = "Over a week"
  )
)
::p
`case_when()` is CASE WHEN reborn: `condition ~ value` pairs, evaluated top-down, `.default` as the ELSE. (In older code you'll see `TRUE ~ "Over a week"` for the default — same thing.) Simpler two-way splits: `if_else(condition, yes, no)`.

**rename(new = old)** fixes names — `rename(admissions, method = admission_method)` — and pairs with `janitor::clean_names()` from Lesson 47. Column order for reports: `relocate(los_days, .after = discharge_date)`. One discipline: mutate freely during analysis, but *derive*, don't overwrite — `los_days_capped = pmin(los_days, 30)` beats destroying the original, because you will want it back.
::quiz
Q In case_when(), a row matching two conditions gets…
- Both values pasted
- The last match
* The first matching condition's value, top-down — same rule as SQL CASE
- NA
! Order conditions most-specific first, exactly as you learned in Lesson 19.
::quiz
Q select(df, -patient_id) does what?
- Deletes patient_id from the source file
* Returns a copy without the patient_id column
- Sorts by patient_id descending
- Renames patient_id
! Minus excludes. The source object (and file) are untouched — dplyr always returns new data.
::ex
On lab_results (export from the playground if needed): keep only patient_id, test, value, date_taken; create flag_high = TRUE for HbA1c >= 48 or Systolic BP >= 140 (FALSE otherwise, using case_when with .default = FALSE); rename date_taken to test_date.
~~~r
labs2 <- lab_results |> ... # pipe comes next lesson; without it:
labs_small <- select(lab_results, patient_id, test, value, date_taken)
labs_flagged <- mutate(labs_small,
  flag_high = case_when(
    test == "HbA1c"       & value >= 48  ~ TRUE,
    test == "Systolic BP" & value >= 140 ~ TRUE,
    .default = FALSE
  )
)
labs_final <- rename(labs_flagged, test_date = date_taken)
glimpse(labs_final)
::key
- select() picks/drops columns (helpers: starts_with, contains); rename(new = old).
- mutate() computes vectorised columns; case_when() is your CASE WHEN, if_else() the quick binary.
- Derive new columns rather than overwrite; keep raw values recoverable.
#LESSON 4|The pipe, group_by and summarise: the analysis engine
::p
Nesting verbs inside-out reads horribly. The **pipe** `|>` (say "then") passes the left side in as the right side's first argument, so pipelines read top-to-bottom like a recipe:
::code|r|The same logic, three ways
# Nested (hard to read):
arrange(filter(admissions, admission_method == "Emergency"), desc(admission_date))

# Piped (professional style):
admissions |>
  filter(admission_method == "Emergency") |>
  arrange(desc(admission_date))
::p
(Older code uses magrittr's `%>%` — read them as identical; write the native `|>`. RStudio inserts it with Ctrl+Shift+M once you set Tools ▸ Global Options ▸ Code ▸ "Use native pipe".)

Now the payoff pair. **group_by() + summarise()** is GROUP BY + aggregates, and together with the pipe it is *the* shape of descriptive health analysis:
::code|r|Admissions summarised by specialty
admissions |>
  mutate(los_days = as.numeric(discharge_date - admission_date)) |>
  group_by(specialty, admission_method) |>
  summarise(
    n          = n(),                              # COUNT(*)
    patients   = n_distinct(patient_id),           # COUNT(DISTINCT ...)
    mean_los   = mean(los_days, na.rm = TRUE),
    median_los = median(los_days, na.rm = TRUE),   # trivial in R!
    .groups = "drop"                               # ungroup afterwards
  ) |>
  arrange(desc(n))
::p
Everything from SQL transfers — and improves: `median()`, `quantile()`, `sd()` are one word each. `count(df, specialty, sort = TRUE)` shortcuts the commonest case. Grouped **mutate** (no collapsing — SQL's window functions!) also works: `group_by(patient_id) |> mutate(test_number = row_number())`. Include `.groups = "drop"` (or `ungroup()`) after summarising: forgotten groups silently haunt later steps — the R equivalent of SQLite's quiet GROUP BY traps.
::quiz
Q df |> filter(x > 5) |> summarise(m = mean(x)) — the pipe's job here is…
- To run both functions in parallel
* To pass each result as the first argument of the next verb, top to bottom
- To save df to disk
- Decoration
! Left-hand result flows into the next call's data slot. Recipes, not nesting.
::quiz
Q After group_by(la_name), the difference between summarise(n = n()) and mutate(n = n()) is…
- None
* summarise collapses to one row per LA; mutate keeps all rows, attaching each LA's n
- mutate errors on grouped data
- summarise needs ungroup first
! Collapse vs annotate — the same distinction as SQL aggregates vs window functions, in two verbs.
::ex
Pipeline on lab_results: keep HbA1c tests; group by patient_id; summarise tests = n(), latest = max(date_taken), mean_value = mean(value); then keep patients with 2+ tests and arrange by mean_value descending, top 10 via slice_head.
~~~r
lab_results |>
  filter(test == "HbA1c") |>
  group_by(patient_id) |>
  summarise(tests = n(),
            latest = max(date_taken),
            mean_value = mean(value),
            .groups = "drop") |>
  filter(tests >= 2) |>            # HAVING, in R clothing
  arrange(desc(mean_value)) |>
  slice_head(n = 10)
::key
- |> pipes data through verbs: read "then"; write pipelines, not nests.
- group_by + summarise = GROUP BY; n(), n_distinct(), median() included; drop groups after.
- group_by + mutate = window functions (annotate without collapsing).
#LESSON 4|Joins in R: left_join, inner_join and the mighty anti_join
::p
dplyr joins mirror SQL exactly — same concepts, same traps, friendlier syntax:
::code|r|Joining patients to their deprivation decile
patients_imd <- patients |>
  left_join(lsoa_imd, by = "lsoa_code")

# Different key names on each side:
adm_full <- admissions |>
  left_join(patients, by = join_by(patient_id)) |>
  left_join(lsoa_imd,  by = join_by(lsoa_code))
::p
- `inner_join()` keeps matches only; `left_join()` keeps all left rows (your default, as in SQL); `full_join()` keeps both sides.
- **Fan-out still applies** (Lesson 27!): joining patients→admissions duplicates patients. Modern dplyr even warns on unexpected many-to-many — read that warning, don't silence it.
- Suffixes: shared non-key columns become `la_name.x` / `la_name.y`; better to select/rename before joining.

Two joins with no SQL keyword of their own earn their keep in QA:
::code|r|Filtering joins
# semi_join: keep patients WHO HAVE at least one emergency admission
frequent_flyers <- patients |>
  semi_join(filter(admissions, admission_method == "Emergency"),
            by = "patient_id")

# anti_join: patients with NO vaccination record at all (Lesson 26's pattern, one verb)
never_vaccinated <- patients |>
  anti_join(vaccinations, by = "patient_id")
nrow(never_vaccinated)
::p
`semi_join` filters by existence (no fan-out, no new columns); `anti_join` by absence. The professional reconciliation habit: after any important join, `anti_join` both directions and *look at what didn't match* — unmatched rows are where data quality stories live.
::quiz
Q anti_join(children, mmr_records, by = "patient_id") returns…
- Children with exactly one MMR record
- All rows from both tables
