* Children with no MMR record — the unvaccinated list
- MMR records with no child
! Anti = present left, absent right. Reverse the table order for orphan MMR records (the other direction of QA).
::quiz
Q semi_join differs from inner_join how?
- It is slower
* It filters the left table by existence of a match — no columns added, no row duplication
- It keeps unmatched rows
- No difference
! semi_join answers "which left rows have a match?" without inheriting fan-out — often exactly what a cohort filter wants.
::ex
Using anti_join twice on admissions and patients: (a) count admissions whose patient_id has no patients row (orphans); (b) count patients with no admissions at all. Then use semi_join to build a tibble of patients who HAVE at least one admission.
~~~r
admissions |> anti_join(patients, by = "patient_id") |> nrow()   # (a) orphans
patients   |> anti_join(admissions, by = "patient_id") |> nrow() # (b) never admitted
ever_admitted <- patients |> semi_join(admissions, by = "patient_id")
::key
- left_join is the default; inner drops non-matches; fan-out and its warnings still apply.
- semi_join = filter by existence; anti_join = filter by absence (the anti-join, named).
- Reconcile every important join by anti_joining both directions and inspecting.
#LESSON 4|Tidy data: pivot_longer and pivot_wider
::p
**Tidy data** = one variable per column, one observation per row, one value per cell. Analysis tools (dplyr, ggplot2, models) want tidy; humans and Excel exports love *wide* — months spread across columns, one row per practice. Reshaping between them is two functions.

**pivot_longer(): wide → tidy.** The classic import from a performance spreadsheet:
::code|r|Melting month columns into rows
wide <- tribble(
  ~practice, ~`2025-04`, ~`2025-05`, ~`2025-06`,
  "P81001",        42,        51,        47,
  "P81002",        30,        28,        35
)

long <- wide |>
  pivot_longer(cols = -practice,          # everything except practice
               names_to  = "month",
               values_to = "admissions")
long
#> # A tibble: 6 x 3
#>   practice month   admissions
#>   P81001   2025-04         42
#>   P81001   2025-05         51
#>   ...
::p
Now `group_by(month)`, joins, and charts all work — three columns, however many months arrive next quarter. **pivot_wider(): tidy → presentation**, for the report table humans expect:
::code|r|Casting back to wide
long |>
  pivot_wider(names_from = month, values_from = admissions)
::p
`values_fill = 0` supplies zeros where combinations are absent (the date-spine idea in reshape form); `values_fn = sum` resolves duplicates if several rows map to one cell — and if you *need* values_fn unexpectedly, that's a hint your data has duplicates to investigate first (Lesson 34 instincts). Rule of thumb: **store and analyse long; present wide** — pivot_wider is usually the last step before a table lands in a report.
::quiz
Q A CSV has columns: la_name, m_2404, m_2405, … m_2603 (24 month columns of counts). To chart a monthly trend you should first…
- pivot_wider
* pivot_longer the month columns into month + count
- Delete the months
- Transpose in Excel
! Charts and grouped summaries want one row per LA-month. Longer first; the month names become data you can parse into dates.
::quiz
Q pivot_wider needs values_fn = sum to work. The professional reaction is…
- Add it and move on
* Pause: multiple rows per cell means duplicates or a missing grouping variable — investigate before aggregating
- Use values_fill instead
- Switch to pivot_longer
! values_fn silently aggregates collisions. Sometimes correct — but only after you know why collisions exist.
::ex
Given long data la_name, month, deaths for 3 LAs x 4 months (make it with tribble or expand from imagination): produce the wide report table (months as columns, values_fill = 0), then pivot it back to long and confirm you recover 12 rows.
~~~r
long <- tribble(
  ~la_name,     ~month,    ~deaths,
  "Barchester", "2025-04",  8,
  "Barchester", "2025-05", 11,
  "Millford",   "2025-04",  5,
  "Kingsbay",   "2025-06",  7
)   # (any similar rows are fine)

wide <- long |>
  pivot_wider(names_from = month, values_from = deaths, values_fill = 0)

back <- wide |>
  pivot_longer(-la_name, names_to = "month", values_to = "deaths")
nrow(back)   # LAs x months present in wide
::key
- Tidy (long) for analysis: one variable per column, one observation per row.
- pivot_longer melts wide imports; pivot_wider casts presentation tables (values_fill for zeros).
- Needing values_fn is a duplicate-data smell — investigate, then aggregate.
#LESSON 4|Strings with stringr: cleaning the text you're given
::p
Health extracts arrive with trailing spaces, inconsistent case, codes embedded in text. **stringr** (loaded with the tidyverse) gives consistent `str_*` functions — vectorised, so they slot straight into mutate:
::code|r|The daily string toolkit
codes <- c(" e10.9", "J18.9 ", "i21.0", "J45.9")

str_trim(codes)                    # strip outer whitespace
str_to_upper(codes)                # case
str_sub(codes, 1, 1)               # slice: first character
str_detect(codes, "^J")            # regex test: starts with J
#> [1] FALSE  TRUE FALSE  TRUE
str_starts(str_trim(codes), "J")   # friendlier for simple prefixes
str_replace(codes, "\\.", "")      # first match; str_replace_all for all
str_length(str_trim(codes))
str_c("ICD:", str_trim(codes))     # concatenate (SQL's ||)
::p
That `"^J"` is a **regular expression** — a pattern mini-language. You need only a starter kit: `^` start, `$` end, `.` any character (so a literal dot is `\\.`), `[0-9]` a digit, `+` one-or-more. `"^J[0-9]+"` = starts with J then digits: respiratory-chapter ICD-10 codes. Anything fancier, look it up when needed.

The standardise-then-compare pattern, now in R (Lesson 16's twin):
::code|r|Defensive cleaning in a pipeline
admissions |>
  mutate(method_clean = str_to_title(str_trim(admission_method))) |>
  count(method_clean)
# "emergency ", "EMERGENCY", "Emergency" -> all "Emergency"
::p
And the extraction move that saves hours: `str_extract(text, "[0-9]{6}")` pulls the first 6-digit run (a practice code buried in a free-text field, say). `separate_wider_delim()` splits "Barchester - P81001" style columns into two.
::quiz
Q str_detect(diagnosis, "^J") returns…
- The J codes themselves
* TRUE/FALSE per element: does it start with J?
- The position of J
- An error on NA
! detect = logical test (perfect inside filter). str_extract returns the matched text; str_which the positions.
::quiz
Q To match a literal full stop in a regex you write…
- .
- 'dot'
* \\. (backslash-escaped, doubled in R strings)
- [.]. is impossible
! Unescaped . means "any character". In R source you type two backslashes to send one to the regex engine. ([.] also works.)
::ex
Clean a messy specialty vector: x <- c(" cardiology", "RESPIRATORY ", "Gen Med", "respiratory") — produce Title Case, trimmed values; count how many are Respiratory; and build labels like "Specialty: Cardiology" with str_c.
~~~r
x <- c(" cardiology", "RESPIRATORY ", "Gen Med", "respiratory")
clean <- str_to_title(str_trim(x))
clean
#> [1] "Cardiology" "Respiratory" "Gen Med" "Respiratory"
sum(clean == "Respiratory")
#> [1] 2
str_c("Specialty: ", clean)
::key
- str_trim + str_to_upper/title before comparing; str_detect for pattern filters.
- Regex starter kit: ^ $ . [0-9] + and \\. for a literal dot — learn more only as needed.
- str_extract/separate pull structure out of free-ish text.
#LESSON 4|Dates with lubridate: UK dates tamed
::p
**lubridate** (in the tidyverse) makes dates almost pleasant. Parsing is named after the order the human wrote: British dates are `dmy()`, ISO are `ymd()`, and each has a `_hm`/`_hms` sibling for times:
::code|r|Parsing every UK format you'll meet
dmy("03/11/2025");  dmy("3 November 2025");  dmy("03-11-25")
#> all -> "2025-11-03"
ymd("2025-11-03")                       # ISO
dmy_hm("03/11/2025 14:30")              # date-times
::p
Once parsed, extraction and arithmetic are civilised:
::code|r|Working with real dates
d <- ymd("2025-11-03")
year(d);  month(d);  day(d);  wday(d, label = TRUE)   # components
floor_date(d, "month")                  # 2025-11-01: the month key!
d + days(30);  d - months(6)            # shifting
as.numeric(ymd("2025-12-01") - ymd("2025-11-03"))     # gap in days: 28

# Exact age (handles leap years and un-had birthdays properly):
age <- floor(interval(ymd("1958-03-14"), ymd("2025-11-03")) / years(1))
#> 67
::p
`floor_date(x, "month")` replaces SQL's strftime month keys — group by it for monthly trends. And here is the **financial year function** you'll reuse forever (Lesson 21's logic, R-shaped):
::code|r|fy(): financial year labeller
fy <- function(date) {
  y <- if_else(month(date) >= 4, year(date), year(date) - 1)
  str_c(y, "/", str_sub(y + 1, 3, 4))
}
fy(ymd(c("2025-03-31", "2025-04-01", "2026-02-10")))
#> [1] "2024/25" "2025/26" "2025/26"
::p
(You're reading your first home-made function — Lesson 56 teaches the mechanics.) One caution: `Sys.Date()` is today, but pinning "today" into saved outputs makes them irreproducible — prefer explicit snapshot dates in anything published.
::quiz
Q A UK column of "31/03/2025" strings becomes real Dates via…
- ymd()
* dmy()
- mdy()
- as.numeric()
! Day-month-year order = dmy(). mdy() would happily misread 03/04 dates and corrupt a third of your rows silently — the ambiguity Lesson 10 warned about.
::quiz
Q floor_date(admission_date, "month") is the R equivalent of which SQL idiom?
- julianday differences
* strftime('%Y-%m', admission_date) month keys for grouping
- date('now')
- BETWEEN
! Both collapse dates to their month for trend grouping — floor_date returns a proper Date (the 1st), which charts even better.
::ex
Parse mixed <- c("14/03/1958", "01/04/2025", "29/02/2024") with dmy; compute each date's financial year with fy() above; and calculate exact age on 2026-03-31 for the first date.
~~~r
mixed <- dmy(c("14/03/1958", "01/04/2025", "29/02/2024"))
fy(mixed)
#> [1] "1957/58" "2025/26" "2023/24"
floor(interval(mixed[1], ymd("2026-03-31")) / years(1))
#> [1] 68
::key
- dmy() for British, ymd() for ISO; parse once, then components/arithmetic are easy.
- floor_date(x, "month") = month keys; interval()/years(1) = exact ages.
- Keep fy() in your toolkit; every UK analysis wants it eventually.
#LESSON 4|Factors with forcats: categories in the right order
::p
Character vectors sort alphabetically — which is why naive charts order age bands `"18-39", "40-64", "5-17", …` and month names `Apr, Aug, Dec…`. **Factors** are R's categorical type: values restricted to defined **levels**, in an order you control. **forcats** (`fct_*`) manages them:
::code|r|Age bands that behave
bands <- c("65-79", "0-4", "18-39", "80+", "5-17", "40-64", "18-39")

band_levels <- c("0-4", "5-17", "18-39", "40-64", "65-79", "80+")
bands_f <- factor(bands, levels = band_levels)

sort(bands_f)        # now sorts by meaning, not alphabet
table(bands_f)       # counts, in order, including zero-count levels
::p
Where factors earn their keep:
- **Ordering chart axes.** ggplot (next module) plots factor levels in order — set levels once, every chart obeys.
- **Ordering by value:** `fct_reorder(specialty, n)` sorts a bar chart by height; `fct_rev()` flips.
- **Lumping small groups:** `fct_lump_n(ethnicity, n = 4)` keeps the 4 commonest and pools the rest into "Other" — tidy tables and a first step toward small-number safety.
- **Recoding labels:** `fct_recode(sex, Female = "F", Male = "M")` (new = old).
- **Surfacing the unexpected:** a typo'd `"0-4 "` becomes its own level and shows up instantly in `table()` — data quality made visible.

The classic factor trap: a *numeric* column accidentally read as factor, where `as.numeric()` returns the level *codes* (1,2,3…) not the values. The safe incantation is `as.numeric(as.character(x))` — you'll meet this in older datasets and interview questions alike.
::quiz
Q Your bar chart shows age bands ordered 0-4, 18-39, 40-64, 5-17, 65-79, 80+. The fix is…
- Sort the data frame with arrange
* Make age_band a factor with levels in the correct order
- Rename 5-17 to 05-17
- Convert the bands to numbers
! Alphabetical "5" > "4" > "1" ordering is the giveaway of an unfactored category. (Renaming to 05-17 also works but changes your labels; factors fix order without touching text.)
::quiz
Q x is a factor with levels "3","7","12"; the value "7" under as.numeric(x) may return…
- 7
* 2 — the internal level code, not the value
- NA always
- "7"
! Factors store codes pointing at level labels. as.numeric(as.character(x)) recovers true values — a classic gotcha.
::ex
Given ethnicity <- c("White","Asian","White","Black","Mixed","White","Other","Asian","Chinese heritage","White"): make it a factor, lump to the top 3 plus "Other" with fct_lump_n, and produce an ordered count table with fct_infreq (most common first).
~~~r
ethnicity <- c("White","Asian","White","Black","Mixed","White",
               "Other","Asian","Chinese heritage","White")
eth_f <- ethnicity |>
  factor() |>
  fct_lump_n(n = 3) |>
  fct_infreq()
table(eth_f)
#> White (4), Asian (2), then remaining pooled into Other
::key
- Factors = categories with controlled, ordered levels; charts and tables obey them.
- fct_reorder (by value), fct_infreq (by frequency), fct_lump_n (pool small groups), fct_recode (relabel).
- Numeric-as-factor needs as.numeric(as.character(x)) — the level-code trap.
#LESSON 4|Writing your own functions
::p
When you copy-paste a block the third time, stop: **write a function**. Functions remove repetition, concentrate bugs in one fixable place, and turn your hard-won logic (fy()!) into one-word tools.
::code|r|Anatomy of a function
rate_per_1000 <- function(events, population, dp = 1) {
  round(1000 * events / population, dp)
}

rate_per_1000(120, 40000)
#> [1] 3
rate_per_1000(120, 40000, dp = 2)
#> [1] 3
rate_per_1000(c(120, 80, 95), c(40000, 26000, 31000))   # vectorised for free
#> [1] 3.0 3.1 3.1
::p
Pieces: a name (verb-ish, snake_case), arguments (with defaults where sensible, like `dp = 1`), a body, and the **last expression is the return value** (explicit `return()` exists but idiomatic R rarely needs it). Because the body is vector code, your function inherits vectorisation.

Add guard rails for functions others will use — fail loudly and early:
::code|r|Defensive version
rate_per_1000 <- function(events, population, dp = 1) {
  if (any(population <= 0, na.rm = TRUE)) {
    stop("population must be positive - check your denominator join")
  }
  round(1000 * events / population, dp)
}
::p
`if () { } else { }` here is **scalar** control flow — it decides what the *function* does once, unlike vectorised `if_else()`/`case_when()` which compute per element. New writers mixing those up is the second-commonest R confusion (after NA). Keep reusable functions in their own file — `R/functions.R` — and pull them into any script with `source("R/functions.R")`: your personal package in embryo.
::quiz
Q In R, a function returns…
- Whatever return() names, which is mandatory
* The value of the last evaluated expression (return() optional)
- Nothing unless printed
- Only single numbers
! Last expression wins. Explicit return() is for early exits; everyday functions just end with their answer.
::quiz
Q Inside a function deciding whether to stop() on bad input, you use if/else rather than if_else because…
- They are identical
* if/else makes one scalar decision about flow; if_else is a vectorised value-chooser per element
- if_else is deprecated
- stop() only works with if_else
! Control flow (do this or that, once) vs data transformation (a value for every row) — different tools for different jobs.
::ex
Write suppress_small(n, threshold = 5) returning "<5" (as character) when 0 < n < threshold, otherwise the number as character; make it vectorised with if_else/case_when; test on c(0, 3, 5, 17). (This is a real disclosure-control helper — Lesson 67 uses the idea in anger.)
~~~r
suppress_small <- function(n, threshold = 5) {
  case_when(
    n > 0 & n < threshold ~ "<5",
    .default = as.character(n)
  )
}
suppress_small(c(0, 3, 5, 17))
#> [1] "0"  "<5" "5"  "17"
::key
- Third copy-paste = write a function; defaults make it friendly, vector bodies make it fast.
- Last expression returns; stop() early on bad input with a helpful message.
- Scalar if/else steers functions; if_else/case_when transform columns. Keep functions in R/functions.R and source() them.
#LESSON 4|Iteration: for loops and purrr's map
::p
Vectorisation and grouped dplyr cover most repetition — reach for explicit iteration when the unit of work is bigger than a row: *per file*, *per organisation*, *per model*. R offers two styles.

**The for loop** — universal, readable:
::code|r|Reading a folder of monthly CSVs
files <- list.files("data/monthly", pattern = "\\.csv$", full.names = TRUE)

results <- list()
for (f in files) {
  results[[f]] <- read_csv(f, show_col_types = FALSE)
}
all_months <- bind_rows(results, .id = "source_file")
::p
**purrr::map()** — the tidyverse's functional style: apply a function to each element, collect the results. The same task in three lines:
::code|r|map: apply and combine
all_months <- map(files, read_csv, show_col_types = FALSE) |>
  bind_rows()

# Typed variants promise the output shape:
map_dbl(list(a = 1:5, b = 10:12), mean)   # always numbers back
#>    a    b
#>  3.0 11.0

# Anonymous functions for custom steps:
map_dbl(files, \(f) nrow(read_csv(f, show_col_types = FALSE)))
::p
`\(x)` is shorthand for `function(x)`. Which style? **Genuinely equivalent** for most analyst work — loops read plainly and debug easily; map is compact and composes with pipes (and `map_dfr`-style patterns dominate NHS-R code you'll inherit). Learn to *read* both, write whichever is clearer. Two disciplines either way: pre-decide how results combine (`bind_rows`, a named list), and make each iteration **independent** — no step relying on the previous loop's leftovers, which is what makes Module 7's parallel/automated pipelines possible.
::quiz
Q map_dbl(x, mean) differs from map(x, mean) how?
- It is faster
* It guarantees a numeric vector back (erroring if not), instead of a list
- It removes NAs
- It sorts the output
! Typed map_* variants are contracts about output shape — they fail loudly instead of returning a surprise list.
::ex
You have three LA names: las <- c("Barchester","Millford","Kingsbay"). Using map (or a for loop), produce a named list where each element is a one-row tibble: la_name plus a placeholder report_date = Sys.Date(); then bind_rows it. (Structure practice for Lesson 66's parameterised reports.)
~~~r
las <- c("Barchester", "Millford", "Kingsbay")

reports <- map(las, \(la) tibble(la_name = la, report_date = Sys.Date())) |>
  set_names(las) |>
  bind_rows(.id = "id")
reports
#> 3 rows, one per LA
::quiz
Q The best reason each loop iteration should be independent is…
- Loops error otherwise
* Independent steps can be re-run, re-ordered, parallelised and automated safely
- It uses less memory
- purrr requires it
! Hidden state between iterations breaks re-runs and scheduling. Independence is what lets Module 7 automate your work.
::key
- Vectorise first; iterate when the unit is a file/org/model.
- for loops and purrr::map are equally professional — read both, write the clearer one.
- Typed map_* variants enforce output shape; keep iterations independent and combine deliberately.
#LESSON 5|ggplot2: the grammar of graphics
::p
**ggplot2** builds charts from three declared parts: **data**, **aesthetics** (`aes()` — which columns map to x, y, colour…), and **geoms** (the marks: bars, lines, points), added with `+`. Once the grammar clicks, every chart is the same sentence with different words.
::code|r|First chart: admissions by specialty
library(tidyverse)

spec_counts <- admissions |>
  count(specialty, name = "n")

ggplot(spec_counts, aes(x = n, y = fct_reorder(specialty, n))) +
  geom_col()
::p
Read it: *take spec_counts; map count to x and specialty (ordered by count — Lesson 55 paying off) to y; draw columns.* Horizontal bars because specialty names are long — a small choice that makes NHS charts instantly more readable.

Two column-geoms to keep straight: `geom_col()` draws values you've already computed (paired with `count()`/`summarise` — the analyst default, because you can check the numbers first); `geom_bar()` counts raw rows itself. Add mappings and the chart grows:
::code|r|Mapping a third variable
admissions |>
  count(specialty, admission_method) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = n, y = fct_reorder(specialty, n), fill = admission_method)) +
  geom_col(position = "dodge")     # side-by-side; "stack" is the default
::p
Inside `aes()` = mapped to data (`fill = admission_method` gives a legend); outside = fixed (`geom_col(fill = "steelblue")` paints everything). Confusing those two is the classic first-week ggplot error. Save any plot with `ggsave("outputs/specialty.png", width = 8, height = 5, dpi = 300)` — real sizes for real reports.
::quiz
Q geom_col(fill = "red") vs aes(fill = method): the difference is…
- No difference
* Outside aes = one fixed colour; inside aes = colour varies by data, with a legend
- aes is for bars only
- fill must always be in aes
! Mapping (data-driven, legended) vs setting (constant). If you "map" to a constant string inside aes you get a mysterious one-item legend — the tell-tale of mixing them up.
::quiz
Q You've summarised admissions per month into a tibble with columns month, n. Which geom draws the bars?
- geom_bar(), which will count rows
* geom_col(), which draws the n values you computed
- geom_point()
- geom_histogram()
! Pre-computed values -> geom_col. geom_bar on this data would count one row per month and draw all bars height 1.
::ex
Chart vaccinations by vaccine: count rows per vaccine, then draw horizontal bars ordered by count, filled a single fixed colour of your choice, and save it to outputs/vaccine_counts.png at 7x4 inches.
~~~r
vaccinations |>
  count(vaccine) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = n, y = fct_reorder(vaccine, n))) +
  geom_col(fill = "#005EB8") +
  labs(x = "Doses recorded", y = NULL)

ggsave("outputs/vaccine_counts.png", width = 7, height = 4, dpi = 300)
::key
- ggplot(data, aes(mappings)) + geom_*() — data, mappings, marks.
- geom_col for computed values (analyst default); horizontal bars for long labels; fct_reorder for order.
- Inside aes = varies with data; outside = fixed. ggsave with explicit sizes.
#LESSON 5|Time series and epi curves
::p
Public health lives on time: monthly activity, weekly cases, winter pressure. The pattern — summarise to one row per period (a real Date, via `floor_date`), then `geom_line()`:
::code|r|Monthly emergency admissions
monthly <- admissions |>
  filter(admission_method == "Emergency") |>
  mutate(month = floor_date(admission_date, "month")) |>
  count(month)

ggplot(monthly, aes(month, n)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 0.8) +
  geom_point(size = 1.5) +
  scale_x_date(date_breaks = "3 months", date_labels = "%b %Y") +
  labs(x = NULL, y = "Emergency admissions")
::p
Because `month` is a Date, `scale_x_date()` gives sensible axis labels (`%b %Y` → "Apr 2025"). Points on top of the line make individual months readable. **The zero-month rule from Lesson 32 applies here too** — R's spine idiom is `complete()`:
::code|r|Filling missing periods with true zeros
monthly_full <- monthly |>
  complete(month = seq(min(month), max(month), by = "month"),
           fill = list(n = 0))
::p
An **epi curve** — the outbreak's signature chart — is the same idea with bars per day/week of symptom onset: `count(onset_week) |> ggplot(aes(onset_week, n)) + geom_col(width = 6)`. Bars (not a line) because each period's count is a discrete tally, and gaps should look like gaps. Add a smoothed layer for noisy dailies with the moving average from Lesson 32's logic (`zoo::rollmean` or a 7-day summarise) rather than `geom_smooth()`'s statistical fit — surveillance wants the actual smoothed data, not a model's opinion. Finally: **start count axes at zero** (`expand_limits(y = 0)`); truncated axes exaggerate wiggles and will be (rightly) challenged in QA.
::quiz
Q For an outbreak's epidemic curve, bars beat a line because…
- Bars are prettier
* Each period's case count is a discrete tally, and missing periods should show as gaps, not be bridged
- Lines cannot show dates
- ggplot has no geom_line
! A line implies continuity and interpolates across gaps; an epi curve is a histogram in time.
::quiz
Q Your monthly trend line dips sharply in the final month. Before narrating a decline, check…
- The chart colours
* Whether the last period is incomplete (data lag) — the Lesson 39 cliff, now in chart form
- The legend position
- The y-axis label
! Partial final periods create fake drops. Standard practice: exclude or visually flag the incomplete period.
::ex
Draw a monthly line chart of Flu vaccinations: floor_date month, count, complete() missing months to zero, line + points, y-axis from zero, labelled "Flu vaccinations per month".
~~~r
flu_monthly <- vaccinations |>
  filter(vaccine == "Flu") |>
  mutate(month = floor_date(date_given, "month")) |>
  count(month) |>
  complete(month = seq(min(month), max(month), by = "month"),
           fill = list(n = 0))

ggplot(flu_monthly, aes(month, n)) +
  geom_line() +
  geom_point() +
  expand_limits(y = 0) +
  scale_x_date(date_breaks = "3 months", date_labels = "%b %y") +
  labs(x = NULL, y = "Doses", title = "Flu vaccinations per month")
::key
- floor_date -> count -> geom_line/geom_col is the trend pattern; scale_x_date for labels.
- complete() supplies true zeros; flag or drop incomplete final periods.
- Epi curves are bars; smooth with moving averages, not model fits; count axes start at zero.
#LESSON 5|Charts people can actually read: style and accessibility
::p
A chart in an NHS board paper or UKHSA report has one job: transmit a finding accurately to a busy, mixed-ability audience. House rules that survive contact with communications teams:

**Say the finding.** Titles state the message ("Emergency admissions rose 12% over winter"), not the dataset ("Admissions by month"). `labs()` carries title, subtitle (the how/what), caption (source + date), and axis names in plain English with units.

**Declutter.** `theme_minimal()` strips the grey; then remove what remains unearned — heavy gridlines, redundant legends (`show.legend = FALSE` when labels sit on the chart). Direct-label lines where possible; legends force eye-travel.

**Colour is information, and 1 in 12 men are colour-vision deficient.** Red-vs-green encodings fail exactly the audience RAG ratings target. Use colour-blind-safe palettes: `scale_fill_viridis_d()` ships with ggplot; the Okabe-Ito palette is the accessibility standard; and check anything important with `colorblindr` or a simulator.
::code|r|A board-ready make-over
monthly |>
  ggplot(aes(month, n)) +
  geom_line(colour = "#005EB8", linewidth = 0.9) +
  expand_limits(y = 0) +
  labs(
    title    = "Emergency admissions climbed through winter 2025/26",
    subtitle = "Monthly emergency admissions, Barsetshire residents",
    caption  = "Source: synthetic SUS-style extract | Produced 2026-04-02",
    x = NULL, y = "Admissions"
  ) +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 12) +
  theme(plot.title = element_text(face = "bold"))
::p
Consistency scales: define your theme + palette once (a function! Lesson 56) and apply everywhere — that's how UKHSA/NHS teams get their recognisable look, and packages like `NHSRtheme` exist to hand you NHS-identity colours. Finally, **alt text**: anywhere a chart is published digitally, one sentence describing the finding for screen-reader users (Quarto: `fig-alt`). Accessibility in UK public bodies isn't optional — public sector websites are legally required to meet accessibility standards.
::quiz
Q The strongest chart title for a board paper is…
- Figure 3
- Admissions data 2024-2026
* Emergency admissions rose 12% over winter, driven by respiratory illness
- Chart produced in R
! Titles carry the finding; subtitles carry the description. Busy readers may only read titles — make them work.
::quiz
Q Why avoid a plain red/green "worse/better" encoding?
- Red is alarming
* Red-green confusion is the most common colour-vision deficiency — the encoding fails ~8% of men
- Green prints badly
- ggplot lacks red
! Pair colour with another cue (shape, label, position) and choose CVD-safe palettes (viridis, Okabe-Ito).
::ex
Take your Lesson 59 flu chart and make it publication-ready: finding-led title, subtitle naming population and period, caption with source and date, theme_minimal, NHS blue line, y from zero. Write the full code.
~~~r
ggplot(flu_monthly, aes(month, n)) +
  geom_line(colour = "#005EB8", linewidth = 0.9) +
  geom_point(colour = "#005EB8", size = 1.4) +
  expand_limits(y = 0) +
  scale_x_date(date_breaks = "3 months", date_labels = "%b %y") +
  labs(
    title    = "Flu vaccination activity peaks each autumn",
    subtitle = "Monthly recorded flu doses, Barsetshire synthetic data",
    caption  = "Source: course playground extract | Produced 2026",
    x = NULL, y = "Doses"
  ) +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 12) +
  theme(plot.title = element_text(face = "bold"))
::key
- Titles state findings; subtitles describe; captions cite source and date.
- theme_minimal + restraint; direct labels over legends where possible.
- Colour-blind-safe palettes (viridis/Okabe-Ito), redundant cues, alt text: accessibility is a legal duty in UK public bodies.
#LESSON 5|Small multiples: facets
::p
"Show me that, but for every LA / practice / age band" — the request that defines organisational analytics. Cramming ten lines onto one chart makes spaghetti; **facets** repeat a small clear chart per group on shared axes:
::code|r|One panel per local authority
adm_geo <- admissions |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id") |>
  left_join(lsoa_imd, by = "lsoa_code") |>
  mutate(month = floor_date(admission_date, "month")) |>
  count(la_name, month)

ggplot(adm_geo, aes(month, n)) +
  geom_line(colour = "#0E7C86") +
  facet_wrap(~ la_name) +
  expand_limits(y = 0) +
  labs(x = NULL, y = "Admissions")
::p
`facet_wrap(~ group)` wraps panels into a grid (`ncol =` to control); `facet_grid(rows ~ cols)` crosses two dimensions (age band × sex — the demographer's grid). The decision that changes the message: **scales**.
- Shared axes (default) compare *sizes* — Barchester dwarfs Kingsbay.
- `scales = "free_y"` compares *shapes* — every panel's own trend fills its frame, but sizes are no longer comparable, and readers WILL misread it unless the subtitle says so.

Ordering panels is a factor job (`fct_reorder(la_name, n, sum)` puts busiest first); tiny-count panels wobble randomly (Lesson 23's small-groups caution goes visual — consider lumping or annotating). Facets are also the honest alternative to dual-axis charts: two measures, two panels, no scale trickery.
::quiz
Q facet_wrap(~ la_name, scales = "free_y") lets each panel set its own y-axis. The cost is…
- Slower rendering
* Panel heights are no longer comparable — readers can't judge relative size across areas
- Facets lose their titles
- Lines become bars
! Free scales trade comparability for per-panel detail. Fine for shape-of-trend questions; label it clearly.
::quiz
Q Sex (2 levels) by age band (6 levels) as a grid of panels calls for…
- facet_wrap(~ sex)
* facet_grid(sex ~ age_band)
- Twelve separate scripts
- A dual-axis chart
! facet_grid crosses two dimensions into rows x columns — 12 aligned panels from one line of code.
::ex
Facet monthly admissions by specialty (one panel each), shared y-axis, panels ordered by total volume (hint: mutate specialty = fct_reorder(specialty, n, .fun = sum, .desc = TRUE) before plotting).
~~~r
adm_spec <- admissions |>
  mutate(month = floor_date(admission_date, "month")) |>
  count(specialty, month) |>
  mutate(specialty = fct_reorder(specialty, n, .fun = sum, .desc = TRUE))

ggplot(adm_spec, aes(month, n)) +
  geom_line() +
  facet_wrap(~ specialty) +
  expand_limits(y = 0) +
  labs(x = NULL, y = "Admissions")
::key
- facet_wrap(~ g) for one grouping; facet_grid(a ~ b) for crossed panels.
- Shared axes compare size; free scales compare shape — and must be labelled.
- Order panels with factors; distrust and annotate tiny-count panels.
#LESSON 5|Distributions: histograms, boxplots and honest summaries
::p
Before summarising a numeric column, **look at its shape** — health data is riotously non-normal, and the shape decides which summary is honest. Length of stay (Lesson 36) is the canonical example:
::code|r|Seeing the LOS distribution
los <- admissions |>
  filter(!is.na(discharge_date)) |>
  mutate(los_days = as.numeric(discharge_date - admission_date))

ggplot(los, aes(los_days)) +
  geom_histogram(binwidth = 1, fill = "#0E7C86", colour = "white") +
  labs(x = "Length of stay (days)", y = "Spells")
::p
A tall spike at 0–2 days and a long right tail: **right-skewed**. Consequences: the mean sits above the median (dragged by the tail); "average stay: 4.1 days" describes almost nobody; the tail *is* the capacity story. `binwidth` is an analytical choice — try 1 and 7; different bins, different visible stories.

**Boxplots compare distributions across groups** — median line, interquartile box, whiskers, outlier dots:
::code|r|LOS by specialty at a glance
ggplot(los, aes(x = los_days, y = fct_reorder(specialty, los_days, median))) +
  geom_boxplot(fill = "#E4F0F1") +
  labs(x = "Length of stay (days)", y = NULL)
::p
Ordering by median makes the comparison instant. For small groups where a box over-summarises, show the actual points (`geom_jitter(alpha = .3)`) or a violin. Reporting rule that follows: **skewed measures get median + IQR** (and percentiles like the 95th for tails); reserve mean ± SD for genuinely symmetric things. When a director asks why your "average" differs from someone's dashboard, the answer is usually mean-vs-median on a skewed measure — now you can draw the picture that explains it.
::quiz
Q For right-skewed LOS data, the mean is typically…
- Below the median
- Equal to the median
* Above the median, pulled by the long tail of long stays
- Undefined
! Tail values drag the mean upward. Median = the typical patient; mean = accounting for total bed-days. Different questions, different statistics.
::quiz
Q A boxplot's box spans…
- Minimum to maximum
* The interquartile range: 25th to 75th percentile, with the median line inside
- Mean ± SD
- The 95% confidence interval
! The middle half of the data. Whiskers extend further; dots beyond are flagged as outliers worth a look.
::ex
Explore Systolic BP values: histogram with binwidth 5; then boxplots of value by patient sex (join to patients), ordered by median. In one sentence: is mean or median the better headline summary here, and why?
~~~r
sbp <- lab_results |>
  filter(test == "Systolic BP") |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id")

ggplot(sbp, aes(value)) +
  geom_histogram(binwidth = 5, fill = "#0E7C86", colour = "white")

ggplot(sbp, aes(value, sex)) +
  geom_boxplot()

# BP is roughly symmetric with mild right tail: mean and median will be close,
# so either is defensible - but check the picture first; that's the lesson.
::key
- Histogram first: shape decides the summary. Skew is the norm in health data.
- Skewed -> median + IQR/percentiles; symmetric -> mean ± SD.
- Boxplots (ordered by median) compare groups; jitter/violin when boxes hide too much.
#LESSON 5|Maps: small-area geography with sf
::p
Public health findings are spatial — deprivation clusters, uptake gaps, outbreak spread — and the UK has superb open geography. The R standard is **sf** (simple features): boundary files become data frames with a geometry column, so every dplyr verb still works, and `geom_sf()` draws them.

The workflow: get boundaries from the **ONS Open Geography Portal** (geoportal.statistics.gov.uk — LSOA, MSOA, LA, ICB boundaries, open licence); join your indicator by area code; map:
::code|r|The choropleth pattern
library(sf)

# 1. Boundaries (downloaded GeoJSON/shapefile from ONS Geoportal)
lsoa_shapes <- st_read("data/lsoa_boundaries.geojson")

# 2. Your indicator by LSOA code (e.g. emergency admission rate)
rates <- admissions |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id") |>
  count(lsoa_code, name = "admissions")

# 3. Join and map
lsoa_shapes |>
  left_join(rates, by = c("LSOA21CD" = "lsoa_code")) |>
  ggplot() +
  geom_sf(aes(fill = admissions), colour = NA) +
  scale_fill_viridis_c(na.value = "grey90") +
  theme_void() +
  labs(fill = "Admissions")
::p
(Your playground's synthetic LSOAs have no real boundaries, so this lesson is pattern-study; the code runs verbatim on real ONS files.) Craft notes: **join direction matters** — shapes on the left keeps every area, so data gaps show as grey rather than holes; **viridis** for continuous fills (CVD-safe, prints well); **counts mislead on maps** even more than usual, because area population varies — map *rates*, or use quantile bins (`cut_number(rate, 5)`) so one extreme area doesn't flatten the palette; **theme_void()** drops axes (lat/long ticks help nobody). Interactive maps for exploration: `leaflet` or `tmap` — but static + clear beats interactive + cluttered in most reports.
::quiz
Q For a fair map of emergency admissions across LSOAs, you should map…
- Raw counts, coloured red
* Rates (or quantile-binned rates), since LSOA populations differ
- Alphabetical area codes
- Only the largest LSOA
! Counts track population size; rates reveal the pattern you actually care about. (Age-standardised rates - Lesson 70 - are fairer still.)
::quiz
Q shapes |> left_join(data) rather than data |> left_join(shapes) is preferred because…
- It's faster
* Every boundary is kept, so areas with missing data appear grey instead of vanishing from the map
- Joins only work that way
- It sorts the legend
! A map with silent holes misleads; grey "no data" areas tell the truth.
::ex
No live boundaries here, so write the code you WOULD run: map crude admission rates per 1,000 by LA using an ONS LA boundary file (lad_boundaries.geojson, key LAD24NM matching la_name), quantile-binned into 5 groups with viridis.
~~~r
library(sf)

la_rates <- admissions |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id") |>
  left_join(lsoa_imd,  by = "lsoa_code") |>
  count(la_name, name = "admissions") |>
  left_join(la_population |> summarise(pop = sum(population), .by = la_name),
            by = "la_name") |>
  mutate(rate = 1000 * admissions / pop,
         rate_band = cut_number(rate, 5))

st_read("data/lad_boundaries.geojson") |>
  left_join(la_rates, by = c("LAD24NM" = "la_name")) |>
  ggplot() +
  geom_sf(aes(fill = rate_band), colour = "white", linewidth = 0.2) +
  scale_fill_viridis_d(na.value = "grey90") +
  theme_void() +
  labs(fill = "Admissions per 1,000")
::key
- sf makes boundaries data frames; ONS Open Geography Portal supplies them freely.
- Shapes-left joins keep every area; map rates (often binned), never bare counts.
- viridis fills, theme_void, grey for no-data: the credible-choropleth kit.
#LESSON 5|Tables for reports: gt and kable
::p
Half your outputs will be tables, and a raw data-frame printout is not a deliverable. Two tools cover the territory: `knitr::kable()` for quick clean tables inside reports, and **gt** for publication-grade ones.
::code|r|From summary to formatted table
library(gt)

summary_tbl <- admissions |>
  mutate(los = as.numeric(discharge_date - admission_date)) |>
  summarise(
    Spells     = n(),
    Patients   = n_distinct(patient_id),
    `Mean LOS` = mean(los, na.rm = TRUE),
    .by = specialty
  ) |>
  arrange(desc(Spells))

summary_tbl |>
  gt() |>
  tab_header(
    title    = "Inpatient activity by specialty",
    subtitle = "Barsetshire synthetic data, FY 2024/25-2025/26"
  ) |>
  fmt_number(columns = `Mean LOS`, decimals = 1) |>
  fmt_number(columns = c(Spells, Patients), decimals = 0, sep_mark = ",") |>
  tab_source_note("Source: course playground extract")
::p
The pipeline mirrors ggplot's philosophy: data first, then layered presentation — headers, number formats (thousands separators! decimals!), source notes. More gt verbs as needed: `cols_label()` to rename, `sub_missing()` to print em-dashes for NA, `tab_style()` to bold a total row, `gtsave("outputs/table1.png")` (or .docx/.html) to export.

Table craft, independent of tool: right-align numbers, consistent decimals per column (rates to 1 dp, counts to 0), order rows meaningfully (by size or an agreed sequence, never accidental), keep to the columns the reader needs, and put the units in the header not every cell. And the health-specific one: **this is where suppression happens** — your Lesson 56 `suppress_small()` runs on the data *before* it reaches gt, so the pretty table is also a safe table (Lesson 67 completes that story).
::quiz
Q fmt_number(columns = Spells, sep_mark = ",", decimals = 0) exists because…
- gt requires it
* 12345 reads as 12,345 — formatting is part of accurate communication
- Numbers must be text in tables
- It sorts the column
! Thousands separators, sensible decimals and alignment are the difference between a printout and a professional table.
::quiz
Q Small-number suppression belongs…
- In gt's styling functions
* In the data step before the table is built (e.g. a suppress_small mutate)
- In the reader's judgement
- Nowhere in tables
! Suppress in data, then format: the safe value flows to every output. Styling can't be trusted to hide what the data still contains.
::ex
Build a gt table of vaccinations: doses and distinct children by vaccine and dose_number, with a header, comma-separated counts, and a source note. (Optional: gtsave it.)
~~~r
vaccinations |>
  summarise(Doses = n(),
            Children = n_distinct(patient_id),
            .by = c(vaccine, dose_number)) |>
  arrange(vaccine, dose_number) |>
  gt() |>
  tab_header(title = "Recorded vaccinations by product and dose") |>
  fmt_number(columns = c(Doses, Children), decimals = 0, sep_mark = ",") |>
  tab_source_note("Source: synthetic course data")
::key
- kable for quick report tables; gt for polished ones — data, then layered formatting.
- Right-aligned numbers, fixed decimals, separators, ordered rows, units in headers.
- Suppression is a data step before formatting — safe first, pretty second.
#LESSON 5|Quarto: reports that rebuild themselves
::p
The reproducibility endgame: a document where narrative, code, results and charts live together, and one command regenerates everything when data updates. That's **Quarto** (`.qmd` — successor to R Markdown `.Rmd`; both ship with RStudio, and you'll meet plenty of .Rmd in the wild).

A `.qmd` has three ingredients: a **YAML header** (metadata + output format), **markdown prose**, and **code chunks**:
::code|text|report.qmd - a minimal skeleton
---
title: "Monthly activity report"
author: "Analytics team"
date: 2026-04-02
format: html
execute:
  echo: false        # readers see results, not code
  warning: false
---

## Emergency admissions

Admissions data refreshed to end of March.

```{r}
#| label: setup
library(tidyverse)
admissions <- read_csv("data/admissions.csv")
```

```{r}
#| label: fig-trend
#| fig-cap: "Monthly emergency admissions"
#| fig-alt: "Line chart showing emergency admissions rising through winter"
admissions |>
  filter(admission_method == "Emergency") |>
  count(month = floor_date(admission_date, "month")) |>
  ggplot(aes(month, n)) + geom_line() + expand_limits(y = 0)
```

Total admissions this period: `r nrow(admissions)`.
::p
Press **Render** and Quarto runs every chunk top-to-bottom **in a fresh session** and produces the output — `format: html` for rich shareable pages, `docx` for the Word documents NHS workflows still expect, `pdf`, or `revealjs` slides. The fresh-session rule is a feature: if it renders, it's reproducible; hidden console state can't sneak in.

The inline `` `r nrow(admissions)` `` is the quiet superpower — numbers in your prose that update with the data, ending the "text says 412, table says 418" class of error forever. Chunk options (`#|` lines) control each block: `echo` (show code?), `fig-cap`, `fig-alt` (Lesson 60's accessibility), `label` for cross-references. Next month's report = drop in new data, Render, proof-read. Minutes, not days.
::quiz
Q Quarto renders in a fresh R session so that…
- Rendering is faster
* The document depends only on its own code and files — guaranteeing reproducibility
- Plots look sharper
- YAML is optional
! Anything relying on leftover console objects fails at render — surfacing irreproducibility immediately instead of on a colleague's machine.
::quiz
Q Inline code like `r round(rate, 1)` in your prose means…
- The reader sees the code
* The computed value is printed in the sentence and updates whenever the report re-renders
- An error in Word output
- A footnote
! Narrative numbers bound to data: the end of copy-paste drift between text and tables.
::ex
Create monthly_report.qmd: YAML with title and format html + echo: false; a setup chunk loading tidyverse and one playground CSV; one finding-titled chart chunk with fig-cap and fig-alt; and a sentence using inline code for the row count. Render it.
~~~text
Success looks like: an HTML file opens with your chart, no visible code,
a caption, and a sentence such as "This report covers 900 admissions."
where 900 was computed, not typed. If render fails, read the error's
chunk label - it names exactly where to look.
::key
- Quarto = YAML + prose + chunks; Render runs clean and outputs html/docx/pdf.
- echo: false for readers; fig-cap/fig-alt for accessibility; labels for cross-refs.
- Inline `r` values keep narrative numbers honest — the update-proof report.
#LESSON 5|Parameterised reports: one template, forty outputs
::p
The request that breaks copy-paste workflows: "lovely report — now one per LA / practice / ICB, monthly." **Parameters** solve it: the report declares inputs in YAML, code reads them, and a loop renders every variant from one template.

**1. Declare parameters and use them:**
::code|text|In la_report.qmd
---
title: "Public health summary"
format: html
params:
  la: "Barchester"
---

```{r}
la_data <- admissions_geo |>
  filter(la_name == params$la)
```

## `r params$la`
This report covers `r nrow(la_data)` admissions for `r params$la` residents.
::p
**2. Render every variant from a script:**
::code|r|render_all.R
library(quarto)

las <- c("Barchester", "Millford", "Kingsbay")

for (la in las) {                       # purrr::walk works too (Lesson 57!)
  quarto_render(
    "la_report.qmd",
    execute_params = list(la = la),
    output_file    = paste0("outputs/report_", janitor::make_clean_names(la), ".html")
  )
}
::p
Three files, three tailored reports, identical methodology — and when the definition of an indicator changes, you fix **one template** and re-render, instead of editing forty documents inconsistently. Design habits that keep templates healthy: make *everything* flow from params (titles, filters, filenames); test with the smallest and the messiest unit (the tiny LA with suppressible counts is where templates break); include a rendered-date and data-version line in the footer; and keep a `combined` param path (`la: "All"`) for the overview report. This pattern — template + parameter loop — is the beating heart of RAP-grade reporting in Module 7.
::quiz
Q A methodology change must reach all 42 ICB reports. With parameterised reporting you…
- Edit 42 files carefully
* Edit the single template and re-render the batch
- Ask each ICB to edit theirs
- Rewrite in Excel
! One source of truth, many outputs: consistency by construction, not diligence.
::quiz
Q Why test the template on the smallest area first?
- Small areas render faster
* Edge cases — zero counts, suppression, empty groups — live there and break naive templates
- Big areas are unimportant
- Quarto requires it
! Templates fail at the margins. The area with 3 events finds your bugs before the director does.
::ex
Sketch the two artefacts for practice-level reports: the params block + first filter chunk of practice_report.qmd, and the render loop over three practice codes writing to outputs/.
~~~r
# --- practice_report.qmd (top) ---
# ---
# title: "Practice profile"
# format: html
# params:
#   practice: "P81001"
# ---
# ```{r}
# pr_data <- admissions_full |> filter(practice_code == params$practice)
# ```

# --- render_all.R ---
library(quarto)
for (p in c("P81001", "P81002", "P81003")) {
  quarto_render("practice_report.qmd",
                execute_params = list(practice = p),
                output_file = paste0("outputs/profile_", p, ".html"))
}
::key
- params in YAML + params$x in code = a report template; a loop renders the batch.
- Fix once, re-render everywhere; date-stamp outputs.
- Test on the smallest, messiest unit — that's where suppression and empty-group bugs hide.
#LESSON 5|Safe outputs: disclosure control before anything leaves
::p
Module 1 gave the law and principles; now the operational habit. **Everything that leaves your team — tables, charts, CSVs, the numbers inside sentences — passes disclosure control first.** In R, that means suppression is a *pipeline step*, not a manual edit.
::code|r|A reusable suppression step
suppress_counts <- function(df, count_cols, threshold = 5) {
  df |>
    mutate(across(
      all_of(count_cols),
      \(n) if_else(n > 0 & n < threshold, "<5", format(n, big.mark = ","))
    ))
}

ward_deaths |>
  suppress_counts("deaths") |>
  gt()
::p
The full checklist, run in order:
- **Primary suppression:** hide small non-zero counts (threshold per your organisation's policy — 5 is common; HES-derived outputs traditionally suppress 1–7). Zeros are usually safe to show; check local rules.
- **Secondary suppression:** if a row/column total lets a reader back-calculate one hidden cell, hide a second cell (or round/remove the total). One suppressed cell alongside an exact total is no suppression at all.
- **Derived values leak too:** a rate of 2.1 per 1,000 on a published denominator of 950 reveals the count of 2. Suppress the rate when its numerator is suppressed; consider rounding denominators.
- **Charts count as outputs:** a bar of height 2 discloses exactly like a table cell; aggregate small categories (fct_lump!) or annotate "<5".
- **Row-level exports are the sharpest edge:** a "harmless" CSV of rare-condition events with LSOA + age + sex can single people out by combination. Aggregate before export; if record-level must move, it goes through IG-approved channels, never email.

Codify it: your team's thresholds live in one sourced function; every report template calls it; QA checks it. When genuinely unsure whether something identifies, the answer is your IG team or Caldicott Guardian *before* release — asking first is a strength signal in this profession, never a weakness.
::quiz
Q A table shows Ward A: <5, Ward B: 12, Total: 15. The problem is…
- Nothing; primary suppression applied
* The total back-calculates Ward A as 3 — secondary suppression (or total removal/rounding) is needed
- Totals are never allowed
- Ward B should also be hidden
! Complementary disclosure: exact margins + one hidden cell = arithmetic, not protection.
::quiz
Q Publishing "rate: 4.2 per 1,000 (population 480)" for a rare event risks…
- Nothing; rates are aggregate
* Revealing the numerator (2) — derived statistics inherit their inputs' disclosure risk
- Confusing readers with decimals
- Breaching copyright
! 4.2/1000 x 480 ≈ 2. Suppress or coarsen derived values whose suppressed ingredients they expose.
::ex
Extend suppress_counts to also blank an accompanying rate column when its count is suppressed (arguments: count_col, rate_col). Show it on a toy tibble of area, deaths, rate.
~~~r
suppress_with_rate <- function(df, count_col, rate_col, threshold = 5) {
  df |>
    mutate(
      hide = .data[[count_col]] > 0 & .data[[count_col]] < threshold,
      "{rate_col}"  := if_else(hide, "*", format(round(.data[[rate_col]], 1))),
      "{count_col}" := if_else(hide, "<5", format(.data[[count_col]])),
    ) |>
    select(-hide)
}

tibble(area = c("A","B","C"), deaths = c(2, 14, 0), rate = c(4.2, 9.8, 0)) |>
  suppress_with_rate("deaths", "rate")
#> A: <5 with rate *, B: 14 / 9.8, C: 0 / 0
::key
- Suppression is a coded pipeline step applied to every leaving output — tables, charts, CSVs, sentences.
- Primary, then secondary (totals!), then derived values (rates leak numerators).
- Record-level exports need IG-approved routes; when unsure, ask before release.
#LESSON 6|Describing data honestly: the right summary for the shape
::p
Statistics starts with description, and description starts with **shape** (Lesson 62). The decision tree an analyst runs on every numeric variable:
- Roughly symmetric? → **mean ± standard deviation (SD)**.
- Skewed / outlier-prone (LOS, waiting times, costs — most health data)? → **median + IQR**, plus percentiles for tails (90th/95th).
- Categorical? → **counts + percentages**, with the denominator stated.
::code|r|A describe-everything block
los_summary <- los |>
  summarise(
    n      = n(),
    mean   = mean(los_days),
    sd     = sd(los_days),
    median = median(los_days),
    q25    = quantile(los_days, 0.25),
    q75    = quantile(los_days, 0.75),
    p95    = quantile(los_days, 0.95),
    max    = max(los_days)
  )
::p
Interpretation beats computation. **SD** describes spread of *individuals* (mean 4.1, SD 6.2 says individuals vary hugely — and SD > mean on a non-negative measure screams skew). Percentages need their denominator in the same breath: "62% (of the 34 patients tested)" and "62% (of 12,000 admissions)" are different classes of claim. Small denominators make wild percentages — one event in a practice of 3 eligible children is "33% uptake"; flag or suppress tiny denominators rather than headline them.

Two more honesty rules. **Report missingness alongside every summary** — "median HbA1c 49 (n = 412; 38 missing)" — because who's missing is often the story. And **round for the reader**: rates to 1 dp, percentages to whole numbers usually; spurious precision ("uptake was 62.3847%") signals a beginner.
::quiz
Q Mean LOS 4.1 days with SD 6.2 days tells you…
- Data entry errors
* Massive individual variation and (given LOS can't be negative) right skew — median needed
- Nothing useful
- The mean is wrong
! SD near or above the mean of a non-negative variable is a skew signature. The picture (histogram) and the median tell the honest story.
::quiz
Q "Screening uptake: 100%" is least trustworthy when…
- The programme is national
* The denominator is tiny (e.g. 2 of 2 eligible) — small denominators produce unstable, headline-grabbing percentages
- It comes from SQL
- Uptake is measured monthly
! Always ask "percent of how many?" Percentages inherit the fragility of their denominators.
::ex
Write a reusable describe_num(df, col) function returning n, n_missing, mean, sd, median, q25, q75 for any numeric column (hint: use {{ col }} for tidy evaluation). Run it on lab_results value for HbA1c only.
~~~r
describe_num <- function(df, col) {
  df |>
    summarise(
      n         = sum(!is.na({{ col }})),
      n_missing = sum(is.na({{ col }})),
      mean      = mean({{ col }}, na.rm = TRUE),
      sd        = sd({{ col }}, na.rm = TRUE),
      median    = median({{ col }}, na.rm = TRUE),
      q25       = quantile({{ col }}, 0.25, na.rm = TRUE),
      q75       = quantile({{ col }}, 0.75, na.rm = TRUE)
    )
}

lab_results |>
  filter(test == "HbA1c") |>
  describe_num(value)
::key
- Shape chooses the summary: symmetric → mean/SD; skewed → median/IQR/percentiles; categorical → n and % with denominator.
- Report missingness with every summary; distrust percentages on tiny denominators.
- Round for humans; precision beyond the data is noise.
#LESSON 6|Rates in R: crude rates, person-time and per-100,000 thinking
::p
Lesson 37 built rates in SQL; R is where they meet inference. Rebuild the pattern tidily — numerators and denominators as data frames, joined, scaled:
::code|r|Crude emergency admission rates by LA
numerator <- admissions |>
  filter(admission_method == "Emergency") |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id") |>
  left_join(lsoa_imd,  by = "lsoa_code") |>
  count(la_name, name = "events")

denominator <- la_population |>
  summarise(pop = sum(population), .by = la_name)

rates <- numerator |>
  left_join(denominator, by = "la_name") |>
  mutate(rate_per_100k = 100000 * events / pop)
::p
Concepts that stop rate mistakes:
- **Choose the scale for the audience**: per 1,000 for common events (GP consultations), per 100,000 for rarer ones (mortality, incidence) — pick whatever makes numbers land between ~1 and a few hundred, and say the scale everywhere.
- **Person-time**: when people enter and leave observation (registrations churn, cohorts age in and out), the honest denominator is **person-years at risk**, not a headcount — 500 people followed for 6 months = 250 person-years. Rate = events / person-years. Mid-year population estimates are the standard approximation for whole-area annual rates.
- **Events vs people**: 300 admissions from 190 patients gives you two different rates — an *admission* rate and a *patient* rate. Decide which the question wants; say which you used.
- **Period matching** (Lesson 37's rule): a 2-year numerator needs 2 years of person-time.
::quiz
Q 500 residents observed for 6 months each contribute how much person-time?
- 500 person-years
* 250 person-years
- 3,000 person-years
- 6 person-years
! 500 x 0.5 years. Rates divide events by time genuinely at risk — headcounts overstate the denominator when follow-up is partial.
::quiz
Q A town has 84 emergency COPD admissions from 51 patients in a year. "The COPD admission rate" should…
- Use 84; patients don't matter
- Use 51; admissions don't matter
* Be stated explicitly as either admissions-based (84) or patient-based (51) — they answer different questions
- Average the two
! Activity questions (bed pressure) want events; population-health questions (how many people affected) want people. Ambiguity here causes real report disputes.
::ex
Compute both rates for each LA: emergency admissions per 100,000 AND emergency patients (distinct) per 100,000, in one summarise (n() and n_distinct), joined to population. Which LA's two rates differ most (in ratio terms)?
~~~r
per_la <- admissions |>
  filter(admission_method == "Emergency") |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id") |>
  left_join(lsoa_imd,  by = "lsoa_code") |>
  summarise(events   = n(),
            patients = n_distinct(patient_id),
            .by = la_name) |>
  left_join(denominator, by = "la_name") |>
  mutate(event_rate   = 100000 * events   / pop,
         patient_rate = 100000 * patients / pop,
         ratio        = event_rate / patient_rate) |>
  arrange(desc(ratio))
per_la
# The highest ratio = most repeat admissions per patient - itself a finding.
::key
- Rate = events / population-or-person-time x scale; choose scale for readability and state it.
- Person-years for churning cohorts; mid-year estimates for annual area rates.
- Decide events vs people explicitly — the ratio between them is the readmission story.
#LESSON 6|Age-standardisation: comparing places fairly
::p
Crude rates confound age (Lesson 37's warning). **Direct age-standardisation** removes it: compute age-specific rates for each area, then apply every area's rates to the *same* reference population — the **European Standard Population 2013 (ESP2013)** in UK practice. The result, a **directly standardised rate (DSR)**, answers: *what would this area's rate be if it had the standard age structure?*
::code|r|DSR by hand (the logic laid bare)
esp <- tribble(          # simplified 3-band ESP-style standard
  ~age_band, ~std_pop,
  "0-39",     45000,
  "40-64",    33000,
  "65+",      22000
)

dsr <- age_specific |>            # columns: la_name, age_band, events, pop
  mutate(rate = events / pop) |>
  left_join(esp, by = "age_band") |>
  summarise(
    dsr_per_100k = 100000 * sum(rate * std_pop) / sum(std_pop),
    .by = la_name
  )
::p
Read the core line: each band's *local rate* × the *standard* band size, summed, divided by the standard total — a weighted average with everyone forced to share weights. In production you don't hand-roll it: the **PHEindicatormethods** package (built for exactly this by UK public health statisticians) does DSRs with confidence intervals in one call — `phe_dsr(data, x = events, n = pop, stdpop = std_pop)` — using the full 5-year ESP2013 bands.

Judgement notes: DSRs are for **comparison**, not for stating the real burden (the crude rate is what actually happens to actual residents — report both); areas with tiny band counts give unstable DSRs (CIs, next lesson, are mandatory); and standardisation only removes *age* (and sex if you standardise by it) — deprivation and everything else still differ. **Indirect standardisation** (SMRs — expected deaths from standard rates applied to local structure) is the sibling used when local band counts are too small; recognise the term.
::quiz
Q A DSR answers which question?
- What is the actual admission rate here?
* What would the rate be if this area had the standard (ESP2013) age structure?
- How many events occurred?
- What is the median age?
! It's a fair-comparison construct. For "what is actually happening to residents", the crude rate remains the truthful number — report both.
::quiz
Q Area A (young) and Area B (old) have equal crude mortality rates. Their DSRs will likely show…
- Equality
* Area A worse — matching an old area's crude rate despite a young population implies higher age-specific rates
- Area B worse
- Nothing; DSRs can't compare
! Standardisation reveals what age structure concealed: a young population "should" have a lower crude rate; parity means A's underlying rates are higher.
::ex
Using the 3-band ESP above and made-up age_specific data for two LAs (you choose plausible events/pop per band, making one LA older), compute crude rates and DSRs, and show a case where the crude ranking and DSR ranking flip.
~~~r
age_specific <- tribble(
  ~la_name,   ~age_band, ~events,  ~pop,
  "Oldshire",  "0-39",       10,  8000,
  "Oldshire",  "40-64",      40, 12000,
  "Oldshire",  "65+",       150, 20000,
  "Youngton",  "0-39",       30, 25000,
  "Youngton",  "40-64",      35, 10000,
  "Youngton",  "65+",        40,  5000
)

crude <- age_specific |>
  summarise(crude = 100000 * sum(events) / sum(pop), .by = la_name)

dsr <- age_specific |>
  mutate(rate = events / pop) |>
  left_join(esp, by = "age_band") |>
  summarise(dsr = 100000 * sum(rate * std_pop) / sum(std_pop), .by = la_name)

left_join(crude, dsr, by = "la_name")
#> Oldshire has the higher crude rate (old population),
#> but Youngton's age-specific rates are worse - its DSR comes out higher.
::key
- Direct standardisation: local age-specific rates x ESP2013 weights = comparable DSRs.
- Use PHEindicatormethods::phe_dsr in real work (CIs included); report crude alongside.
- Standardisation removes age only; small bands need CIs; SMR = the indirect sibling.
#LESSON 6|Uncertainty: confidence intervals for counts and rates
::p
Sixteen deaths this year, twelve last year — real change or chance? Counts of events wobble naturally (statisticians model them as **Poisson**), and small counts wobble *proportionally more*. A **95% confidence interval** expresses that wobble: the range of underlying rates reasonably compatible with what you observed.

The UK public health workhorse is **Byar's method** — an excellent Poisson approximation you can compute directly, and the one PHEindicatormethods implements:
::code|r|Byar's CI for a count, then a rate
byars_ci <- function(x, conf = 0.95) {          # x = observed count
  z <- qnorm(1 - (1 - conf) / 2)
  lower <- x * (1 - 1/(9*x) - z / (3 * sqrt(x)))^3
  upper <- (x + 1) * (1 - 1/(9*(x+1)) + z / (3 * sqrt(x + 1)))^3
  tibble(lower, upper)
}

byars_ci(16)          # observed 16 deaths
#> lower ~9.1, upper ~26.0

# As a rate per 100,000 on population 40,000:
100000 * c(9.1, 16, 26.0) / 40000
#> ~22.9  40.0  64.9  per 100,000
::p
Sixteen deaths is compatible with an underlying count anywhere from ~9 to ~26 — so twelve-vs-sixteen is comfortably within chance. Reading rules that keep you honest:
- **Overlapping CIs → don't claim a difference** (non-overlap suggests one; the strict test comes later, but this heuristic prevents most over-claiming).
- **Small counts, huge intervals**: 3 events → CI roughly 0.6–8.8. This is the statistical twin of suppression: small numbers can't support strong claims.
- CIs quantify **random variation only** — no interval fixes a biased numerator or wrong denominator.
- In production: `PHEindicatormethods::phe_rate()` and `phe_dsr()` return rates with Byar's-based CIs ready for publication; error bars in ggplot via `geom_errorbar(aes(ymin = lower, ymax = upper))`.
::quiz
Q Ward A rate 58 (95% CI 40-82); Ward B rate 71 (95% CI 52-95), per 100,000. The defensible statement is…
- B is significantly worse
* The intervals overlap substantially — the data are consistent with no real difference
- A is improving
- Both are zero
! Wide, overlapping intervals = chance can explain the gap. Claiming otherwise is the commonest statistical error in local reports.
::quiz
Q Why do 3 observed events produce an enormous relative CI?
- Byar's formula is inaccurate
* Poisson variation is proportionally huge at small counts — the data barely constrain the underlying rate
- Rates cannot be computed under 10 events
- The population must be wrong
! Small-count instability is fundamental, not fixable by formula choice — which is why small numbers get suppressed and never headlined.
::ex
Compute Byar's 95% CIs for observed counts 3, 16 and 160 with the function above, express each as (count ± interval) and note how the RELATIVE width (upper/lower ratio) shrinks as counts grow.
~~~r
map_dfr(c(3, 16, 160), \(x) byars_ci(x) |> mutate(x = x, .before = 1)) |>
  mutate(rel_width = upper / lower)
#> x=3:   ~0.6-8.8   (ratio ~14 - almost no information)
#> x=16:  ~9.1-26    (ratio ~2.9)
#> x=160: ~136-187   (ratio ~1.4 - now quite precise)
::key
- Event counts are Poisson-wobbly; Byar's method gives the standard UK CI for counts, rates and DSRs.
- Overlapping CIs = no claimed difference; small counts = wide intervals = weak claims.
- CIs cover chance only — bias needs fixing at source. Use PHEindicatormethods in production.
#LESSON 6|Comparing proportions: 2x2 tables, chi-square and risk ratios
::p
"Is uptake lower in deprived areas?" is a two-proportion question, and its home is the **2×2 table**: exposure (deprived: yes/no) by outcome (vaccinated: yes/no).
::code|r|Build the 2x2 and the measures
tab <- imms |>                       # child-level: imd_group, vaccinated
  count(imd_group, vaccinated) |>
  pivot_wider(names_from = vaccinated, values_from = n)   # yes / no columns

# Proportions with CIs, per group:
imms |>
  summarise(n = n(), vacc = sum(vaccinated), .by = imd_group) |>
  mutate(p = vacc / n,
         se = sqrt(p * (1 - p) / n),
         lower = p - 1.96 * se,
         upper = p + 1.96 * se)
::p
Two complementary tools:
- **The risk ratio (RR)** — the *size* of the difference: p₁/p₂. "Uptake 84% vs 92%: RR 0.91 — deprived-area children are 9% less likely to be vaccinated." Effect sizes carry the public health meaning; a risk *difference* (92% − 84% = 8 percentage points) translates directly into children missed.
- **The chi-square test** — the *chance* question: could a gap this size arise if uptake were truly equal? `chisq.test(table(imms$imd_group, imms$vaccinated))` returns a p-value: the probability of a gap at least this large under "no real difference". Small p (< 0.05 by convention) → chance is an unlikely explanation. For small cell counts (expected < 5), use `fisher.test()` instead.

The professional framing: **report the effect size with its CI, use the test as support, never headline a bare p-value**. "p = 0.03" says almost nothing; "8 percentage points lower (95% CI 3–13), p = 0.03" says everything. And with large denominators, trivially small gaps become "significant" — statistical significance ≠ public-health importance; 0.2 percentage points can be p < 0.05 and still not worth a meeting.
::quiz
Q Uptake gap of 0.3 percentage points, p = 0.001, n = 400,000 per group. Best interpretation?
- A major inequality demanding action
* A real but tiny difference — huge samples make trivial gaps "significant"; judge importance by effect size
- The test failed
- Uptake is equal
! p-values measure detectability, not importance. With n that large, almost any true difference is detectable.
::quiz
Q A 2x2 has an expected cell count of 2. You should…
- Use chisq.test anyway
* Use fisher.test, designed for small counts
- Double the data
- Skip the analysis
! Chi-square's approximation breaks with sparse cells; Fisher's exact test is the standard fallback (and R will warn you).
::ex
From the playground, export a child-level MMR table (or construct a toy one): 480 deprived children with 396 vaccinated; 510 non-deprived with 466. Compute both proportions, the risk difference, the RR, and run chisq.test on the 2x2 counts.
~~~r
tab <- matrix(c(396, 480-396,
                466, 510-466),
              nrow = 2, byrow = TRUE,
              dimnames = list(c("Deprived","Not deprived"),
                              c("Vaccinated","Not")))
prop1 <- 396/480; prop2 <- 466/510
prop1; prop2
#> 0.825, 0.914
prop2 - prop1          # risk difference ~0.089 (8.9 points)
prop1 / prop2          # RR ~0.90
chisq.test(tab)
#> p-value well under 0.05 - chance is an unlikely explanation
::key
- 2x2 tables → risk difference and risk ratio (the meaning) + chi-square/Fisher (the chance check).
- Report effect size with CI; p-values support, never headline.
- Large n makes trivia "significant"; small expected cells demand fisher.test.
#LESSON 6|Comparing means: t-tests, Mann-Whitney and what "significant" means
::p
"Is mean HbA1c different between two groups?" — the two-group numeric comparison. Two standard tools, chosen (again) by shape:
- **Welch's t-test** — compares means; R's default `t.test(value ~ group, data = d)` (unequal variances assumed — the sensible default). Wants roughly symmetric data or decent sample sizes.
- **Mann-Whitney / Wilcoxon rank-sum** — `wilcox.test(value ~ group)` — compares distributions via ranks; the go-to for skewed measures like LOS, where means mislead anyway.
::code|r|Both tests on lab data
hba <- lab_results |>
  filter(test == "HbA1c") |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id")

t.test(value ~ sex, data = hba)
#> t = ..., p-value = ...
#> 95 percent CI of the difference: [lo, hi]
#> sample means: F ..., M ...

wilcox.test(value ~ sex, data = hba)
::p
Read the t-test output in this order: **the two means** (the finding), **the CI of the difference** (plausible size range — crossing 0 means "no difference" is compatible), then the p-value (support). A p-value's actual meaning — worth memorising because it's an interview classic — is: *the probability of a difference at least this large if the groups were truly identical*. It is **not** the probability the finding is true, and 0.05 is a convention, not physics.

Practical wisdom: paired designs (same patients before/after) need `paired = TRUE` — pairing is powerful and forgetting it wastes it; check the shape first (Lesson 62's histograms) rather than running normality tests ritually; with many outcome comparisons, some p < 0.05 results appear by chance alone (multiple testing — flag it, or adjust); and always ask whether the difference *matters clinically* — 0.4 mmol/mol of HbA1c can be "significant" and clinically irrelevant.
::quiz
Q The 95% CI for a difference in means is (-0.8, +3.2). This tells you…
- The difference is exactly 1.2
* Values from slightly negative to moderately positive are all compatible — including zero, so no confident direction
- The test is broken
- Both groups are identical
! A CI crossing zero is the interval version of p > 0.05 — but it says more: how big the difference could plausibly be, either way.
::quiz
Q For length-of-stay comparisons between two wards, the usual better choice is…
- t-test, because means matter
* Mann-Whitney (wilcox.test), because LOS is heavily skewed and rank-based tests aren't distorted by the tail
- Chi-square
- No test exists
! Skew wrecks mean-based inference at modest n. Rank tests (or comparing medians with bootstrap CIs) fit the data's reality.
::ex
Compare Systolic BP by sex: histogram to check shape, then run the appropriate test, and write ONE sentence reporting group summaries, the difference (with CI if t-test), and the p-value - in that order.
~~~r
sbp <- lab_results |>
  filter(test == "Systolic BP") |>
  left_join(patients, by = "patient_id")

ggplot(sbp, aes(value)) + geom_histogram(binwidth = 5) + facet_wrap(~sex)
# Roughly symmetric -> t-test is fine:
t.test(value ~ sex, data = sbp)

# Reporting template:
# "Mean SBP was X in females and Y in males (difference Z, 95% CI a-b, p = c)."
::key
- t.test for means (Welch default), wilcox.test for skewed data; paired = TRUE when it's the same people.
- Read: means → CI of difference → p-value; CI crossing zero = direction unproven.
- Significance is not importance; multiple comparisons inflate false positives.
#LESSON 6|Correlation and the ecological fallacy
::p
"Deprivation and emergency admissions look related — how strongly?" **Correlation** puts a number on the straight-line association between two continuous variables: `r`, from −1 (perfect negative) through 0 (none) to +1 (perfect positive).
::code|r|Correlation in R
library(tidyverse)
set.seed(3)
lsoa <- tibble(
  imd_score = runif(60, 5, 55),
  adm_rate  = 60 + 1.9 * imd_score + rnorm(60, 0, 14)
)

cor(lsoa$imd_score, lsoa$adm_rate)                 # Pearson r, ~0.8
cor(lsoa$imd_score, lsoa$adm_rate,
    method = "spearman")                            # rank-based
cor.test(lsoa$imd_score, lsoa$adm_rate)            # r + CI + p-value
::p
**Pearson** measures *linear* association and is disturbed by outliers and skew; **Spearman** correlates the *ranks*, so it captures any consistently increasing/decreasing relationship and shrugs at outliers — often the safer default for skewed health data. Rough reading of |r|: 0.1 weak, 0.3 moderate, 0.5+ strong — but context rules; in noisy population data, r = 0.4 can be a headline.

Non-negotiable habit: **plot before you correlate.** Anscombe's quartet (type `anscombe` into R) is four datasets with identical r ≈ 0.82 — one linear, one curved, one outlier-driven, one vertical-line-with-a-fluke. The number cannot distinguish them; your eyes can in half a second. `ggplot(lsoa, aes(imd_score, adm_rate)) + geom_point()` costs one line.

Two famous traps, both career-relevant:

**Correlation is not causation.** Deprived areas have higher admission rates — does deprivation *cause* admissions, do admissions-relevant factors (age structure, chronic disease, access) cluster in deprived areas, or does something upstream drive both? r cannot say. Language discipline follows: "associated with", never "leads to", unless the study design earns it.

**The ecological fallacy.** Your correlation used *areas* as the unit. It tells you deprived *LSOAs* have higher rates — it does **not** tell you deprived *individuals* within them are the ones being admitted. Area-level and individual-level associations can differ in size and even direction (a classic: areas with more immigrants once showed higher literacy — because immigrants settled in literate cities, not because immigrants were more literate). Public health lives on area data, so this fallacy is an occupational hazard: keep conclusions at the level of the data.
::quiz
Q An LSOA-level analysis finds r = 0.7 between IMD score and smoking-related admissions. Which claim is justified?
- Deprived individuals smoke more
- Deprivation causes smoking admissions
* More deprived areas tend to have higher smoking-related admission rates
- 70% of admissions are due to deprivation
! Area-level association, area-level claim — anything about individuals commits the ecological fallacy, and "causes" needs far more than r. (And r is not a percentage of anything.)
::quiz
Q Why does "plot before you correlate" matter if you already have r?
- It doesn't; r is sufficient
* Very different relationships (curves, outlier artefacts) can share the same r — the scatterplot reveals which you have
- Plots change the value of r
- Journals require plots
! Anscombe's quartet: identical r, wildly different stories. r summarises a shape it cannot see; you must look.
::ex
Using the lsoa tibble: draw the scatterplot with a linear trend line (geom_smooth(method = "lm")), compute both Pearson and Spearman correlations, and write a one-sentence conclusion that respects both traps above.
~~~r
ggplot(lsoa, aes(imd_score, adm_rate)) +
  geom_point(colour = "#0e7c86") +
  geom_smooth(method = "lm", se = TRUE, colour = "#005EB8") +
  labs(x = "IMD score (higher = more deprived)",
       y = "Emergency admission rate per 1,000")

cor(lsoa$imd_score, lsoa$adm_rate)                       # ~0.8
cor(lsoa$imd_score, lsoa$adm_rate, method = "spearman")

# "At LSOA level, higher deprivation is strongly associated with higher
#  emergency admission rates; this area-level association does not by
#  itself show that deprived individuals are those admitted, nor that
#  deprivation causes admission."
::key
- Pearson = linear, outlier-sensitive; Spearman = ranks, robust; always scatterplot first.
- Correlation ≠ causation: say "associated with".
- Ecological fallacy: area-level findings license only area-level claims.
#LESSON 6|Linear regression: modelling a continuous outcome
::p
Correlation says *how strongly*; **regression** says *how much* — "each additional IMD point is associated with 1.9 more admissions per 1,000" is a sentence commissioners can act on. Linear regression fits the best straight line through the data and hands you its equation.
::code|r|lm: the workhorse
model <- lm(adm_rate ~ imd_score, data = lsoa)
summary(model)
::p
The output's essentials: under `Coefficients`, the `(Intercept)` (predicted rate when IMD = 0 — often outside the data, mathematically necessary, substantively meaningless) and the **slope** for `imd_score` — the change in outcome per one-unit change in predictor. Each has a standard error, and `Multiple R-squared` reports the share of outcome variation the model explains (R² = r² with one predictor).

The **broom** package turns that printout into a tidy tibble — far better for reports:
::code|r|Tidy model output
library(broom)
tidy(model, conf.int = TRUE)
#  term        estimate  conf.low conf.high  ...
#  (Intercept)    60.2      52.9     67.5
#  imd_score       1.91      1.68     2.14
glance(model)     # R², n, and friends in one row
::p
Reading: rate = 60.2 + 1.91 × IMD, and we're 95%-confident the true slope lies between about 1.7 and 2.1 — comfortably away from zero, so the association isn't plausibly noise.

**Multiple regression** adds predictors, and this is the real prize — each coefficient becomes the association *holding the others constant*:
::code|r|Adjusting for a second variable
# lm(adm_rate ~ imd_score + pct_over_65, data = lsoa2)
::p
If the IMD slope shrinks once age is included, part of the raw deprivation gradient was really an age-structure story — regression is how "adjusted for age" happens outside of standardisation.
