Honesty checklist before believing any lm: the relationship should look reasonably straight (scatterplot again); residuals shouldn't fan out or curve (`plot(model, which = 1)`); outliers shouldn't own the slope (refit without the extreme point; if the answer changes materially, say so); and predictions outside the observed x-range are extrapolation — the line knows nothing out there. And the ecological fallacy still applies: an area-level model licenses area-level sentences.
::quiz
Q In tidy(model), imd_score has estimate 1.91, conf.low 1.68, conf.high 2.14. The plain-English reading?
- IMD explains 191% of admissions
* Each 1-point rise in IMD score is associated with ~1.9 more admissions per 1,000 (95% CI 1.7 to 2.1)
- Admissions rise by 1.91% per year
- The intercept is 1.91
! The slope is change-in-outcome per unit-of-predictor, with the CI expressing precision. Getting this sentence right is half of applied statistics.
::quiz
Q Adding pct_over_65 to the model halves the imd_score coefficient. Best interpretation?
- The model is broken
* Part of the crude deprivation association was accounted for by age structure; the remaining slope is the age-adjusted association
- Age causes deprivation
- R² must have fallen
! Coefficients in multiple regression are adjusted for co-included variables. Shrinkage on adjustment = the adjuster explained some of the story.
::ex
Fit the model on lsoa, extract a tidy coefficient table with CIs, and use the model to predict the admission rate for an LSOA with IMD score 40 (predict() with newdata). State one caveat about that prediction.
~~~r
model <- lm(adm_rate ~ imd_score, data = lsoa)
tidy(model, conf.int = TRUE)

predict(model, newdata = tibble(imd_score = 40))
# ≈ 60.2 + 1.91*40 ≈ 137 per 1,000

# Caveat: a point prediction from an area-level model with scatter around
# the line (R² < 1); real LSOAs at IMD 40 vary widely around ~137, and the
# model says nothing about individuals.
::key
- lm(outcome ~ predictor); slope = change in outcome per unit predictor; broom::tidy for clean output.
- Multiple regression = adjusted associations; coefficient shifts reveal confounding.
- Check linearity, residuals, outlier influence; never extrapolate quietly.
#LESSON 6|Logistic regression and odds ratios
::p
Health outcomes are usually yes/no — readmitted or not, vaccinated or not, died or survived. A straight line can't model a probability (it would cheerfully predict 130% or −20%), so we use **logistic regression**, which models the *log-odds* of the outcome. Its coefficients, exponentiated, become the **odds ratios** that fill every epidemiology paper you'll ever read.
::code|r|glm with family = binomial
set.seed(9)
readm <- tibble(
  age65    = rbinom(500, 1, 0.45),
  imd_low  = rbinom(500, 1, 0.4),            # most-deprived flag
  readmit  = rbinom(500, 1, plogis(-2 + 0.9*age65 + 0.55*imd_low))
)

model <- glm(readmit ~ age65 + imd_low,
             data = readm, family = binomial)

library(broom)
tidy(model, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)
#  term        estimate conf.low conf.high
#  (Intercept)    0.13     0.09     0.18      <- baseline ODDS, not a ratio
#  age65          2.42     1.57     3.77
#  imd_low        1.69     1.10     2.61
::p
`exponentiate = TRUE` is the magic switch: raw coefficients are log-odds (unreadable); exp() turns them into odds ratios. Reading the table: patients 65+ have **2.4 times the odds** of readmission compared with under-65s, *adjusted for deprivation*; the most deprived have 1.7 times the odds, adjusted for age. OR = 1 means no association — so a CI crossing 1 (e.g. 0.9–1.4) means the data can't rule out "nothing".

Precision of language, because it's routinely fumbled:
- **Odds** = p/(1−p): an event with probability 0.25 has odds of 1-to-3 (0.33). Odds are not probabilities.
- An odds *ratio* compares two groups' odds. "2.4 times the odds" is correct; "2.4 times as likely" is not (though for rare outcomes — roughly <10% — the OR approximates the risk ratio, which is why the sloppy phrasing survives).
- Adjusted ORs answer "holding the other variables in this model constant" — no more, no less. Unmeasured confounders remain unmeasured.

Continuous predictors work too — `glm(readmit ~ age, …)` gives the OR *per year of age*; per-decade is often friendlier (`I(age/10)`).

This model is the analytic backbone of case-control studies, risk-factor analyses and adjusted service comparisons; recognising `tidy(m, exponentiate = TRUE)` output means you can now read the results tables of most observational papers.
::quiz
Q An adjusted OR for smoking is 1.8 (95% CI 1.2-2.7) for delayed discharge. Correct sentence?
- Smokers are 80% likely to be delayed
* Smokers have about 1.8 times the odds of delayed discharge, adjusted for the model's other variables; the CI excludes 1, so an association is supported
- Smoking causes 1.8 delays
- 1.8% of delays are due to smoking
! Odds ratio ≠ probability ≠ percentage of cases. And "adjusted" earns exactly one clause: conditional on covariates in THIS model.
::quiz
Q An OR's 95% CI runs 0.85 to 1.60. The takeaway?
- The exposure is protective
- The exposure is harmful
* The data are consistent with anything from a modest decrease to a moderate increase — no clear association demonstrated
- The model failed to converge
! CIs crossing 1 (the null for ratios) mean uncertainty spans "less", "same" and "more". Report the interval, not a verdict.
::ex
Fit the readmission model above but add an interpretation task: produce the tidy OR table, then write the one-sentence result for imd_low suitable for a report, including the CI and the word "adjusted".
~~~r
model <- glm(readmit ~ age65 + imd_low, data = readm, family = binomial)
tidy(model, exponentiate = TRUE, conf.int = TRUE)

# "After adjusting for age group, patients living in the most deprived
#  areas had 1.7 times the odds of 30-day readmission compared with other
#  patients (OR 1.69, 95% CI 1.10 to 2.61)."
::key
- Binary outcome → glm(..., family = binomial); exponentiate coefficients → odds ratios.
- OR = ratio of odds, not of probabilities; CI crossing 1 = no demonstrated association.
- "Adjusted for" means: for the variables in this model, only.
#LESSON 6|SPC charts: Making Data Count
::p
Every metric wiggles. The monthly ritual of "admissions rose 6% — investigate!" followed next month by "fell 5% — celebrate!" is organisational noise-chasing, and NHS England's **Making Data Count** programme exists to end it. Its tool is the **statistical process control (SPC) chart**: your metric over time, with a centre line (the mean) and **control limits** at ±3 standard deviations marking the range that pure routine variation should occupy.

The core distinction: **common-cause variation** (the ordinary jitter of a stable process — inside the limits, no pattern) versus **special-cause variation** (a signal that something real changed). Only special cause deserves investigation; reacting to common cause is management by lottery.

Signals, per the NHS rules: a point outside a control limit; a **shift** — 6 or more consecutive points on the same side of the centre line; a **trend** — 6 or more consecutively rising or falling; and 2 of 3 points hugging a limit (outer third). No signal = the process is doing what it always does, however dramatic this month's wiggle feels.
::code|r|NHSRplotthedots: the community standard
library(tidyverse)
# install.packages("NHSRplotthedots")   # NHS-R community package
library(NHSRplotthedots)

set.seed(21)
monthly <- tibble(
  month = seq(as.Date("2024-04-01"), by = "month", length.out = 24),
  falls = c(rpois(15, 30), rpois(9, 41))      # a real step-up at month 16
)

ptd_spc(monthly,
        value_field = falls,
        date_field  = month,
        improvement_direction = "decrease")
::p
`ptd_spc()` draws the chart with Making Data Count's standard colouring: grey points for common cause, orange/blue highlighting for special cause in the concerning/improving direction. `improvement_direction` tells it which way is good (falls: down). The example's built-in step change gets flagged as a shift — the chart finds in seconds what a table of 24 numbers hides.

Practice notes: you need ~12+ points before limits mean much (fewer = plot the run chart and wait); when a process *genuinely* changes (a new pathway, a merged service), recalculate limits from the change point rather than blending two eras; and never put targets on as if they were control limits — a target is an aspiration, limits are what the process actually delivers. The chart's most valuable sentence is often "this process will keep producing ~30–45 falls a month until the process itself changes" — capability, not commentary.

This is also your defence in meetings: "this month is within control limits" is the polite, evidenced version of "that's noise, and chasing it wastes a ward manager's afternoon."
::quiz
Q A metric falls 8% from last month but sits well inside its control limits with no rule triggered. The SPC-literate response?
- Launch an investigation — 8% is a lot
* Note it as common-cause variation; no action from one wiggle inside the limits
- Recalculate the limits immediately
- Delete the point as an outlier
! Two-point comparisons are how noise gets promoted to news. The limits define what "normal wiggle" is for THIS process; inside them, this month is unremarkable.
::quiz
Q Seven consecutive months sit below the centre line, all inside the limits. This is…
- Nothing — no point breached a limit
* A shift: a special-cause signal that the process level has likely changed
- Proof the target was met
- A reason to widen the limits
! Runs rules catch sustained change that never breaches ±3σ. Six-plus one side of the centre is astronomically unlikely by chance — investigate (and if it's a real new level, re-baseline).
::ex
The falls data above shows a shift. Suppose investigation confirms a genuine process change (new frailty ward opened month 16). Describe — in code comments or prose — the two things the SPC chart should now do, and re-run ptd_spc using its rebase argument (ptd_rebase) to implement the first.
~~~r
# 1) Recalculate limits from the change point, so the chart describes the
#    NEW process rather than blending two different eras:
ptd_spc(monthly,
        value_field = falls,
        date_field  = month,
        improvement_direction = "decrease",
        rebase = ptd_rebase(as.Date("2025-07-01")))

# 2) Annotate the change (caption/vertical line) so readers connect the
#    signal to its cause — the chart shows THAT it changed; the note says WHY.
::key
- Common cause = routine wiggle (no action); special cause = signal (limit breach, 6+ shift, 6+ trend).
- NHSRplotthedots::ptd_spc implements Making Data Count house style; state improvement_direction.
- Re-baseline at confirmed process changes; targets are not control limits.
#LESSON 6|Funnel plots: comparing organisations fairly
::p
Rank ten GP practices by any percentage and someone finishes last — but with 40 patients, one practice's "worst" may be pure chance while a giant practice's small deviation is a genuine signal. **Funnel plots** are the fair way to compare units of wildly different sizes, and they're standard furniture in NHS and UKHSA benchmarking.

The construction: x-axis = size (denominator: list size, admissions, births); y-axis = the indicator (a proportion or rate); a horizontal line at the overall average; and **control limits** that funnel inward as size grows — wide for small units (chance moves them a lot), tight for large ones (it can't). Limits at ±2SD (~95%) and ±3SD (~99.8%) are conventional.
::code|r|Building one
library(tidyverse)
set.seed(5)
practices <- tibble(
  practice = sprintf("P810%02d", 1:12),
  n        = c(2100, 3400, 5600, 1200, 8200, 4400,
               2900, 6100, 950, 7300, 3800, 5100)
) |>
  mutate(uptake = rbinom(12, n, 0.62) / n)      # true rate 62% everywhere

overall <- weighted.mean(practices$uptake, practices$n)

limits <- tibble(n = seq(500, 9000, 50)) |>
  mutate(se    = sqrt(overall * (1 - overall) / n),
         lo95  = overall - 1.96 * se,  hi95 = overall + 1.96 * se,
         lo998 = overall - 3.09 * se,  hi998 = overall + 3.09 * se)

ggplot() +
  geom_line(data = limits, aes(n, lo95), linetype = "dashed", colour = "grey55") +
  geom_line(data = limits, aes(n, hi95), linetype = "dashed", colour = "grey55") +
  geom_line(data = limits, aes(n, lo998), colour = "grey40") +
  geom_line(data = limits, aes(n, hi998), colour = "grey40") +
  geom_hline(yintercept = overall, colour = "#005EB8") +
  geom_point(data = practices, aes(n, uptake), colour = "#0e7c86", size = 2.5) +
  scale_y_continuous(labels = scales::label_percent()) +
  labs(title = "Vaccination uptake by practice",
       x = "Eligible patients", y = "Uptake",
       caption = "Dashed 95% and solid 99.8% limits around county average")
::p
Reading it: points **inside** the funnel are consistent with the average — differences are what chance produces at that size, and ranking them is astrology. Points **outside** (especially beyond 99.8%) are genuinely different — worth understanding, which is not the same as worth blaming: outliers can reflect case mix, coding practice, data quality or population differences before anything about performance. The funnel starts conversations; it doesn't end them.

Every point in this simulation came from an identical 62% true rate — yet the small practices scatter dramatically. That's the whole argument against league tables in one picture.

Real-world refinements: indicators often need risk adjustment or age-standardisation *before* the funnel (compare like with like); count/rate indicators use Poisson-based limits (the **PHEindicatormethods** package computes both flavours properly); and **overdispersion** — when far more than ~5% of points fall outside 95% limits, unit-level differences beyond chance exist system-wide, and methods that widen limits accordingly (as UKHSA's Fingertips does) stop the plot crying wolf.
::quiz
Q A 950-patient practice sits 6 points below average, inside the 95% funnel. A 8,200-patient practice sits 3 points below, outside 99.8%. Who warrants attention?
- The small practice — bigger gap
* The large practice — its smaller gap is statistically incompatible with chance at that size; the small one is within expected variation
- Both equally
- Neither; averages are all that matter
! The funnel exists precisely because gap size alone misleads: precision scales with n. Distance-in-SEs, not distance-in-points, is the currency.
::quiz
Q A practice sits above the 99.8% limit on an unadjusted indicator. Before "performance", rule out…
* Case mix, population age structure, coding and data-quality differences
- Nothing; the statistics are conclusive
- The colour of the point
- Whether the practice complained before
! Outside-the-funnel = "really different", not "really worse at the job". Standardise or risk-adjust, check the data, then talk performance.
::ex
Add labels to the plot for any practice outside the 95% limits (join practices to the limit formula: flag when |uptake − overall| > 1.96*sqrt(overall*(1−overall)/n)), using ggrepel or geom_text.
~~~r
flagged <- practices |>
  mutate(se = sqrt(overall * (1 - overall) / n),
         outside95 = abs(uptake - overall) > 1.96 * se)

# add to the ggplot:
#  geom_text(data = filter(flagged, outside95),
#            aes(n, uptake, label = practice),
#            vjust = -1, size = 3)
# In this simulation, most runs flag ~0-1 practices — consistent with
# a common true rate plus chance, which is exactly the lesson.
::key
- Funnel plots: indicator vs denominator size; limits narrow as n grows; inside = chance-compatible.
- Small units swing wildly by chance — league tables punish smallness, funnels don't.
- Outside the funnel starts an inquiry (case mix, coding, data) — not a verdict; use PHEindicatormethods for proper rate funnels.
#LESSON 6|Screening and tests: sensitivity, specificity and the PPV trap
::p
Screening programmes, diagnostic pathways, surveillance case definitions — all rest on four numbers from one 2×2 table, and on one counterintuitive fact every public health analyst must be able to explain to a room.

The table crosses test result against true status:
::table
 |Disease present|Disease absent
Test positive|True positive (TP)|False positive (FP)
Test negative|False negative (FN)|True negative (TN)
::p
- **Sensitivity** = TP / (TP+FN): of people *with* the disease, the share the test catches. A property of the test.
- **Specificity** = TN / (TN+FP): of people *without* it, the share correctly cleared. Also a property of the test.
- **PPV** (positive predictive value) = TP / (TP+FP): of people who *test positive*, the share who actually have it. **Not** a fixed property of the test — it depends on how common the disease is.
- **NPV** = TN / (TN+FN): of negatives, the share truly clear.

The trap, worked honestly. A good test — 90% sensitive, 95% specific — screens 10,000 people for a condition with **1% prevalence** (100 cases):
::code|r|The base-rate calculation
prev <- 0.01; sens <- 0.90; spec <- 0.95; N <- 10000

tp <- N * prev * sens              # 90 true positives
fn <- N * prev * (1 - sens)        # 10 missed
fp <- N * (1 - prev) * (1 - spec)  # 495 false positives!
tn <- N * (1 - prev) * spec        # 9405

ppv <- tp / (tp + fp)              # 0.154 — 15%
npv <- tn / (tn + fn)              # 0.999
::p
A positive result from this genuinely good test is wrong **85% of the time** — because the 5% false-positive rate applies to 9,900 healthy people, swamping the 90 real cases. Raise prevalence to 20% (a symptomatic clinic population) and PPV jumps past 80% with the *same test*. This is why screening programmes confirm positives with second-stage tests, why "test everyone for everything" backfires, and why the UK National Screening Committee weighs harms of false positives as seriously as benefits of detection.

The sensitivity/specificity **trade-off** is a dial, not a fact of nature: loosen a case definition (or a test threshold) and sensitivity rises while specificity falls. Surveillance during an outbreak often chooses sensitive definitions (miss nothing, tolerate false alarms); confirmation for a harmful treatment chooses specific ones. State the choice; don't let it be an accident.

For analysts, the same table evaluates *your* artefacts: a data-quality flag, a frequent-attender prediction, a record-linkage rule — each has a sensitivity, a specificity, and a PPV that collapses when the thing you're hunting is rare. "The algorithm is 95% accurate" is meaningless until you ask: accurate at what prevalence, and which errors does it make?
::quiz
Q Same test (90% sens, 95% spec) used in a population where prevalence is 0.1% instead of 1%. PPV roughly…
- Stays 15% — it's the same test
* Falls to under 2% — false positives from the huge healthy majority swamp the few true cases
- Rises, because the disease is rarer
- Becomes 90%
! PPV rides prevalence. At 1-in-1,000: ~9 TPs vs ~500 FPs → PPV ≈ 1.8%. The rarer the target, the less a positive means — the core argument against indiscriminate screening.
::quiz
Q An outbreak team broadens the case definition "so we don't miss anyone". The predictable statistical consequence?
- Higher specificity, lower sensitivity
* Higher sensitivity, lower specificity — more false positives to investigate
- Both improve
- PPV becomes 100%
! Casting a wider net catches more true cases AND more bystanders. Sometimes right for surveillance — but the false-positive workload is a real cost to plan for.
::ex
A frequent-attender prediction model flags patients: of 200 who truly became frequent attenders, it flagged 150; of 9,800 who didn't, it flagged 490. Compute sensitivity, specificity and PPV, and write the sentence a service manager needs to hear about acting on flags.
~~~r
tp <- 150; fn <- 50; fp <- 490; tn <- 9310

sens <- tp / (tp + fn)        # 0.75
spec <- tn / (tn + fp)        # 0.95
ppv  <- tp / (tp + fp)        # 0.234

# "The model catches 75% of future frequent attenders, but only about
#  1 in 4 flagged patients will actually become one — any intervention
#  offered to flagged patients must be cheap and harmless enough to give
#  to three 'wrong' patients for every right one."
::key
- Sensitivity/specificity describe the test; PPV describes a positive result and depends on prevalence.
- Rare conditions → even excellent tests yield mostly false positives; hence confirmatory stages.
- The same 2×2 evaluates models, flags and linkage rules — always ask "at what prevalence?"
#LESSON 7|Connecting R to databases: DBI and odbc
::p
Until now you've imagined CSV exports ferrying data from SQL to R. Professionals skip the ferry: R talks to the warehouse **directly**, so the query, the analysis and the report are one unbroken, re-runnable chain. Two packages do it: **DBI** (the universal steering wheel) and **odbc** (the connector for the ODBC drivers that NHS SQL Server estates run on).
::code|r|The canonical connection
library(DBI)
library(odbc)

con <- dbConnect(odbc(),
                 Driver   = "ODBC Driver 18 for SQL Server",
                 Server   = "trust-dw.example.nhs.uk",
                 Database = "PublicHealth",
                 Trusted_Connection = "yes",     # your Windows login
                 TrustServerCertificate = "yes") # common on internal estates
::p
`Trusted_Connection = "yes"` uses your network identity — **no password in the script**, which is exactly how it should be. If a system genuinely needs credentials, they live in environment variables or the OS keyring (the **keyring** package), never in code: scripts get shared, emailed and committed; secrets in them leak. Treat a password in a script as an incident, not a style choice.

Once connected, the verbs are few:
::code|r|Talk to the database
dbListTables(con)                        # what's there?
adm <- dbGetQuery(con, "
  SELECT admission_id, patient_id, admission_date, specialty
  FROM admissions
  WHERE admission_date >= '2025-04-01'
")                                        # runs SQL, returns a tibble-like df
dbDisconnect(con)                         # always, when done
::p
Everything from Modules 2–3 pours through that `dbGetQuery()` string — CTEs, window functions, the lot — and lands as a data frame ready for dplyr and ggplot. The division of labour from Lesson 40's world holds: let the database filter, join and aggregate millions of rows; bring back the thousands you'll actually analyse.

One-time driver setup per machine:
::oscode|text
@win
Usually already present on NHS machines (check: search "ODBC Data Sources (64-bit)" → Drivers tab). If missing, install "Microsoft ODBC Driver 18 for SQL Server" from Microsoft's site — or via IT self-service on managed laptops.
@mac
brew install unixodbc
brew tap microsoft/mssql-release https://github.com/Microsoft/homebrew-mssql-release
brew install msodbcsql18
@linux
sudo apt install unixodbc unixodbc-dev
# then Microsoft's msodbcsql18 package per their apt instructions for your distro
::p
Diagnostics, in order, when connection fails: does the driver name in your code *exactly* match one listed by `odbc::odbcListDrivers()`? Can you reach the server at all (VPN? on-site network?)? Do you have permission on that database (the DBA grants it; ask)? Error messages here are verbose but honest — read the first line.
::note|warn|You are on live rails now
A connection is real. SELECT freely, but everything from Lesson 41 applies double: create only in your sandbox, and treat DELETE/UPDATE/DROP as requiring a colleague's eyes. The playground forgives; production remembers.
::quiz
Q Why is Trusted_Connection = "yes" preferred over Uid/Pwd arguments in the connection string?
- It's faster
* It authenticates with your network identity, so no credential ever appears in the script
- It bypasses permissions
- It works without a driver
! Scripts travel — passwords in them travel too. Integrated auth (or keyring/env vars when unavoidable) keeps secrets out of code, which is baseline governance.
::quiz
Q dbGetQuery(con, sql) returns…
- A live pointer that updates as data changes
* A regular data frame: the result copied into R's memory at execution time
- A CSV on disk
- Nothing until you call collect()
! dbGetQuery executes and pulls the full result immediately — a snapshot. (Lazy, stays-in-database references are the NEXT lesson's dbplyr trick.)
::ex
Write the connection-and-extract script for a monthly job: connect with integrated auth, pull emergency admissions for the latest complete month into emerg, print how many rows arrived (a sanity check that belongs in every extract), and disconnect. Use a clearly-labelled date placeholder.
~~~r
library(DBI); library(odbc)

con <- dbConnect(odbc(),
                 Driver = "ODBC Driver 18 for SQL Server",
                 Server = "trust-dw.example.nhs.uk",
                 Database = "PublicHealth",
                 Trusted_Connection = "yes")

emerg <- dbGetQuery(con, "
  SELECT admission_id, patient_id, admission_date, specialty
  FROM admissions
  WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'
    AND admission_date >= '2026-06-01'
    AND admission_date <  '2026-07-01'
")
message(nrow(emerg), " rows extracted")   # eyeball vs expectation

dbDisconnect(con)
::key
- DBI + odbc connect R to SQL Server; integrated auth keeps credentials out of scripts.
- dbGetQuery runs any SQL you can write and returns a data frame; disconnect when done.
- Heavy lifting in the database, analysis in R; sandbox rules apply on live systems.
#LESSON 7|dbplyr: dplyr that writes SQL for you
::p
Here's a happy secret: the dplyr you learned in Module 4 can run *inside the database*. **dbplyr** translates dplyr pipelines into SQL, ships them to the server, and only pulls results when you say so — one syntax, two engines.
::code|r|Lazy tables
library(dplyr)
library(dbplyr)

adm_db <- tbl(con, "admissions")     # a REFERENCE, not a download

summary_q <- adm_db |>
  filter(admission_method == "Emergency",
         admission_date >= "2025-04-01") |>
  count(specialty, sort = TRUE)

summary_q                 # runs on the server, previews first rows
show_query(summary_q)     # reveals the generated SQL
::p
`tbl()` creates a **lazy table**: nothing moves. Each verb adds to a query plan; printing runs it server-side and shows a preview (note the row count displays as `??` — it genuinely hasn't counted). The pipeline above becomes `SELECT specialty, COUNT(*) ... GROUP BY ... ORDER BY ...` — check with `show_query()`, which is also a wonderful SQL tutor: write dplyr, read the SQL it makes.

The verb that matters most is the one that ends laziness:
::code|r|collect(): the moment of transfer
local_summary <- summary_q |> collect()   # NOW rows travel into R memory
::p
The professional pattern is thus: **filter and aggregate lazily, collect small.** A million-row table grouped to 40 specialty counts should cross the network as 40 rows, not a million. Collecting a whole raw table to "work on it locally" recreates the CSV-ferry problem with extra steps.

What translates? Most of your toolkit — filter, select, mutate, group_by, summarise, joins between `tbl()`s, many functions (`year()`, `paste0`, `if_else`) map to SQL equivalents. What doesn't: arbitrary R functions (your own `age_at()`, model fitting, ggplot) — those need real data, so they come *after* `collect()`. The error "no translation for function X" means exactly that: do X post-collect.

Mixed workflows are the everyday reality:
::code|r|Lazy heavy lifting, local finesse
plot_df <- tbl(con, "admissions") |>
  filter(admission_date >= "2024-04-01") |>
  count(month = substr(admission_date, 1, 7)) |>   # translates to SQL
  collect() |>                                     # small result crosses
  mutate(month = as.Date(paste0(month, "-01")))    # R-only finishing

# ggplot(plot_df, aes(month, n)) + geom_line()
::quiz
Q adm_db <- tbl(con, "admissions") followed by filter() and count() has so far transferred how much data to your laptop?
- The whole table
- The filtered rows
* Essentially none — lazy tables build a query; data moves only at collect() (or a preview when printed)
- One page of rows per verb
! Laziness is the feature: computation goes TO the data. The network bill arrives only when collect() does.
::quiz
Q Your pipeline errors: "no known translation for my_age_function()". The fix?
- Rewrite the function in C
* Move that step after collect() — custom R functions can't run inside the database
- Wrap it in show_query()
- Use = instead of <-
! dbplyr can only translate what SQL can express. Aggregate lazily, collect the small result, then apply bespoke R locally.
::ex
Using lazy tables on con: count completed admissions per specialty for FY 2025/26 with mean length of stay (julianday isn't SQL Server — use datediff(day, admission_date, discharge_date) via sql() or rely on dbplyr's difftime translation; simplest portable route: compute los in the database with sql("DATEDIFF(day, admission_date, discharge_date)")), show the generated SQL, then collect.
~~~r
q <- tbl(con, "admissions") |>
  filter(!is.na(discharge_date),
         admission_date >= "2025-04-01",
         admission_date <  "2026-04-01") |>
  mutate(los = sql("DATEDIFF(day, admission_date, discharge_date)")) |>
  group_by(specialty) |>
  summarise(n = n(), mean_los = mean(los, na.rm = TRUE)) |>
  arrange(desc(n))

show_query(q)     # inspect the T-SQL it wrote
result <- collect(q)
::key
- tbl(con, "table") = lazy reference; verbs build SQL; show_query() reveals it.
- collect() is the only data transfer — filter/aggregate first, collect small.
- Untranslatable R (own functions, models, plots) belongs after collect().
#LESSON 7|Parameterised queries: the injection lesson
::p
Sooner or later you'll build SQL from user input — a practice code typed into a Shiny box, a month passed to a report. The obvious way is string-gluing, and the obvious way is a security hole famous enough to have a cartoon (xkcd's "Little Bobby Tables"):
::code|r|NEVER build SQL like this
practice <- input_from_somewhere      # imagine: "P81004"

sql <- paste0("SELECT * FROM admissions WHERE practice_code = '",
              practice, "'")
dbGetQuery(con, sql)
::p
If that input is ever `P81004'; DROP TABLE admissions;--`, your polite SELECT becomes two statements, the second catastrophic. That's **SQL injection** — for decades the top web vulnerability class, and the mechanism is exactly this: data allowed to rewrite code. Even without malice, an innocent apostrophe (`O'Brien` in a surname filter) shatters the string and crashes the query. Same bug, friendlier symptom.

The fix is structural, not vigilance: **parameterised queries** keep SQL and values in separate channels. The database receives the query shape with placeholders, then the values — which can *never* be executed, only compared:
::code|r|The safe pattern
sql <- "SELECT admission_id, admission_date, specialty
        FROM admissions
        WHERE practice_code = ?
          AND admission_date >= ?"

dbGetQuery(con, sql, params = list("P81004", "2025-04-01"))
::p
`?` marks each slot (SQL Server via odbc uses `?`; some backends use `$1` — DBI handles the dialect). Apostrophes, semicolons, comment markers — all arrive as inert text. The **glue** package's `glue_sql()` offers the same safety with readable named templates:
::code|r|glue_sql for readable templates
library(glue)
q <- glue_sql("
  SELECT * FROM admissions
  WHERE practice_code = {practice}
    AND admission_date >= {start_date}
", practice = "P81004", start_date = "2025-04-01", .con = con)
dbGetQuery(con, q)
::p
(`glue_sql`, not plain `glue` — the `_sql` variant quotes and escapes for the connection.) One legitimate wrinkle: placeholders carry *values*, not identifiers — you can't parameterise a table or column name. When a table name must vary, validate it against a hard-coded allow-list first (`match.arg()` is perfect), then interpolate. Choose from known-good; never trust free text into structure.

The habit generalises beyond security: parameterised queries are also how Lesson 66-style reports take a month or an organisation as input — the same three lines rerun for any practice, injection-proof and typo-proof at once.
::quiz
Q Why does a parameterised query stop injection where careful string escaping tends to fail?
- Parameters are encrypted
* SQL and data travel separately by design — a value cannot become code, so there's nothing to escape imperfectly
- The database blocks apostrophes
- It runs in a sandbox
! Escaping is a fight you must win every time; separation removes the fight. The value channel is inert by construction.
::quiz
Q A user-supplied string chooses which TABLE to query. Correct handling?
- paste0 it in — tables can't be injected
- Parameterise it with ?
* Validate against a fixed allow-list of known table names, then interpolate the matched value
- Refuse ever to vary a table
! Placeholders can't hold identifiers, so the defence shifts: user input SELECTS from your list; it never BECOMES the name. match.arg(input, c("admissions","vaccinations")) is the idiom.
::ex
Write a safe function monthly_extract(con, specialty, month_start, month_end) returning that specialty's admissions between two dates, using params. Bonus: reject unknown specialties with match.arg against a small allow-list.
~~~r
monthly_extract <- function(con, specialty, month_start, month_end) {
  specialty <- match.arg(specialty,
    c("Respiratory", "Cardiology", "General Medicine",
      "Elderly Care", "Gastroenterology"))

  dbGetQuery(con, "
    SELECT admission_id, patient_id, admission_date
    FROM admissions
    WHERE specialty = ?
      AND admission_date >= ?
      AND admission_date <  ?",
    params = list(specialty, month_start, month_end))
}
# monthly_extract(con, "Respiratory", "2026-06-01", "2026-07-01")
::key
- Never paste user input into SQL; one apostrophe breaks it, one payload owns it.
- params = list(...) / glue_sql separate code from data — injection becomes impossible, not just unlikely.
- Identifiers can't be parameters: allow-list them; values always ride placeholders.
#LESSON 7|Project structure and {here}: work that travels
::p
Open a six-month-old folder containing `analysis_FINAL_v3_ACTUAL.R`, `data(2).csv` and `Untitled1.R`, and you meet the enemy: past-you. Structure is the cheap defence, and one layout serves almost every analysis:
::code|text|The standard analysis project
readmissions-2026/
├── readmissions-2026.Rproj    ← open THIS, always
├── README.md                  ← what, why, how to run, who to ask
├── data/                      ← raw inputs, READ-ONLY, never edited
├── R/                         ← functions used by multiple scripts
├── scripts/                   ← numbered pipeline steps
│   ├── 01_extract.R
│   ├── 02_clean.R
│   ├── 03_analyse.R
│   └── 04_report.qmd
├── outputs/                   ← everything generated; deletable & rebuildable
└── docs/                      ← specs, definitions, sign-offs
::p
The numbered scripts encode run order — a stranger (or a scheduler) knows 01 precedes 02 without asking. The deeper contract: `outputs/` is **disposable by design**. Delete it, run 01→04, and everything returns identically. If a chart exists that no script can regenerate, that chart is a rumour.

Paths are where projects break in transit, and the villain is the absolute path: `C:/Users/priya/Documents/...` works for exactly one human on one machine. `setwd()` is the same trap with extra steps — it hard-codes *your* geography into shared code. The cure is the **here** package:
::code|r|here(): paths that work everywhere
library(here)

adm     <- read_csv(here("data", "admissions.csv"))
ggsave(here("outputs", "figures", "trend.png"), p)
::p
`here()` finds the project root (the `.Rproj` marks it) and builds paths from there — identical code on Windows, Mac, Linux, a colleague's laptop, or the server that runs it nightly at 3am. Combined with Lesson 42's clean-session settings, you get the professional test of reproducibility: **fresh R session, Run All, zero errors** — on someone else's machine.

The `README.md` deserves five honest minutes: purpose in one paragraph, data sources and their sensitivity, the run order, known caveats, and an owner's name. Six months on, that file is the difference between refresh-in-an-hour and archaeology-for-a-week. The same applies to naming inside the project: `02_clean.R` and `rate_by_lsoa.csv` explain themselves; dates in output filenames (`summary_2026-07.csv`) stop last month's numbers impersonating this month's.
::note|study|The 20-minute refactor
Take any past piece of work and re-house it in this layout with here() paths. Then the acid test: fresh session, Run All. The errors you hit are exactly the hidden dependencies this lesson exists to kill.
::quiz
Q A script beginning setwd("C:/Users/asha/projects/flu") fails on every other machine. The structural fix?
- Everyone creates that folder path
* An RStudio Project plus here() — paths resolve from the project root wherever it lives
- Comment out the setwd
- Use shorter paths
! Portability comes from relative-to-root paths, not from cloning one analyst's disk layout. .Rproj + here() is the standard.
::quiz
Q Why must outputs/ be entirely regenerable from scripts?
- To save disk space
* It proves the analysis is real: every number and figure has executable provenance — and refreshes become trivial
- Because Git ignores it
- Outputs are unimportant
! "Delete outputs, rerun, identical results" is reproducibility made operational. Anything that can't be rebuilt can't be trusted or updated.
::ex
Sketch (as a text tree) the project structure for a quarterly vaccination-uptake report that extracts from SQL, standardises rates in R, and renders a Quarto report — then write the here()-based read and save lines used in 02_clean.R.
~~~text
vax-uptake-quarterly/
├── vax-uptake-quarterly.Rproj
├── README.md
├── data/                       # any fixed lookups (ESP weights etc.)
├── R/standardise.R             # shared rate functions
├── scripts/
│   ├── 01_extract.R            # DBI pull → data/raw_extract.csv
│   ├── 02_clean.R
│   ├── 03_standardise.R
│   └── 04_report.qmd
└── outputs/
~~~r
# in 02_clean.R
library(here)
raw   <- read_csv(here("data", "raw_extract.csv"))
clean <- raw |> ...              # cleaning steps
write_csv(clean, here("outputs", "clean_uptake.csv"))
::key
- One layout: data/ (read-only), scripts/ (numbered), outputs/ (regenerable), README (five honest minutes).
- here() + .Rproj replace setwd() and absolute paths — code runs anywhere.
- The test: fresh session, Run All, on another machine.
#LESSON 7|Git: version control for analysts
::p
`report_v2_final_AMENDED_use_this.R` is version control by filename, and it fails exactly when it matters. **Git** — the real thing — records snapshots of your project so you can see what changed, when, why, and by whom, and return to any point. It is standard equipment in NHS analytics job adverts, and the backbone of the RAP lesson ahead.

Install once:
::oscode|text
@win
winget install --id Git.Git
(Or download from git-scm.com. On managed NHS laptops: IT self-service catalogue.)
Then in Git Bash or PowerShell:
  git config --global user.name  "Your Name"
  git config --global user.email "you@example.nhs.uk"
@mac
xcode-select --install   # if you did Lesson 46's setup, Git is already there
git config --global user.name  "Your Name"
git config --global user.email "you@example.nhs.uk"
@linux
sudo apt install git
git config --global user.name  "Your Name"
git config --global user.email "you@example.nhs.uk"
::p
The mental model is three zones: your **working folder** (files as they are), the **staging area** (changes you've selected for the next snapshot), and the **repository** (the permanent history). Four commands cover 90% of solo use:
::code|text|The daily loop
git init                      # once per project: start tracking
git status                    # what's changed? (run constantly)
git add scripts/02_clean.R    # stage chosen changes ("." for all)
git commit -m "Exclude test patients from cohort"
git log --oneline             # the story so far
::p
A **commit** is a labelled snapshot of the whole project. Commit small and often — one logical change per commit — and write messages that state *why*: "Fix FY boundary so 31 March lands in prior year" will save a future investigator an afternoon; "changes" will not. RStudio's Git pane does all of this with checkboxes if the terminal isn't your happy place; the concepts are identical.

Two guardrails specific to health work. First, **`.gitignore`**: a file listing what Git must never track — and in the NHS that means *data*:
::code|text|.gitignore for a health analytics repo
data/
outputs/
*.csv
*.xlsx
.Rhistory
.Rdata
::p
Git histories are forever and get copied around; a patient-level extract committed once is exceptionally hard to truly delete. **Code in Git, data in the warehouse** — the repository holds the recipe, never the ingredients. Second, look before you leap back: `git diff` shows exactly what changed since the last commit, and `git restore file.R` discards uncommitted changes to a file — the "undo" that makes fearless experimentation rational.
::quiz
Q The single most important entry in a health-analytics .gitignore?
- .Rhistory
* Data files/folders — patient-level data must never enter the repository's permanent history
- README.md
- The .Rproj file
! Git never forgets, and repos travel. Ignoring data/ and spreadsheet/CSV patterns keeps IG intact by construction. Recipe in Git; ingredients stay home.
::quiz
Q Which commit message earns its keep?
- "stuff"
- "final version"
* "Use ONS 2025 mid-year estimates as rate denominators"
- "asdfgh"
! Messages are letters to future investigators (usually you). State the why/what; the diff already shows the how.
::ex
Write the terminal session that takes the vax-uptake project under version control: initialise, create a .gitignore protecting data and outputs, stage everything, and make a well-messaged first commit. Then show the command to view what changed after you later edit a script.
~~~text
cd vax-uptake-quarterly
git init
printf "data/\noutputs/\n*.csv\n*.xlsx\n.Rhistory\n.RData\n" > .gitignore
git add .
git status                      # confirm data/ is NOT listed
git commit -m "Initial pipeline: extract, clean, standardise, report"

# ... later, after editing 02_clean.R ...
git diff scripts/02_clean.R     # exact line changes since last commit
::key
- Commit = labelled snapshot; small commits, messages that say why.
- status → add → commit → log is the daily loop; diff/restore make experiments safe.
- .gitignore data and outputs: Git holds code only — always, in health work.
#LESSON 7|GitHub: sharing, review and the audit trail
::p
Git on your laptop is a diary; **GitHub** (or an NHS-hosted GitLab/Azure DevOps — same ideas) makes it a shared, backed-up, reviewable record. NHS-R, UKHSA and a growing list of trusts and DHSC teams publish real pipelines there; your future employer will likely ask for your profile.

Connect local to remote:
::code|text|First push
# after creating an empty repo on github.com:
git remote add origin https://github.com/you/vax-uptake-quarterly.git
git push -u origin main          # upload history; -u remembers the pairing
# thereafter just:  git push  (send)  /  git pull  (fetch colleagues' work)
::p
(Authentication today means a Personal Access Token or SSH key, not your password — GitHub walks you through it once.)

The collaboration engine is the **branch + pull request** loop, and it's worth learning even solo because it's how teams actually operate:
::code|text|The feature-branch loop
git switch -c fix-fy-boundary     # new branch: a parallel line of history
# ...edit, commit as usual...
git push -u origin fix-fy-boundary
# then on GitHub: open a Pull Request
::p
A **pull request (PR)** says "here are my changes — review before they join `main`." A colleague reads the diff, comments line-by-line ("should this be < rather than <=?"), you push fixes, they approve, it merges. For analytical code this is **QA with an audit trail**: six months later, the PR shows what changed, who checked it, and what they debated — governance evidence that a folder of emailed scripts can never produce. Keep `main` as the always-works branch; experiments live on branches until reviewed.

Etiquette that makes review work: small PRs (one purpose, reviewable in ten minutes), a description saying what and why plus how you tested it, and reviews that critique code, never people. Reviewing others' PRs, incidentally, is the fastest reading-skills course an early-career analyst can take.

Round out the habit with the platform's furniture: **Issues** as the to-do/bug list wired to commits ("Fixes #12" in a commit message closes the ticket), the **README** rendering as your repo's front page, and — because mistakes happen — the rule that a secret or dataset pushed even briefly counts as exposed: rotate the credential and treat removal as damage limitation, not erasure. Public repo or private, the .gitignore discipline from last lesson is doing load-bearing work.
::quiz
Q For an NHS analytics team, the pull request's chief value is…
- Prettier diffs
* Documented peer review: every change to the pipeline carries evidence of who checked it and why it was made
- Faster code execution
- Automatic bug fixing
! PRs turn QA from a favour into a record. When an indicator is challenged, "reviewed and approved, discussion attached" is an answer; "Dave glanced at it" is not.
::quiz
Q You accidentally pushed a file containing a database password, then deleted it in the next commit. Status?
- Safe — it's gone
* Compromised — history retains it; rotate the credential now and scrub history as secondary cleanup
- Safe if the repo is private
- GitHub deletes secrets automatically
! Deletion adds a new snapshot; the old one persists (and forks/caches may hold it). Credentials that touch a remote are burned: rotate first, tidy second.
::ex
Describe the full loop — commands plus GitHub actions — for safely changing the suppression threshold in a shared team pipeline from <5 to <8, with a colleague's review, referencing issue #23.
~~~text
git switch main && git pull                 # start current
git switch -c raise-suppression-threshold
# edit R/safe_outputs.R; update tests/docs
git add R/safe_outputs.R
git commit -m "Raise small-number suppression to <8 (fixes #23)"
git push -u origin raise-suppression-threshold
# On GitHub: open PR "Raise suppression threshold to <8"
#   - description: why (new IG guidance), what changed, tests re-run
#   - request review from colleague
# Colleague comments → you push fixes → approval → Merge
git switch main && git pull                 # everyone's main now has it
::key
- push/pull sync local Git with a shared remote; main stays always-working.
- Branch → PR → review → merge = QA with a permanent audit trail.
- Pushed secrets/data are exposed forever: rotate credentials; prevention beats scrubbing.
#LESSON 7|RAP: Reproducible Analytical Pipelines
::p
Everything in this module has been converging on one idea with an official name. A **Reproducible Analytical Pipeline (RAP)** is analysis built so that a competent stranger can regenerate every published number from code alone — no manual steps, no mystery spreadsheets, no "then Sandra copies column C". Born in the Government Statistical Service, championed across the NHS (NHS Digital's RAP community, the Goldacre Review's "RAP should be the norm"), it is less a technology than a standard of evidence.

Why organisations care: the monthly report that takes three days of copy-paste becomes a ten-minute re-render; the "how was this figure derived?" audit question gets answered by pointing at a script; the analyst who wins the lottery stops being a single point of failure; and errors, when found, are fixed once in code rather than hunted through seventeen spreadsheets.

The commonly-cited **baseline RAP** ingredients — all of which you now own:
::table
Ingredient|You learned it
Open-source language (R/Python), not point-and-click|Modules 4-6
Version control with meaningful history|Git, previous lessons
Peer review of code|Pull requests
Data pipelines, not manual steps (extract→clean→analyse→report as code)|DBI, dplyr, Quarto
No manual output editing — reports generated whole|Quarto, gt
Documentation a stranger can follow|README, project structure
::p
Silver and gold levels add packaging functions properly, automated testing (next lesson), and continuous integration. But the mindset upgrade is the real content: **the pipeline is the product.** A one-off analysis answers today's question; a pipeline answers it every month forever, survives your promotion, and can be inspected by anyone with a licence to look.

Honest words about the friction: RAP is slower the *first* time — writing `01_extract.R` takes longer than clicking export. The payback arrives at refresh two and compounds monthly; "we publish this quarterly" is the strongest possible RAP business case. Legacy processes get converted incrementally (automate the extract this quarter, the report next), and the pragmatic minimum for any new work is simply: *could I delete outputs/ and rebuild it with one command?* If yes, you're RAP-shaped already.

Where to see it done: NHS England's RAP community of practice publishes guidance and real examples on GitHub; UKHSA and DHSC repos show production pipelines; the Goldacre Review (2022) is the strategic argument, and quoting it in interviews rarely hurts.
::quiz
Q The defining test of a RAP is…
- It uses R
* A competent stranger can reproduce every published output from the code and documentation alone
- It runs in under a minute
- It has no bugs
! Language, speed and perfection are incidental; independent reproducibility is the standard. R+Git+PRs are the usual means to that end.
::quiz
Q The strongest early candidate for RAP conversion in a team's portfolio?
- A one-off ministerial question answered last year
* The quarterly report rebuilt by hand every three months
- A colleague's personal scratch analysis
- A dataset nobody uses
! RAP costs up front and pays per repetition. Recurring products amortise the investment fastest — and remove the most copy-paste risk.
::ex
Audit this described process against the baseline RAP table and list what passes/fails, then name the single highest-value fix: "Analyst exports SUS data via a GUI each month, pastes into master.xlsx, updates pivot tables, copies charts into Word, emails v7 to the team; last year's analyst left no notes."
~~~text
Fails: open-source pipeline (GUI export + Excel), no version control
       (emailed v7), no code review, manual outputs (paste into Word),
       no documentation (no notes). Passes: essentially nothing.

Highest-value first fix: script the extract (01_extract.R via DBI) —
it anchors the chain in code, kills the riskiest manual step, and
every later stage (clean → analyse → Quarto report) can then attach
to it incrementally. Add git init the same afternoon.
::key
- RAP = published numbers regenerable from code by a stranger; the pipeline is the product.
- Baseline: open language, Git, peer review, no manual steps, generated outputs, docs.
- Convert recurring work first; incremental adoption is expected and fine.
#LESSON 7|Scheduling and testing: analysis that runs itself (safely)
::p
The finished pipeline still needs a finger on the Run button — until it doesn't. Automation is the last mile of RAP, and it has two halves: making the run happen, and making sure a *bad* run can't slip through quietly.

**Scheduling.** First make the pipeline runnable in one non-interactive command — a `run_all.R` that sources 01→04 (or `quarto render`). Then hand it to the OS:
::oscode|text
@win
Task Scheduler (built in): Create Task → Trigger: Monthly, day 1, 07:00 →
Action: Program = "C:\Program Files\R\R-4.5.1\bin\Rscript.exe"
        Arguments = "run_all.R"
        Start in  = C:\path\to\project
Tick "Run whether user is logged on or not". Test via right-click → Run.
@mac
crontab -e   # then add:
0 7 1 * * cd /Users/you/project && /usr/local/bin/Rscript run_all.R >> logs/run.log 2>&1
(launchd .plist files are the native alternative; cron is simpler to start.)
@linux
crontab -e   # minute hour day month weekday
0 7 1 * * cd /home/you/project && Rscript run_all.R >> logs/run.log 2>&1
# server estates may offer cron, systemd timers, or Airflow — same idea.
::p
Non-negotiables for unattended runs: **absolute certainty about paths** (the scheduler's working directory isn't your terminal's — hence `here()` and the explicit `cd`/Start-in), **logging** (append stdout/stderr to a dated log; a silent 3am failure discovered at the 9am board is the classic automation injury), and **credentials** via integrated auth or env vars, never freshly typed.

**Testing** is the other half, in two flavours. *Code tests* check your functions once and forever — the **testthat** package:
::code|r|tests/testthat/test-age.R
library(testthat)

test_that("age_at handles the birthday boundary", {
  expect_equal(age_at("1960-03-01", "2026-02-28"), 65)
  expect_equal(age_at("1960-03-01", "2026-03-01"), 66)
})

test_that("financial_year assigns 31 March correctly", {
  expect_equal(financial_year(as.Date("2026-03-31")), "2025/26")
  expect_equal(financial_year(as.Date("2026-04-01")), "2026/27")
})
::p
`testthat::test_dir("tests")` runs the lot; every bug you ever fix should leave a test behind as a tombstone, so it can't return. *Data validation* checks each run's inputs and outputs against expectations — because the code being right doesn't make this month's feed right:
::code|r|Assertions that stop bad runs loudly
stopifnot(
  nrow(adm) > 1000,                          # feed didn't arrive empty
  !any(duplicated(adm$admission_id)),        # key still unique
  all(adm$admission_date <= Sys.Date()),     # no future admissions
  all(rates$denominator >= rates$numerator)  # sanity of construction
)
::p
(The **pointblank** package dresses this up with reports and thresholds; `stopifnot` is the honest core.) The design principle: **fail loud, fail early** — a pipeline that halts with "duplicate admission_ids: 214" at 07:02 and emails the log is a guardian; one that publishes a wrong dashboard politely is a liability. Wire the assertions between every stage, and have the final stage refuse to overwrite yesterday's good output when today's checks fail.

That's the module complete: connection, translation, safety, structure, history, review, standard, automation. Your analysis now runs without you — which, paradoxically, is what makes you valuable.
::quiz
Q A scheduled pipeline broke at 3am; the team learned when a director questioned the dashboard. The missing engineering?
- A faster server
* Logging plus loud failure: assertions halting the run and surfacing the error (log/email) before outputs publish
- More RAM
- Running it manually instead
! Automation without observability is risk-transfer to your readers. Halt on failed checks, write logs, never overwrite good outputs with bad.
::quiz
Q Where should the lesson of a just-fixed bug permanently live?
- In the analyst's memory
* As a testthat case reproducing it — so the suite fails if it ever returns
- In an email thread
- In the commit message only
! Tests are institutional memory that executes. The FY-boundary bug fixed today, encoded as expect_equal, can never quietly reoffend.
::ex
Write the pre-publication assertion block for the vaccination pipeline covering: at least 10,000 rows extracted; no duplicate (patient_id, vaccine, dose_number); all date_given values within 2020-01-01..today; every uptake proportion in [0, 1]; and each practice's denominator at least its numerator.
~~~r
stopifnot(
  nrow(vax) >= 10000,
  !any(duplicated(vax[, c("patient_id", "vaccine", "dose_number")])),
  all(vax$date_given >= as.Date("2020-01-01")),
  all(vax$date_given <= Sys.Date()),
  all(uptake$uptake >= 0 & uptake$uptake <= 1),
  all(uptake$denominator >= uptake$numerator)
)
# Place after cleaning, before render; schedule wraps the whole script,
# so any failure halts the run and lands in the log — loudly.
::key
- One-command run_all + OS scheduler (Task Scheduler / cron / launchd); log everything, mind paths.
- testthat guards functions forever; each fixed bug becomes a test.
- stopifnot/pointblank validate each run's data; fail loud and early, never publish past a failed check.
#LESSON 8|How LLMs actually work — and why they confidently invent things
::p
You'll use AI assistants throughout your career, so this module teaches them as what they are: power tools with a distinctive failure mode. That starts with an honest model of the machine.

A large language model (LLM) — the engine behind Claude, ChatGPT, Copilot and colleagues — is a system trained on vast amounts of text to do one thing extraordinarily well: **predict plausible continuations**. Ask a question and it generates, token by token, the response most consistent with the patterns it learned. Because human knowledge is written down in patterns, this produces genuinely useful behaviour: it has absorbed more SQL, more R, more epidemiology documentation than any human ever will, and it can remix that fluently for your specific situation.

But note what's absent from that description: a database of facts, a concept of truth, and any awareness of *not knowing*. When you ask about the boundary between mastered territory and gaps, the machine does the same thing in both cases — produces the most plausible-sounding continuation. Plausible-and-true and plausible-and-false feel identical from the inside. The result is **hallucination**: fabricated function arguments, invented ICD-10 codes, confidently-cited papers that don't exist, an R package that *should* exist but doesn't. The fluency never wavers, which is exactly what makes it dangerous — wrongness arrives wearing the same confident prose as rightness.

Consequences worth internalising before you write a single prompt:
- **Confidence is not evidence.** The model's tone carries zero information about correctness. Calibrate on verification, never on how sure it sounds.
- **Errors cluster at the edges**: niche packages, recent changes (training data has a cutoff), UK-specific details amid US-dominated training text, and precise identifiers (codes, dosages, section numbers) where "plausible" and "correct" diverge sharpest. A model may nail the shape of an age-standardisation function and fumble the exact ESP2013 weights.
- **It mirrors your framing.** Ask "why does eating late cause weight gain?" and it tends to answer inside your premise. Leading questions get led answers — ask neutrally when you actually want the truth.
- **Code has a home advantage**: generated code can be *run*. Execution is a truth-test essays never get, which is why coding is among the highest-value, most verifiable uses — provided you actually run and check it, the discipline the rest of this module builds.

None of this is a reason for refusal any more than Wikipedia's editability was. It defines the contract: **AI drafts, you verify, you remain accountable.** The analyst who signs the report is you; "the AI said so" has precisely the professional standing of "some bloke in the pub said so".
::quiz
Q An LLM cites "Fenton & Marsh (2021), Journal of Public Health Analytics" for a claimed statistic. Correct posture?
- Trust it — citations mean it checked
* Treat it as unverified until you find the paper yourself; plausible-looking citations are a classic hallucination
- Trust it if the journal sounds real
- Ask the model to confirm it's real
! The model generates citation-shaped text by the same mechanism as everything else. Asking it to self-confirm just samples the same process twice. Look it up.
::quiz
Q Why is code generation a comparatively SAFE use of LLMs?
- Models are trained mostly on code
* Code can be executed and tested — you get an objective external check that prose claims never receive
- Code cannot contain errors
- Syntax highlighting reveals mistakes
! Verifiability, not infallibility, is the safety. A wrong function fails a test; a wrong "fact" just sits there sounding right. This asymmetry shapes the whole module.
::ex
Classify each task as lower-risk or higher-risk for AI assistance, with one line of reasoning: (a) "explain what this LEFT JOIN does", (b) "what is the exact ICD-10 code for viral pneumonia?", (c) "draft a first-pass R function to band ages", (d) "what were the 2025 changes to UKHSA notification regulations?"
~~~text
(a) Lower — explanation of code you can read alongside; errors are visible.
(b) Higher — precise identifier where plausible ≠ correct; verify in the
    official ICD-10 classification, never trust recall.
(c) Lower — a draft you will run and test; execution is the check.
(d) Higher — recent, UK-specific, possibly post-training-cutoff; go to
    the primary source (legislation/UKHSA guidance), use AI only to
    summarise text YOU supply.
::key
- LLMs predict plausible text; they contain no truth-check and no sense of not-knowing.
- Hallucination clusters at edges: niche, recent, UK-specific, exact identifiers; confidence ≠ correctness.
- Contract: AI drafts, you verify, you sign. Code is safest because execution tests it.
#LESSON 8|Prompting for SQL: context is everything
::p
Ask an AI to "write a query for readmissions" and you'll get syntactically perfect SQL for tables you don't have, against a readmission definition you didn't choose. The model can't see your database. **Everything it needs must travel in the prompt** — and learning what that is turns AI from a toy into a colleague.

The anatomy of a working SQL prompt:
::code|text|A prompt that earns its answer
You are helping with SQL for health analytics (SQLite dialect).

Schema (fictional training data, no real patients):
  admissions(admission_id INTEGER PK, patient_id, admission_date TEXT
    ISO-8601, discharge_date TEXT nullable = still in hospital,
    admission_method 'Emergency'/'Elective', specialty)
  patients(patient_id INTEGER PK, sex 'F'/'M', birth_date TEXT,
    practice_code)

Task: emergency admission COUNT per specialty for financial year
2025/26 (2025-04-01 to 2026-03-31 inclusive), completed spells only,
sorted busiest first.

Constraints: half-open date logic (>= start, < next FY start);
exclude NULL discharge_date; plain ANSI-ish SQL, no vendor functions.

Return: the query, then one sentence on any assumption you made.
::p
Each block is doing load-bearing work. **Dialect** stops it reaching for `GETDATE()` in SQLite (or `strftime` on SQL Server — your Lesson 40 knowledge, now a prompt ingredient). **Schema with types and quirks** ("nullable = still in") prevents invented columns and wrong NULL handling. **Precise definitions** — you specified the FY boundary rather than letting the model pick one; date logic is exactly where plausible-but-wrong hides. **Stated constraints** encode your house rules. And asking it to **surface assumptions** converts silent guesses into visible, checkable ones.

Then the non-negotiable step: **run it, and interrogate the result like you would a junior colleague's work.** Row count sane? Do the specialties match a simple `SELECT DISTINCT specialty`? Does a hand-checked month agree? Lesson 39's data-quality reflexes apply to AI output *more*, not less, because the errors are fluent.

Iteration beats mega-prompts. Get the core query working, then refine in turns: "now add a column for the percentage of each specialty's total", "now restrict to patients 65+ at admission — age from birth_date, exact, not year subtraction". Each turn is small enough to verify before building on it. And when a query *fails*, paste the exact error plus the query back — error-message debugging is among the model's strongest events, because millions of identical stack-trace conversations trained it.

Two prompts for learning rather than producing: "explain this query line by line, then tell me what breaks if discharge_date is NULL" (comprehension check on inherited code), and "here's my query and my intent — what edge cases would make it silently wrong?" (the model as adversarial reviewer; you'll meet this properly in the code-review lesson).
::note|ai|The schema-context habit
Keep a sanitised schema snippet (table names, columns, types, quirks — never data) in a text file. Pasting it into any SQL conversation is ten seconds that eliminates the entire invented-column class of failure.
::quiz
Q The single highest-value ingredient missing from "write me a query for flu admissions by month"?
- Politeness
* The schema — table and column names, types, and quirks the model cannot otherwise know
- A request for comments
- The output format
! Without schema, every table and column is a guess in disguise. Dialect, definitions and constraints follow — but schema is the difference between your database and a fictional one.
::quiz
Q AI returns a plausible query using admissions.age_at_admission — a column that doesn't exist. This most likely happened because…
- The model read your database and misremembered
* You didn't supply the schema, so it generated a column that commonly exists in similar tables
- SQLite renamed the column
- The query is fine; the column is hidden
! Hallucinated columns are the signature failure of schema-free prompts: the model predicts what a table LIKE yours typically has. Feed the real structure and this class of error largely vanishes.
::ex
Write the full prompt (schema included) asking for: patients with 3+ emergency admissions in FY 2025/26, returning patient_id and the count, using the playground's real tables. Then list the two checks you'd run on the returned query before trusting it.
~~~text
Dialect: SQLite. Fictional training data.
Schema: admissions(admission_id PK, patient_id, admission_date TEXT ISO,
        discharge_date TEXT nullable, admission_method
        'Emergency'/'Elective', specialty)
Task: patient_id and number of emergency admissions for patients with
      3 or more emergency admissions between 2025-04-01 (inclusive)
      and 2026-04-01 (exclusive). Sort by count descending.
Constraints: GROUP BY + HAVING, half-open dates, no vendor functions.
Return the query plus any assumptions.

Checks before trusting:
1. Run it; pick one returned patient_id and list their raw emergency
   admissions with a simple WHERE query — is the count right and are
   all dates inside the FY window?
2. Sanity-scan the logic: HAVING COUNT(*) >= 3 present? Filter on
   admission_method = 'Emergency' BEFORE grouping? Date logic half-open?
::key
- Prompts must carry what the model can't see: dialect, schema + quirks, exact definitions, constraints.
- Ask for stated assumptions; iterate in small verified steps; paste exact errors when debugging.
- Interrogate AI SQL like a junior's work: run, count, spot-check, cross-check.
#LESSON 8|Prompting for R and debugging with AI
::p
R prompting shares the SQL rules — context, precision, iteration — with different context to carry: not schema, but **data shape and tidyverse dialect**.

The data-shape trick is `dput()` on a tiny sample, which serialises real structure into pasteable text:
::code|r|Give the model your actual structure
dput(head(adm, 4))
# structure(list(admission_id = c(101, 102, 103, 104),
#   sex = c("F","M","F","F"), age = c(67,45,82,34), ...
::p
Paste that (fictional/sanitised data only — the governance lesson is next) and the model stops guessing your column names and types. Alternatively `glimpse()` output works for structure-without-values. Add your dialect preferences explicitly — "tidyverse style, native pipe |>, no loops where a vectorised solution exists" — or you'll receive base-R answers in one reply and data.table in the next.

**Debugging is the killer app.** The workflow that works:
::code|text|The debugging prompt pattern
[1] What I'm trying to do (one sentence of intent)
[2] Minimal code that reproduces the problem
[3] The COMPLETE error or warning, verbatim
[4] What I expected vs what happened
[5] glimpse()/dput() of the data involved
::p
Intent matters because the commonest R disasters aren't errors at all — they're *wrong results*: the join that fanned out (Lesson 51's trap), the `mean()` silently NA, the filter that dropped NAs you meant to keep. "Here's my code and output — I expected 5,210 rows and got 8,914" hands the model the discrepancy, which is where diagnosis lives. For honest-to-goodness errors, paste them whole: `object of length 0`, `unexpected symbol`, `could not find function` each have signature causes the model pattern-matches instantly (the last one usually means a missing `library()` call — it'll tell you).

Verification discipline, R-flavoured: run suggested code on your data *in a fresh object* (never overwrite your working data with an untested fix); check `nrow()` before/after; spot-check a few rows manually; and beware the model's fondness for **inventing arguments and packages** — `read_csv(guess_encoding = TRUE)` looks plausible and doesn't exist; a recommended package may be real, abandoned, or imaginary. `?function_name` settles arguments in five seconds; a CRAN search settles packages.

Two upgrade prompts: "rewrite this working-but-ugly pipeline for clarity, tidyverse style, same behaviour — explain each change" (refactoring with a diff you can judge), and "add error handling: what happens to this function when the input has zero rows / all NAs / a missing column?" (the model enumerates edge cases faster than you'll brainstorm them; you decide which matter).
::note|ai|Fresh-object discipline
adm2 <- suggested_fix(adm), compare, THEN promote. The thirty seconds this costs has saved more analyses than any other habit in this module. Untested AI code overwriting your only in-memory copy of an hour's cleaning is a self-inflicted outage.
::quiz
Q An AI-suggested fix uses arrange(.by_group = TRUE, desc(n)) and you're unsure .by_group is real. Fastest reliable check?
- Ask the AI if it's sure
* ?arrange in the console — the official documentation lists the actual arguments
- Try random alternatives
- Assume it's fine; it sounds plausible
! Invented-but-plausible arguments are a signature R hallucination. Documentation is authoritative and one keystroke away; the model re-asserting itself is not evidence. (This one happens to be real — but you check.)
::quiz
Q Your pipeline runs clean but returns 8,914 rows where you expected ~5,200. The most productive AI prompt includes…
- Just the code
* Code + the expected-vs-actual row counts + glimpse() of both input tables — wrong-result bugs live in the discrepancy
- "Why is R broken?"
- The code with column names redacted for safety
! No-error bugs need the model to see what you EXPECTED. Counts plus structure usually lets it spot the fan-out join or NA-dropping filter immediately. (Sanitised structure, not patient data.)
::ex
You have a join producing duplicates. Compose the debugging prompt using the pattern above (invent minimal reproducing code with two tiny tribbles where the lookup table secretly has a duplicate key), and state what a GOOD answer should identify.
~~~text
Intent: one row per admission with practice name attached.
Code:
  adm <- tribble(~admission_id, ~practice_code, 1, "P1", 2, "P2")
  lkp <- tribble(~practice_code, ~practice_name,
                 "P1", "Riverside", "P2", "Hilltop", "P2", "Hilltop Branch")
  left_join(adm, lkp, by = "practice_code")
Expected: 2 rows. Got: 3.
Structure: [glimpse output of both]

A good answer identifies: lkp is not unique on practice_code (P2 twice),
so the join fans out; fixes = deduplicate/choose one row per key in lkp
(distinct or a rule for which branch), and cite checking
count(lkp, practice_code) |> filter(n > 1) before joining — plus dplyr's
relationship = "many-to-one" argument to make the expectation enforced.
::key
- Carry context: dput()/glimpse() of sanitised data, dialect preferences, intent.
- Debug with the 5-part pattern; wrong-results bugs need expected-vs-actual, not just code.
- Verify: fresh objects, row counts, ?docs for invented arguments, CRAN for invented packages.
#LESSON 8|AI code review: the adversarial second pair of eyes
::p
So far AI has written code; the higher-leverage habit points it the other way. **Review** is where models shine: reading is cheaper than writing, your code is right there to check its claims against, and — unlike your kind colleague — the machine has infinite patience and no feelings to spare. But the value depends entirely on how you ask.

"Is this code okay?" begets "Looks good! Here are some minor suggestions ✨". Models are agreeable by disposition; vague review prompts harvest that agreeableness. The fix is **adversarial framing with specific targets**:
::code|text|A review prompt with teeth
Act as a sceptical senior analyst reviewing this R for a published
NHS report. Do NOT be polite. Find reasons the OUTPUT could be WRONG.

Check specifically:
1. Joins — any that could fan out or silently drop rows?
2. NA handling — where do missing values change a result invisibly?
3. Date logic — FY boundaries, half-open vs closed intervals, timezone?
4. Aggregation — counting rows where it should count distinct people?
5. Filters — do exclusions match the stated definition in the comment?

Definition it must implement: emergency readmission within 30 days,
index = first emergency admission per patient in FY, readmission
counted from DISCHARGE date.

[code pasted here]

Report: numbered findings, each with severity (wrong-answer / fragile /
style), the line involved, and a one-line test that would expose it.
::p
The ingredients: a **role with permission to be harsh**; a **checklist drawn from the actual failure taxonomy** of health analytics (you built that taxonomy in Modules 2–4 — fan-out, NULLs, boundaries, rows-vs-people); the **intended definition**, so the model reviews against your spec rather than guessing one; and **findings-with-tests**, because "line 14 counts admissions not patients; test: compare against COUNT(DISTINCT patient_id)" is actionable where "consider reviewing your aggregation" is fog.

Then triage like a reviewer of the reviewer: findings split into *true positives* (fix, and add the exposing test to your testthat suite — Lesson 87's tombstone habit), *false positives* (the model misread; fine, ignore — but if it misread, would a human? maybe a comment is due), and *style opinions* (adopt or don't; consistency with your team beats the model's aesthetics). Expect false alarms; an adversarial reviewer who never cries wolf isn't trying.

Where this fits your real QA: AI review is a **pre-filter, not a replacement** for the human peer review your PR process requires (Lesson 85) — it catches the mechanical 60% cheaply so your human reviewer spends attention on the judgement calls: is the definition right, is the comparison fair, should this be published at all. Those questions stay human. And run the review *before* the PR, not instead of it; arriving pre-reviewed is a courtesy to colleagues and makes their genuine findings pop.

One more move for inherited code: "explain what this script actually does, step by step, then list any behaviour that would surprise someone reading only the comments." Mismatches between code and comments are where legacy landmines live.
::quiz
Q Why does "review this code" underperform the adversarial checklist prompt?
- Models can't review code
* Models default to agreeable; without a mandate to find faults and specific failure classes to hunt, they produce compliments plus trivia
- Checklists slow the model down
- Politeness is required by the API
! You get what you frame for. Permission-to-be-harsh plus your domain's actual bug taxonomy (joins, NAs, dates, grain) converts a cheerleader into an auditor.
::quiz
Q The AI reviewer flags a genuine bug: your "patients admitted" metric counts admissions. After fixing the code, the durable next step is…
- Thank the model
* Encode the exposing check as a permanent test (e.g. distinct-patient count comparison) so the bug class can't silently return
- Delete the old commit history
- Re-run the same review weekly
! A finding is transient; a test is institutional memory (Lesson 87). The review's real product is your strengthened test suite.
::ex
Your colleague inherited a 200-line script with the comment "excludes day cases" but nobody trusts it. Write the review prompt, and name the two categories you expect findings to fall into that you'd treat differently.
~~~text
Prompt:
"Act as a sceptical reviewer. This R script claims to exclude day cases
(LOS = 0) before computing specialty LOS averages. (1) Explain step by
step what it ACTUALLY does. (2) List every point where behaviour could
diverge from the stated claim — NA discharge dates, LOS calculated
before vs after exclusion, joins altering row counts, filters on the
wrong table. (3) For each, give severity and a one-line test.
[script pasted]"

Treat differently:
- Wrong-answer findings (e.g. NAs make LOS NA, so day cases with missing
  discharge survive the filter): verify by running the proposed test,
  fix, keep the test.
- Style/clarity findings (naming, pipe formatting): batch for later or
  adopt via team conventions — never let them crowd out correctness.
::key
- Frame review adversarially: harsh role, domain-specific checklist, the intended definition, findings-with-tests.
- Triage findings: fix + keep the test / dismiss false alarms / treat style as optional.
- AI review pre-filters; human peer review still owns judgement — arrive at PRs pre-reviewed.
#LESSON 8|Governance: what never goes in the box
::p
This is the lesson to over-learn, because one bad paste can end a career and breach thousands of patients' trust at once. The tools change; the rule doesn't:

**Never put person-identifiable or sensitive data into a public AI tool. Ever.**

Typing into a consumer chatbot is **disclosure to a third party** — legally and practically like emailing the data to a company you have no agreement with. Depending on the service, prompts may be retained, reviewed by humans, or used in training. Public health work is built on data given under legal duties and public trust; UK GDPR, the DPA 2018, the common-law duty of confidentiality and your Caldicott training (Lesson 7) all reach this keyboard moment. "I was just asking for help with a script" mitigates nothing if the script's test data was real patients.

What counts as unsafe to paste is broader than names:
::table
Category|Examples
Direct identifiers|Names, NHS numbers, addresses, DOBs, postcodes
Quasi-identifying detail|Rare diagnosis + small area + age; "the 94-year-old in ward 3"
Small-number outputs|The table with a cell of 2 you're trying to suppress
Business-sensitive|Unpublished performance, incident details, contract terms
Credentials & infrastructure|Passwords, connection strings, server names, internal URLs
::p
The **sanitise-then-ask** workflow keeps AI useful anyway, because almost every technical question survives having its data removed:
::code|text|Same help, zero disclosure
UNSAFE:  "Why does this fail? filter(df, nhs_number == '943 476 5919')
          — Mrs Ellen Mabey, DOB 12/3/1941, HIV clinic list..."

SAFE:    "Why might filter(df, id_col == 'a value I know exists')
          return zero rows when the value is visibly in the data?
          id_col is character; the source was Excel."
          (Answer arrives just the same: trailing whitespace/encoding —
          and no human being was described to a server.)
::p
Structure travels, values don't: column names (if non-sensitive), types, `glimpse()` shapes, **invented** example rows that mimic the problem.
