If a bug genuinely needs data to reproduce, *fabricate* a minimal dataset that shows the same behaviour — a skill this course has quietly drilled via every `tribble()` example.

Know your **tool tiers**, because "AI" is not one risk level: public consumer tools (assume everything leaves; PID never); enterprise/approved deployments — some NHS organisations provide sanctioned AI with contracts, data-protection assessments and retention controls (their rules define what's permitted; often more, never everything); and local models running on trust infrastructure (data stays inside; still governed by your IG framework). The operative question is never "is AI allowed?" but "**which tool, under which agreement, for which data?**" — and your organisation's AI/IG policy answers it. If no policy exists yet, the public-tool rule above is the floor, and asking IG *before* a novel use is the move that ages well.

If a paste happens — autocomplete betrayal, wrong window, a moment's autopilot — it's a **data incident**: report it through your organisation's incident route immediately, same as a misdirected email. Fast honest reporting is protective, for patients first and, genuinely, for you; discovery-months-later is the version that destroys trust.
::quiz
Q A colleague pastes ten real rows — NHS numbers included — into a public chatbot "just to fix a pivot_wider error". The correct framing of what occurred?
- Fine: debugging is a legitimate purpose
* A disclosure of identifiable data to a third party — a reportable data incident regardless of intent
- Fine if the chat is deleted afterwards
- A problem only if the model was training that day
! Purpose doesn't transform disclosure into non-disclosure, and deletion buttons don't undo transmission. Ten fabricated rows would have fixed the same bug.
::quiz
Q Your trust provides an approved enterprise AI tool with a signed data-protection agreement. This means…
- Anything may now be pasted
* Its governance rules define what's permitted — approved ≠ unlimited; the IG framework still applies
- It's identical to the public tool
- IG approval is no longer needed for new uses
! Sanctioned tools widen the safe envelope under explicit conditions; they don't abolish the envelope. Read the policy; novel uses still go through IG.
::ex
Rewrite this unsafe prompt as a safe one that would get equally useful help: "My query returns the wrong readmission count for patient 4859223601 (John Okafor, DOB 03/05/1958, discharged 12 Jan from Barchester General cardiology after MI). Here are his 6 admission rows: [real data]. Why is the 30-day window missing the February one?"
~~~text
Safe version:
"SQLite. 30-day readmission logic seems to miss a qualifying event.
Schema: admissions(admission_id, patient_id, admission_date TEXT ISO,
discharge_date TEXT ISO, admission_method). Fabricated repro:

INSERT INTO t VALUES (1, 99, '2026-01-05', '2026-01-12', 'Emergency');
INSERT INTO t VALUES (2, 99, '2026-02-10', '2026-02-14', 'Emergency');

My rule: readmission if next admission_date is within 30 days of prior
DISCHARGE date. My query: [query]. It returns 0 readmissions for
patient 99; I expect 1 (Feb 10 is 29 days after Jan 12). Why?"

(Likely culprit the model can now find: comparing to admission_date
instead of discharge_date, or an off-by-one/exclusive boundary —
findable entirely without any real person in the prompt.)
::key
- PID, quasi-identifiers, small cells, credentials: never into public AI — pasting is disclosure.
- Sanitise-then-ask: structure and fabricated repro data get the same help with zero risk.
- Tool tiers differ (public / enterprise-approved / local); your IG policy governs; accidental pastes are incidents — report fast.
#LESSON 8|AI for documentation, explanation and learning
::p
The unglamorous uses are the reliably profitable ones. Documentation, explanation and translation are tasks where AI's weaknesses matter least — the source material is *in the prompt*, so the model summarises what's in front of it rather than recalling facts from the void — and where analyst time haemorrhages quietly.

**Documenting your own code.** Paste a working script; ask for a README section, roxygen-style function comments, or a plain-English "what this pipeline does" for the analyst who inherits it:
::code|text|Prompts that pay rent
"Write concise comments for this function: one line of purpose, one
per argument, one on the return value. Don't restate the code —
explain intent and any non-obvious choices."

"Draft the README 'How to run' section for this project structure:
[paste tree]. Audience: an analyst new to the team, R installed,
no context."

"Summarise what this 300-line inherited script does as: purpose,
inputs, outputs, step list, and anything surprising. Flag any step
whose purpose you CAN'T infer — don't guess."
::p
That last clause — *flag, don't guess* — is the safety catch on all summarisation prompts. You still read the draft against the code (it's a draft, and you sign it), but reviewing accurate-ish prose beats composing from a blank page by a factor your calendar will notice.

**Explaining upward and outward.** Analysts translate constantly: methods for a board paper, caveats for a press office, a funnel plot for a practice manager. AI drafts these translations well *when you supply the substance*:
::code|text|Audience translation
"Rewrite for a non-technical Trust board audience, ~120 words,
no jargon: 'Rates were directly age-standardised to ESP2013;
Barchester's apparent excess attenuated after adjustment,
suggesting age structure confounds the crude comparison.'
Keep the caveat. Do not add claims I haven't made."
::p
"Do not add claims" earns its place: models pad, and padding in a board paper becomes a commitment someone must later defend. The reverse direction also works — "explain this epidemiology paper's methods section like I'm a new analyst who knows R but not survival analysis" turns dense reading into scaffolded reading (verify against the paper; it's in your hand).

**Learning itself.** As a tutor, an LLM offers what documentation can't: infinitely patient reformulation. Stuck on window functions? "Explain PARTITION BY with a 6-row worked example, then give me three exercises with hidden answers, then critique my attempt." The **generation effect** from Lesson 11 still rules — you learn by attempting, so have it quiz you rather than lecture you. And keep the mnemonic honest: AI explanations of *concepts* are usually sound (concepts are heavily represented in training text); AI recall of *specifics* (this year's fingertips indicator IDs, exact regulation wording) is where you verify against the source. Concepts: trust-but-sample. Specifics: always check.

The meta-skill is noticing which of your weekly hours are draft-shaped. First-pass commit messages, tidy meeting notes into actions, an outline for a QA checklist, alt-text for twelve charts — none are the analysis, all consume the afternoon. Delegate drafts; keep judgement; sign only what you've read.
::quiz
Q Why is "summarise this script I've pasted" much safer than "what does the ukhsa_notifications package do"?
- It isn't; both hallucinate equally
* The first grounds the model in supplied text; the second relies on recall, where plausible invention thrives
- Scripts are shorter
- Packages are copyrighted
! Grounded summarisation constrains the model to material in-context. Recall questions sample training memory — exactly where confident fabrication lives. Structure your asks to be grounded whenever possible.
::quiz
Q In the board-paper translation prompt, "do not add claims I haven't made" defends against…
- Rudeness
* Padding that introduces unsupported statements you'd then own in a governance meeting
- Long words
- British spelling errors
! Models elaborate helpfully; in official documents, elaboration = new commitments. Fidelity constraints keep the draft inside what your analysis actually showed.
::ex
You must produce alt text for 12 report charts (accessibility requirement, Lesson 61). Write the prompt that gets useful drafts, including the inputs you'd supply and the check you'd perform.
~~~text
Prompt: "Draft alt text (max ~25 words each) for these charts. For each
I give: chart type, variables, and the key takeaway. Alt text should
convey the takeaway, not just describe axes. Do not invent numbers —
use only figures I provide.

1. Line chart; weekly flu admissions Oct-Mar; peak of 214 in w/c 5 Jan.
2. Funnel plot; MMR uptake by practice vs list size; 2 practices below
   99.8% limit.
[...etc]"

Check: read each draft against its actual chart — numbers match, takeaway
faithful, nothing invented. (Grounded inputs make this fast; you supplied
every fact, so verification is comparison, not research.)
::key
- Grounded tasks (summarise/translate/document what's pasted) are AI's reliability sweet spot.
- Guard rails in the prompt: flag-don't-guess, don't-add-claims, use-only-my-numbers.
- Delegate the draft-shaped hours; verify concepts by sampling, specifics always; you sign what ships.
#LESSON 8|Failure modes: a field guide to confident wrongness
::p
You've met hallucination in principle; this lesson is the bestiary — the specific ways AI assistance goes wrong in analytical work, each with its tell and its counter. Pattern recognition here is what separates "uses AI safely" from "got lucky so far".

**The invented specific.** Function arguments, package names, ICD-10 codes, indicator IDs, regulation clauses — anywhere the answer is a precise token, plausibility and truth part company. *Tell:* the detail is load-bearing and you can't recall seeing it elsewhere. *Counter:* primary source — `?func`, CRAN, the official classification. Five seconds each.

**The obsolete answer.** Training data has a cutoff; the model doesn't reliably know what changed after it — new package versions, reorganised institutions, updated guidance. It answers from a world that may have moved. *Tell:* questions containing "current", "latest", or anything post-dating the tool's knowledge. *Counter:* live sources for live facts; use AI to interpret documents you fetch, not to be the document.

**The premise swallow.** Ask "why does X cause Y?" and the model explains — even when X doesn't cause Y. It optimises for answering your question, not auditing it. *Tell:* you embedded a claim in the question. *Counter:* ask neutrally ("what's the evidence on X and Y?") or explicitly invite challenge ("first: is the premise sound?").

**The sycophancy slide.** Push back — "are you sure? I think it's the other way" — and models often fold, apologising and adopting *your* error. Agreement under pressure is not evidence; it's the disposition again. *Tell:* it reversed because you frowned, not because either of you produced a new fact. *Counter:* resolve disagreements with authoritative sources or a decisive test, never with a second round of vibes. (And notice the mirror-image trap: it folding doesn't mean you were right, either.)

**The 90% solution.** Generated code that runs, produces sensible-looking output, and is subtly wrong — the closed interval that double-counts boundary days, `n()` where `n_distinct()` was meant, a `left_join` that should have been inner. Nothing errors; the number is just wrong. *Tell:* none visible — that's the point. *Counter:* everything Modules 2–7 built: hand-check a case, reconcile totals, test boundary dates, review the definition line by line. This failure mode is why those habits exist.

**The confident tone, everywhere.** Not a separate failure but the amplifier on all of them: fluency is constant while accuracy varies, so your calibration must come from verification frequency, never from prose style.

Assemble the counters and you get the **verification ladder** — match the check to the stakes:
::table
Stakes|Verification owed
Throwaway exploration|Run it; eyeball the result
Analysis you'll rely on|Run + spot-check cases + reconcile counts against a simpler query
Feeds a decision or publication|All the above + tests in the suite + independent human review
Clinical/legal/regulatory detail|Primary source verification, full stop — AI drafts nothing load-bearing alone
::p
The economics stay excellent: a 30-second draft plus five minutes of laddered checking still demolishes an hour of unaided work. The failure isn't using AI; it's charging the checking time to nobody.
::quiz
Q You challenge the AI's (correct) answer and it apologises and switches to your (wrong) one. The lesson?
- The model was wrong initially
* Model agreement tracks social pressure, not truth — settle disputed facts with sources or tests, not with whoever pushed last
- Apologies indicate reliability
- Asking twice improves accuracy
! Sycophancy means capitulation carries no information. The tie-breaker is external: documentation, a run, a primary source.
::quiz
Q Which failure mode is MOST dangerous in NHS analytical work, and why?
- The invented package — it errors immediately
* The 90% solution — it runs, looks right, and ships a wrong number with no visible symptom
- The obsolete answer — dates give it away
- The premise swallow — rereading the question reveals it
! Loud failures self-announce. The silent almost-right query survives every glance and dies only under spot-checks, reconciliation and review — which is why those are non-negotiable for anything that feeds decisions.
::ex
Diagnose the failure mode in each vignette and name the counter: (a) AI: "use fingertipsR::fetch_indicator_v2()" — no such function; (b) you ask "why did the 2025 data-flow rules ban practice-level extracts?" and get a fluent explanation, but no such ban exists; (c) an AI-drafted query for "admissions in June" uses BETWEEN '2026-06-01' AND '2026-06-30' on a datetime column; (d) AI names the "current" UKHSA chief statistician from two years ago.
~~~text
(a) Invented specific → check package docs/CRAN; the real call is
    fingertips_data() — 5-second lookup.
(b) Premise swallow → the question asserted the ban; ask neutrally or
    demand a source; verify in actual guidance before repeating it.
(c) 90% solution → runs fine, silently drops June 30 admissions with
    times after midnight; counter = boundary test rows + half-open
    dates (>= '2026-06-01' AND < '2026-07-01') per Lesson 21.
(d) Obsolete answer → post-cutoff fact; check the live UKHSA site;
    AI interprets sources, it isn't one.
::key
- Name the modes: invented specific, obsolete answer, premise swallow, sycophancy, 90% solution — fluent tone amplifies all.
- Disagreements resolve by source or test, never by who pressed harder.
- Scale verification to stakes (the ladder); checking time is part of the job, budget it.
#LESSON 8|Judgement: the analyst's half of the partnership
::p
Close the module by naming what you bring, because using AI well requires knowing precisely what it cannot do — not as comfort, but as a job description.

**Question formulation.** Before any query exists, someone turned "the board is worried about winter" into *"emergency respiratory admissions among over-65s, by week and LA, versus the previous two winters, age-standardised"*. That translation — from concern to answerable question — draws on context no prompt contains: what the board can act on, what data actually exists, what was tried last year, which definition the medical director will accept. Module 1's question-first discipline is now your scarcest skill. AI answers questions; deciding *which question* is you.

**Definition authority.** "Readmission", "frequent attender", "vaccinated", "excess" — every metric in this course hid a contestable choice, and you learned to make those choices deliberately (30 days from *discharge*; *emergency* only; dose 2 by which birthday?). AI will happily implement any definition and invent one if you don't supply it. The signature on the definition is yours, and defending it in the governance meeting cannot be delegated.

**Plausibility sense.** By now a rate of 840 per 1,000, a mean LOS of 45 days, or a practice with 200% uptake should produce a physical sensation. That reflex — compiled from denominators, seasonality, data-quality scars and knowing that Kingsbay is small so its rates swing — is your fastest error detector and the one no tool replicates, because it lives in accumulated contact with *your* system's data.

**Ethical and contextual weight.** Should small numbers be shown to this audience? Is comparing these two trusts fair given case mix? Will publishing this map stigmatise an estate? Does the request even serve patients? These aren't technical questions with technical answers; they're judgement calls made by someone accountable, inside relationships and obligations a language model doesn't have. Lesson 7's Caldicott principles and Lesson 67's disclosure control were always judgement wearing procedure's clothes.

**Accountability itself.** When the number is challenged — and good numbers get challenged — someone stands behind it: explains the method, owns the caveat, corrects the record if needed. That someone has a name and a professional reputation, and the tools they used are as relevant to the audience as the brand of their calculator.

So the working partnership, stated plainly: **you own questions, definitions, verification, context and the signature; AI accelerates drafting, translation, recall-shaped labour and the hunt for your blind spots.** Analysts who thrive treat the model as a tireless junior with brilliant recall, no judgement and no stake — delegating accordingly, checking accordingly, and spending the reclaimed hours on exactly the human work above. Analysts who struggle either refuse the tool (and lose the hours) or abdicate to it (and eventually ship the 90% solution with their name on it).

The rest of this course — careers, then capstones — assumes the partnership. The capstones in Module 10 will explicitly ask you to work both ways: some tasks AI-assisted with documented verification, some unaided, so you can feel where each mode earns its keep. That felt sense, more than any rule, is what "AI-literate analyst" means on a CV.
::quiz
Q A director asks for "something on winter pressures". The step AI fundamentally cannot do for you is…
- Writing the SQL once specified
* Converting the vague concern into the specific, answerable, actionable question — using organisational context, data knowledge and knowing what the audience can act on
- Formatting the chart
- Summarising the final report
! Question formulation runs on context outside any prompt: politics, history, data availability, actionability. It's the analyst's first and least automatable act.
::quiz
Q The healthiest description of an AI-literate analyst's stance?
- Trust the tool; it's smarter than us
- Refuse the tool; it hallucinates
* Delegate drafts and recall to it, keep questions, definitions, verification and accountability — like managing a brilliant junior with no judgement
- Use it only for emails
! Both extremes lose: refusal forfeits the hours, abdication ships unowned errors. The professional posture is managed delegation with retained judgement.
::ex
For each, write one sentence assigning it to YOU or AI-with-your-verification, with the reason: (a) choosing whether readmissions count from admission or discharge date; (b) drafting the Quarto prose around finished tables; (c) deciding whether a practice-level funnel plot is fair to publish given two tiny practices; (d) generating five candidate edge-case tests for your FY function; (e) signing off the final report.
~~~text
(a) YOU — a definition with clinical/policy meaning; AI implements
    whichever you choose but cannot own the choice.
(b) AI draft, your verification — grounded prose around numbers you
    supply; you check fidelity and sign.
(c) YOU — fairness, disclosure and stigma are contextual ethical calls;
    Lesson 78's caveats inform it, but the judgement is accountable.
(d) AI generate, you select — enumeration is its strength; deciding
    which cases matter (31 March!) is yours.
(e) YOU — always. The signature is the one deliverable that never
    delegates.
::key
- Your monopoly: question formulation, definitions, plausibility sense, ethics/context, accountability.
- AI's lane: drafts, translation, recall-shaped work, adversarial enumeration — under your verification.
- Manage it like a brilliant junior with no judgement; the capstones will make you practise both modes.
#LESSON 9|The NHS data landscape: what flows where
::p
Interviews and first weeks in post reward one thing disproportionately: knowing **what the national datasets are** and roughly how data moves. Module 1 sketched this; here's the working map you should be able to reproduce on a whiteboard.

The spine of it: care happens → providers record it in local systems → standardised extracts flow to national collections → analysts query local warehouses and national tools built on them.
::table
Dataset|What it covers|Analyst's angle
SUS (Secondary Uses Service)|Hospital activity submitted by providers: admitted care, outpatients, A&E|The raw feed; payment-grade; what your trust warehouse ingests
HES (Hospital Episode Statistics)|Cleaned, curated version of hospital data for analysis|Research/benchmarking-grade national comparisons
ECDS|Emergency Care Data Set — richer A&E detail (chief complaint, acuity)|Urgent-care analysis; injury surveillance
MHSDS|Mental Health Services Data Set|Community & inpatient mental health flows
CSDS|Community Services Data Set|Health visiting, community nursing, therapies
SGSS|UKHSA's lab surveillance system|Infectious disease: organisms, specimens, notifications
ODS|Organisation Data Service|The reference list of NHS orgs & codes (your practice_code's home)
::p
Two structural ideas make all of these legible. First, **episodes vs spells** (Lesson 36): admitted-care data arrives as consultant episodes that analysts assemble into hospital spells — half of all "why is my count different?" mysteries live here. Second, **the terminology stack** (Lesson 6): SNOMED CT for recording care, ICD-10 for classifying diagnoses, OPCS-4 for procedures — plus the identifiers (NHS number for people, ODS codes for organisations, LSOA for places) that make joining possible.

The umpire for all of it is the **NHS Data Model and Dictionary** (via digital.nhs.uk) — the official definition of every field: what "admission method" code 21 means, how "finished consultant episode" is defined. Professional reflex: when two systems disagree or a field is ambiguous, the Dictionary is where you check before you argue. Knowing it exists and using it puts you ahead of a surprising share of the field.

Depth is not required on day one — nobody memorises MHSDS tables for fun. The interview-ready standard is: name the major collections, say what each covers in a sentence, explain episode/spell and the coding stack, and know where definitions live. That's this lesson, retold in your own words.
::quiz
Q Your trust's admission counts differ from a published HES figure. Before suspecting an error, the FIRST structural explanation to consider?
- Someone hacked the warehouse
* Definitional differences — episodes vs spells, different inclusion rules, different time basis; check definitions in the Data Dictionary
- HES is always wrong
- Your SQL is fine so the data must be
! National curated datasets and local extracts differ by construction: cleaning rules, episode/spell grain, provider vs residence basis. Reconcile definitions first; most "discrepancies" dissolve there.
::quiz
Q A colleague asks what ODS is. The one-sentence answer:
- A dashboard for outbreak data
* The Organisation Data Service — the national reference list of NHS organisations and their codes
- The old name for SNOMED
- A type of SQL join
! ODS codes (like your GP practice_code) are the organisational identifiers that make cross-system joining possible — reference data, unglamorous and essential.
::ex
Whiteboard drill: without looking back, write the one-line description of SUS, ECDS, SGSS, and the Data Dictionary — then check yourself against the table and note which you fumbled for revision.
~~~text
SUS: the national submission route for hospital activity data
     (admissions, outpatients, A&E) — payment-grade raw feed.
ECDS: the emergency care dataset — richer A&E detail like chief
     complaint and acuity.
SGSS: UKHSA's laboratory surveillance system for infectious disease
     results and notifications.
Data Dictionary: the official definition of every NHS data field —
     where you settle "what does this code actually mean".
(Fumbled any? That one goes on tomorrow's revision list.)
::key
- Care → provider systems → national collections (SUS/ECDS/MHSDS/CSDS/SGSS) → warehouses & curated sets (HES).
- Episodes vs spells and the SNOMED/ICD-10/OPCS-4 stack explain most cross-system confusion.
- The NHS Data Model and Dictionary is the umpire; checking it is a professional reflex.
#LESSON 9|Open data: ONS, UKHSA and the art of the free denominator
::p
A huge share of public health questions can be answered — or at least benchmarked — without touching a single patient record, because the UK publishes extraordinary open data. Knowing this landscape makes you productive before your smartcard arrives, keeps your portfolio legal, and supplies the denominators everything in Module 6 depended on.

**ONS (ons.gov.uk)** is the census and statistics mothership: mid-year **population estimates** by age/sex/geography (your rate denominators, Lesson 37), deaths and mortality series, life expectancy, census tables, and the **Open Geography Portal** boundaries you mapped with in Lesson 63. Population estimates by LSOA × age band × sex is the single most reused file in public health analytics — learn its shape.

**UKHSA and DHSC** publish surveillance and indicator data, with the crown jewel being **Fingertips** — hundreds of public health indicators (smoking, obesity, immunisation, screening, mortality) computed consistently for every LA, ICB and practice, with trends, confidence intervals, and comparisons built in. Two career-relevant facts: decision-makers already use it, so quoting it fluently earns trust; and it has an R package:
::code|r|fingertipsR: indicators straight into a tibble
# install.packages("fingertipsR")
library(fingertipsR)

# discover what exists
profs <- profiles()                 # indicator profiles
inds  <- indicators()               # every indicator + ID

# pull one indicator for all local authorities
smoking <- fingertips_data(IndicatorID = 92443,
                           AreaTypeID = 502)   # counties/UAs
glimpse(smoking)
::p
(IDs change and grow — the discovery functions are the durable skill, not memorised numbers.) A Fingertips pull piped into your Lesson 58-65 charting skills is a legitimate, shareable analysis of *real* data with zero governance risk — portfolio gold, as the portfolio lesson will insist.

**NHS England statistics** (statistics under england.nhs.uk) round out the set: waiting times, A&E performance, workforce, vaccination programmes — the operational numbers newspapers quote, downloadable as spreadsheets your Module 4 skills can tame.

Working habits for open data: automate the download in your script (a `download.file()` or direct `read_csv(url)` beats "I clicked and saved it somewhere" for reproducibility — Lesson 86); record the retrieval date and exact source in the README; expect format drift (this year's spreadsheet adds a header row — your pipeline's assertions from Lesson 87 will catch it); and read the accompanying methodology notes, because open data has definitions too — an ONS "death involving X" and "death due to X" differ, and the difference has burned real headlines.
::quiz
Q You need age-band denominators for LA-level rates. The canonical open source is…
- Your trust's PAS
* ONS mid-year population estimates
- Fingertips comments section
- The GP practice website
! ONS mid-year estimates by geography/age/sex are THE standard denominator for population rates — free, documented, and what published indicators themselves use.
::quiz
Q The durable skill with fingertipsR is…
- Memorising indicator ID 92443
* Using profiles()/indicators() to discover current indicators, then fingertips_data() to pull them reproducibly in-script
- Screenshotting the website
- Editing the package source
! IDs and indicator lists evolve; discovery-then-pull inside a script is reproducible against a moving catalogue. Click-and-save is neither.
::ex
Sketch the script skeleton for a reproducible open-data pull: fetch an indicator via fingertipsR for all LAs in a region, record provenance, and assert basic sanity before saving to outputs/.
~~~r
library(fingertipsR); library(tidyverse); library(here)

pulled_at <- Sys.time()
dat <- fingertips_data(IndicatorID = 92443, AreaTypeID = 502) |>
  filter(ParentName == "South East region")     # adjust to taste

stopifnot(nrow(dat) > 0,
          all(c("AreaName", "Value", "Timeperiod") %in% names(dat)))

write_csv(dat, here("outputs", "smoking_by_la.csv"))
writeLines(c(paste("Indicator 92443 pulled", pulled_at),
             "Source: Fingertips via fingertipsR"),
           here("outputs", "smoking_by_la_PROVENANCE.txt"))
::key
- ONS = populations, deaths, boundaries; Fingertips = computed PH indicators (fingertipsR for R); NHSE = operational stats.
- Open data + your R skills = legal, shareable, portfolio-ready analysis.
- Script the download, record provenance, assert sanity, read the methodology notes.
#LESSON 9|Communities, competencies and the shape of the profession
::p
Health analytics has an unusually generous professional ecosystem — free, welcoming, and disproportionately valuable to newcomers. Plugging in early is a career accelerant; here's the circuit diagram.

**NHS-R Community** (nhsrcommunity.com, github.com/nhs-r-community) is the beating heart of R in the NHS: free training, webinars, an annual conference, active discussion, and open-source packages you've already met (NHSRplotthedots from Lesson 77). Everything is volunteer-built and beginner-tolerant; lurking is fine, and answering one question a month later is how members are made. A sibling **NHS Python community** exists for when your second language calls.

**AphA — the Association of Professional Healthcare Analysts** — is the professional body: branch events, mentoring, and a **competency framework** that names what analysts at each level should know. Read the framework even if you never join: it converts "get better at analysis" into a checklist of specific, assessable skills — and mapping this course against it (you'll find substantial coverage) tells you precisely what to learn next. Related: the government **DDaT capability framework** describes analytical roles across the civil service, useful for UKHSA/DHSC-flavoured careers.

**The Goldacre Review** (2022, *Better, Broader, Safer*) is the strategy document worth actually reading: it argued for RAP as the norm, open code, proper analyst careers and training in the NHS — and it's why several things in this course (Git, RAP, publishing code) moved from "nice" to "expected". Citing it accurately in an interview signals you understand where the profession is heading. **AnalystX / FutureNHS** hosts cross-system communities and resources on the NHS collaboration platform once you have an NHS email.

Conduct in these spaces is a skill of its own: ask questions the way Lesson 90 taught you to prompt (context, minimal example, what you tried — humans appreciate good prompts too); share fictional-data examples, never work data (Lesson 92's rules apply to Slack as much as to chatbots); and contribute at whatever scale fits — a typo fix to an NHS-R package README is a genuine open-source contribution with your name on it, and precisely the kind of thing the portfolio lesson monetises.

The realistic promise: none of this replaces competence, but community is where competence gets noticed — jobs are advertised, collaborators found, and reputations quietly built by people who show up helpfully over time.
::quiz
Q The AphA competency framework's practical use for a self-taught analyst is…
- It's required reading for legal reasons
* Turning vague ambition into a specific skills checklist you can assess yourself against and plan learning from
- It contains SQL tutorials
- Membership guarantees interviews
! Frameworks operationalise "get better": named skills at named levels. Self-assessment against one produces your actual curriculum — and interview-ready language about your development.
::quiz
Q Why does the Goldacre Review matter in a Band 5 interview?
- It's a trick question; it doesn't
* It's the strategic case for RAP, open code and analyst careers — showing you know it signals you understand the profession's direction, not just its tools
- Quoting it is mandatory
- It lists approved software
! "Better, Broader, Safer" reframed NHS analytics policy. Connecting your Git/RAP habits to it turns "I know commands" into "I understand why this matters here".
::ex
Draft your 90-day community plan: one thing to join, one thing to attend, one thing to contribute, each with a concrete first action you could take this week.
~~~text
Join: NHS-R Community — action: subscribe to the newsletter and join
      the Slack/discussion space today; read, don't post, for week one.
Attend: one NHS-R webinar this month — action: check the events page
      now and calendar the next beginner-friendly session.
Contribute: one small open-source touch within 90 days — action:
      while studying, note any unclear README sentence in an NHS-R
      package; when found, open an issue or a one-line PR fixing it.
(Small, named, dated — plans without first actions are moods.)
::key
- NHS-R = free training/community/packages; AphA = professional body + competency framework; Goldacre = the strategy to cite.
- Ask well, share only fictional data, contribute at any scale.
- Community converts competence into visibility; show up helpfully, repeatedly.
#LESSON 9|Building a portfolio that proves it
::p
"Self-taught" is a claim; a portfolio is evidence. For career-changers and graduates without NHS experience, a small public body of work is the single strongest differentiator — it lets an interviewer *watch you think* before they've met you. The good news: everything required is already in your hands.

**The rules of admissible evidence.** Only open data (Lesson 97) or fictional data (this course's playground, or data you synthesise). Never anything from a workplace, however anonymised you believe it is — the portfolio that breaches IG proves the opposite of what you intended. State the data source and its licence in every README.

**What three good projects look like** (three finished beats ten abandoned):
- *An open-data analysis with a question.* Not "I explored smoking data" but "Has the deprivation gap in smoking prevalence narrowed since 2015?" — Fingertips pull, tidy pipeline, two or three publication-quality charts (Lesson 61 styling), honest caveats. Question-first structure (Lesson 8) is itself being assessed.
- *A capstone from Module 10*, upgraded to full RAP dress: project structure, here(), Git history with meaningful commits, README with how-to-run, tests. The commit log is quietly examined — it shows working rhythm, not just results.
- *One small tool or contribution*: a documented function (your FY converter), a Quarto template, or that NHS-R issue/PR from last lesson. Signals you build for others, not just for marks.

**Presentation multiplies substance.** Each repo: a README that opens with the question and a headline chart (recruiters give you ninety seconds; spend them wisely), reproducible structure, and *rendered output* — a Quarto HTML via GitHub Pages lets non-cloners see the goods. Pin your best three on your GitHub profile; add a profile README with two sentences about who you are and what you're learning. Code style counts: consistent naming, comments that explain *why* — reviewers read three files, then extrapolate.

**The AI disclosure question**, answered with Module 8's confidence: it's increasingly normal to note "AI-assisted drafting; all logic verified by tests X and manual reconciliation Y" in a README. Handled that way, it reads as professional maturity — you're demonstrating the workflow employers actually want, verification included. What reads badly is unexplained AI boilerplate you can't discuss in interview; never ship code you couldn't whiteboard.

Finally, aim the portfolio: an NHS trust panel lights up at SPC charts and RAP structure; a UKHSA-flavoured panel at surveillance epi curves and standardised rates. One shared core, one project angled at the door you're actually knocking on.
::quiz
Q The strongest single artefact in an entry-level health-analytics portfolio is…
- A list of completed courses
* A finished, question-driven analysis on open/fictional data with reproducible code, clear README and honest caveats
- Screenshots of dashboards from a previous job
- 15 half-started repositories
! Finished + reproducible + question-first = watchable thinking. Workplace screenshots are an IG breach in a trench coat; course lists are claims, not evidence.
::quiz
Q Best handling of AI assistance in portfolio work?
- Hide it — admitting it looks weak
* Disclose it briefly alongside the verification you performed; be ready to explain any line in interview
- Use AI for everything and say so proudly with no checks
- Avoid AI entirely so the question can't arise
! The employable skill IS the verified-AI workflow. Disclosure + evidence of checking demonstrates it; concealment risks the one interview question you can't survive: "walk me through this function you didn't write."
::ex
Write the opening block of the README for your best project — question, data+licence, one-line method, headline finding, how to run — in under 120 words.
~~~text
# Has the deprivation gap in adult smoking narrowed? (England, 2015-2024)

Question: Is the absolute gap in smoking prevalence between the most
and least deprived decile LAs closing?

Data: Fingertips indicator (smoking prevalence, adults), pulled via
fingertipsR on 2026-07-03. Open Government Licence.

Method: tidyverse pipeline; decile-grouped trends; gap series with CIs.

Headline: the gap narrowed ~2.9 percentage points but remains ~9pp;
progress stalled after 2021 (see chart below).

Run: open .Rproj, `renv::restore()`, `quarto render analysis.qmd`.
Outputs regenerate into /outputs. AI-assisted drafting; logic verified
via tests/ and manual spot-checks documented in NOTES.md.
::key
- Portfolio = evidence: 3 finished, question-driven, reproducible projects on open/fictional data only.
- README with question + headline chart; rendered HTML; meaningful commit history; pinned repos.
- Disclose AI use with its verification; never ship code you can't explain aloud.
#LESSON 9|Getting hired: applications, interviews and the technical test
::p
The market context, plainly: NHS and UKHSA analytical posts are advertised constantly (trusts, ICBs, local authorities, national bodies), entry typically at **Band 4–5** (analyst/officer) with **Band 6** for demonstrated experience — and this course's content maps squarely onto Band 5 person specifications: SQL, R, communication, IG awareness, reproducibility. Roles hide under many titles: performance analyst, intelligence analyst, public health information officer, data officer — search by skill words, not titles.

**The application** in the NHS world is usually a *supporting statement* scored against the person specification, criterion by criterion. The technique is unglamorous and decisive: mirror their structure. For each essential criterion, one short paragraph: name the criterion, evidence it with a specific example (your capstones and portfolio now exist precisely for this), state the result. "Experience of SQL" ⇒ "Built a 30-day readmission pipeline in SQL (CTEs, window functions) on a simulated hospital dataset; validated against hand-checked cases; code at github.com/…". Generic enthusiasm scores zero; addressed evidence scores.

**Interviews** blend values-based questions ("tell us about a time you handled conflicting priorities") with technical probes. Prepare STAR stories (Situation-Task-Action-Result) from your projects — yes, self-directed projects count as evidence of planning, problem-solving and delivery. Technical probes at this level are rarely trick questions; they're the concepts this course drilled, asked plainly:
::table
They ask|You have
"Difference between INNER and LEFT JOIN?"|Lesson 25-26, plus the anti-join party trick
"How would you check data quality in a new extract?"|Lesson 39's checklist, recited with love
"A director wants patient-level data emailed — what do you do?"|Lessons 7 & 92: purpose, minimisation, proper channels, IG advice
"This month's figure jumped 15% — your response?"|Lesson 77: control limits before conclusions
"How do you make analysis reproducible?"|Module 7, ideally with the word RAP and a Goldacre nod
::p
When you don't know, say so *and show the finding reflex*: "I haven't used that dataset, but I'd start with the Data Dictionary definition and a data-quality profile" beats bluffing by a mile — they're hiring your approach, not your cache.

**Technical tests** — increasingly common — are usually a small dataset plus questions (write a query, spot the issue, chart a trend), sometimes live, sometimes take-home. Your edge is process made visible: comment assumptions, check row counts aloud, state definitional choices ("I'll treat FY as April–March; flagging that 31 March boundary"), sanity-check the result. Examiners reward the analyst who catches their planted duplicate more than the one with the fanciest window function. If AI tools are permitted, use them exactly as Module 8 taught — and narrate the verification; if not permitted, you've practised unaided throughout this course by design.

Ask *them* questions that reveal you know the terrain: "Is analysis version-controlled — is there a RAP ambition here?", "What does QA look like before numbers reach the board?", "R, Python, or both on the team?" The answers tell you about the job; the questions tell them about you.
::quiz
Q An NHS supporting statement is strongest when it…
- Expresses passion for healthcare at length
* Addresses each person-specification criterion explicitly with a specific evidenced example and outcome
- Lists every technology ever touched
- Stays under 100 words to respect their time
! Statements are scored against the spec. Mirroring it with concrete evidence (your portfolio projects) is how points are awarded; passion without evidence scores like weather.
::quiz
Q In a technical test you meet an unfamiliar dataset quirk. The response examiners reward:
- Silence until solved perfectly
* Narrated process: state the assumption, check the definition/data, flag the uncertainty, proceed transparently
- Confident bluffing
- Refusing the question as unfair
! Tests audition your working method. Visible checking, stated assumptions and honest flags ARE the assessed skill — the planted data-quality trap is usually the real question.
::ex
Draft your STAR answer (≤150 words) to "Tell us about a time you found and fixed an error in analysis" using a genuine moment from this course (everyone has one — a fan-out join, an FY boundary, a NULL surprise).
~~~text
S: Building my readmissions capstone, my monthly counts looked ~40% too
   high versus a simple total.
T: Find the discrepancy before trusting any output.
A: I reconciled grain first (Lesson 39 habit): COUNT(*) vs
   COUNT(DISTINCT admission_id) showed duplication after joining a
   practice lookup. count() on the lookup key revealed duplicate
   practice_code rows — a fan-out join. I deduplicated the lookup,
   added an assertion (row count unchanged by the join) so it can't
   silently recur, and documented the fix in the commit message.
R: Counts reconciled exactly; the assertion later caught the same issue
   when I refreshed the lookup. I now check key uniqueness before every
   join — cheap insurance, permanently adopted.
::key
- Search by skills not titles; Band 4-5 entry; supporting statements mirror the person spec with evidenced examples.
- Interviews test course-core concepts + values; STAR stories from your own projects; "here's how I'd find out" beats bluffing.
- Technical tests score visible process: assumptions, checks, definitions, sanity — narrate them.
#LESSON 9|Your 12-week plan: from course to career
::p
A course ends; a practice continues. This closing lesson turns everything behind you into a schedule you can actually keep — because the analysts who land roles are rarely the ones who studied hardest for a fortnight, but the ones who compounded modest effort for months.

**The honest audit first.** You cannot schedule what you haven't measured. Spend one session self-assessing against three checklists: this course's module map (which lessons would you fail if quizzed cold today?), the AphA competency framework (Lesson 98), and three real job adverts at your target band (their essential criteria are the market's syllabus). The gaps — not your favourite topics — set the plan. Expect the classics: window functions rusty, standardisation formula fuzzy, Git branching theoretical.

**The 12-week shape** — adjust ruthlessly to your life, keep the proportions:
::table
Weeks|Focus|Deliverable
1-2|Consolidate: rework weak-spot lessons; daily playground reps; rebuild spaced-repetition deck (Lesson 11)|Self-audit doc; 20-card deck in rotation
3-5|Capstone A + B properly (Module 10), full RAP dress, Git throughout|Two pinned portfolio repos
6-7|Capstone C or D + fingertipsR open-data project of YOUR question|Third repo with rendered Quarto output
8|Portfolio polish week: READMEs, Pages, profile; community contribution (Lesson 98)|Reviewable-by-a-stranger portfolio
9-10|Applications: statement per real advert, mirrored to spec; mock technical test under time|3+ tailored applications sent
11-12|Interview prep: STAR bank, concept drills aloud, questions for them; keep applying|Interview-ready; pipeline running
::p
**The weekly rhythm that survives real life:** three focused sessions beat daily heroics — two build sessions (project work, where learning consolidates) and one maintenance session (spaced-repetition cards, one community touch, one job-board scan). Protect the build sessions on your calendar like shifts. The generation-effect rule from Lesson 11 still governs: reading about window functions is anaesthetic; writing one from a blank playground is the rep that counts.

**Plateaus and dips are features.** Around week 4-6 the novelty is gone and the finish line isn't visible — this is where most self-teaching quietly dies. The countermeasures are structural, not motivational: deliverables with dates (a repo shipped is progress you can see), a study partner or community check-in (borrowed accountability), and permission to shrink a bad week to one session rather than zero. Momentum, not intensity.

**And afterwards** — because week 12 is a waypoint: the field moves (new packages, new datasets, the AI tooling of Module 8 evolving fastest of all), so the professional habit is a steady trickle — an NHS-R webinar a month, release notes skimmed, one new technique tried per quarter on a toy problem before it's needed on a real one. You now possess the rarer thing than knowledge: a proven method for acquiring it. That method — question first, evidence over vibes, verify everything, ship reproducibly — is the actual qualification this course awards.

Go build something. The playground's still open.
::quiz
Q Week 5, motivation gone, plan slipping. The evidence-based rescue is…
- Wait for motivation to return
* Structural fixes: shrink to a minimum viable session, anchor to a dated deliverable, and add external accountability (partner/community)
- Restart the course from Lesson 1
- Double the hours to catch up
! Motivation follows momentum, not vice versa. Smaller-but-nonzero sessions, visible deliverables and borrowed accountability are what get plans through the trough — heroic doubling produces week-6 burnout.
::quiz
Q The self-audit ranks topics by…
- How enjoyable they are to revise
* Gap severity against the module map, competency framework and real job adverts
- Alphabetical order
- Whatever the last lesson covered
! Plans built on favourite topics feel productive and change nothing. The market's syllabus (adverts) and honest cold-quiz failures define where the next hour actually pays.
::ex
Write YOUR week-1 plan right now: three calendar-ready sessions (day, duration, exact task), one deliverable for Friday, and the single lesson number you most need to rework first.
~~~text
(Model — replace with yours:)
Mon 19:00-20:30 — Rework Lesson 31-33 (window functions): re-do all
  three exercises from blank playground, no peeking until attempted.

#LESSON 10|Capstone A: the readmissions pipeline (SQL)
::p
Time to graduate from exercises to *work*. Capstone A is a pure-SQL build, done entirely in the playground, producing the analysis every acute trust runs: **emergency readmissions within 30 days**. You built the pieces in Module 3; now you assemble them, alone, to a specification — because real briefs arrive as prose, not as half-written queries.

**The brief** (as a service manager would send it):
::note|info|Commission
"For financial year 2025/26, what is our 30-day emergency readmission rate? I need the headline rate, the monthly trend, and which specialties drive it. And please make sure people who died aren't counted as 'not readmitted' — the last report got grief for that."
::p
**Definitions you must implement** (and in real life, would confirm in writing): an *index admission* is any completed emergency admission discharged in FY 2025/26; a *readmission* is a subsequent **emergency** admission whose admission_date falls within 30 days **after the index discharge_date** (1–30 days; a same-day return counts, an admission on day 31 doesn't); each index admission is assessed independently (an admission can be both a readmission and a later index); *deaths*: exclude index admissions where the patient died within 30 days of discharge without readmission — they had no opportunity to return (the deaths table is your friend); report the rate as readmitted-index / eligible-index.

**Build order** (the professional decomposition — resist writing one giant query first):
- Stage 1 — cohort CTE: completed FY 2025/26 emergency discharges with admission_id, patient_id, discharge_date. *Check:* row count; a few hand-verified members.
- Stage 2 — readmission flag: for each index row, does ANY qualifying later admission exist? (Correlated EXISTS, or a self-join + window — your choice; Lesson 33's gap logic and Lesson 38's pattern both work.) *Check:* pick two flagged and two unflagged patients; verify by eye against their raw admissions.
- Stage 3 — mortality exclusion: LEFT JOIN deaths; exclude the died-within-30-days-unreadmitted. *Check:* how many rows did this remove? Plausible?
- Stage 4 — outputs: headline rate; rate by discharge month (spine the months!); rate by specialty of the index admission, sorted, with an n column so small denominators are visible.
::code|sqltry|Starter scaffold (finish it yourself first!)
WITH cohort AS (
  SELECT admission_id, patient_id, discharge_date, specialty
  FROM admissions
  WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'
    AND discharge_date >= '2025-04-01'
    AND discharge_date <  '2026-04-01'
)
SELECT COUNT(*) AS index_admissions FROM cohort;
::p
**Quality bar** (this is the marked rubric — self-assess honestly): correct half-open date logic everywhere; readmission window measured from *discharge*, emergency-only, 1–30 days inclusive; deaths handled per spec; every stage checked with a written-down verification (comment them into your SQL); monthly output has no missing months; specialty output includes denominators. Stretch goals: exclude index admissions in the final 30 days of the FY *or* justify why you didn't (the follow-up-window problem — real reports must choose); compute day-to-readmission distribution (LAG/julianday) and its median.
::quiz
Q A patient's index discharge is 2026-03-20; they're readmitted (emergency) on 2026-04-05. Under this brief's definitions, the index admission is…
- Not in the cohort — the readmission is outside the FY
* In the cohort and counted as readmitted — the INDEX discharge sets cohort membership; the 30-day window may cross the FY boundary
- Excluded as a death
- Counted twice
! Cohort membership keys on index discharge in-FY; the outcome window follows the patient wherever it lands. (The stretch goal asks whether late-March indexes get a FAIR window — a different, deliberate decision.)
::quiz
Q Why must the died-without-readmission group be EXCLUDED rather than counted as "not readmitted"?
- Small numbers rules
* They had no opportunity to be readmitted — counting them as successes deflates the rate and misrepresents the pathway
- Deaths are confidential
- SQL cannot join to deaths
! Denominators must contain only those at risk of the outcome. Immortal-to-the-outcome members flatter the metric — exactly the grief the manager remembers.
::ex
Complete Stage 2: extend the scaffold so each cohort row gains readmitted = 1/0 using a correlated EXISTS against admissions (emergency, admission_date > discharge_date, within 30 days via julianday). Then produce the headline rate.
~~~sql
WITH cohort AS (
  SELECT admission_id, patient_id, discharge_date
  FROM admissions
  WHERE admission_method = 'Emergency'
    AND discharge_date >= '2025-04-01'
    AND discharge_date <  '2026-04-01'
),
flagged AS (
  SELECT c.*,
    EXISTS (
      SELECT 1 FROM admissions r
      WHERE r.patient_id = c.patient_id
        AND r.admission_method = 'Emergency'
        AND r.admission_date > c.discharge_date
        AND julianday(r.admission_date) - julianday(c.discharge_date) <= 30
    ) AS readmitted
  FROM cohort c
)
SELECT COUNT(*) AS eligible,
       SUM(readmitted) AS readmitted,
       ROUND(100.0 * SUM(readmitted) / COUNT(*), 1) AS rate_pct
FROM flagged;
-- (Stage 3 next: LEFT JOIN deaths and exclude died<=30d & readmitted=0.)
::key
- Real briefs are prose: extract definitions, confirm them, then build in checked stages.
- Cohort → flag → exclusions → outputs, with a verification comment at every stage.
- Denominators = those at risk; windows from discharge; half-open dates; show your n.
#LESSON 10|Capstone B: vaccination equity (SQL extract + R analysis)
::p
Capstone B is the full relay: **SQL extracts, R analyses** — the everyday shape of professional work — applied to an equity question that public health teams genuinely run: *is MMR uptake lower in more deprived areas, and which practices need support?*

**The brief:** "For children turning 2 during FY 2025/26 (i.e. born 2023-04-01 to 2024-03-31), what proportion had received **2+ MMR doses by their 2nd birthday**? Show the deprivation gradient (IMD decile of the child's LSOA), and give me a fair practice-level comparison — last time someone just ranked percentages and the small practices were furious."

**Part 1 — SQL (playground).** One tidy extract, child-level:
::code|sqltry|The extract to build on
SELECT p.patient_id,
       p.birth_date,
       date(p.birth_date, '+2 years')            AS second_birthday,
       p.practice_code,
       li.imd_decile,
       COALESCE(v.mmr_doses_by_2, 0)             AS mmr_doses_by_2
FROM patients p
JOIN lsoa_imd li ON li.lsoa_code = p.lsoa_code
LEFT JOIN (
  SELECT vx.patient_id, COUNT(*) AS mmr_doses_by_2
  FROM vaccinations vx
  JOIN patients p2 ON p2.patient_id = vx.patient_id
  WHERE vx.vaccine = 'MMR'
    AND vx.date_given < date(p2.birth_date, '+2 years')
  GROUP BY vx.patient_id
) v ON v.patient_id = p.patient_id
WHERE p.birth_date >= '2023-04-01'
  AND p.birth_date <  '2024-04-01';
::p
Study the two Module 2/3 patterns doing the heavy lifting: the **LEFT JOIN to a pre-aggregated subquery** (so zero-dose children survive — an inner join here silently deletes the very children the question is about), and the **derived subquery computing doses *before each child's own* second birthday** (a per-row deadline, not a fixed date). Verify like a professional: total children plausible? Any imd_decile NULLs (unmatched LSOAs — decide and document their fate)? Hand-check one child with 2 doses, one with 1, one with 0.

**Part 2 — R.** Export mentally (in real life: `dbGetQuery`; here, imagine the extract as `kids`) and deliver:
- *Gradient:* uptake (`mean(mmr_doses_by_2 >= 2)`) by IMD decile; a clean ordered bar or point chart, Lesson 61-styled, with counts shown. Is the gradient monotone? Say what you see *and its limits* (ecological, Lesson 74 — deciles describe areas, not families).
- *Fair practice comparison:* a **funnel plot** (Lesson 78) of practice uptake vs eligible n, 95%/99.8% limits around the overall proportion — the direct answer to the furious-small-practices problem. Flag outliers as *conversation-starters*, with the case-mix caveat in the caption.
- *Safe outputs* (Lesson 67): suppress any published cell with 1–4 children; check the decile table and any practice list against secondary-suppression leaks before "sending".
::p
**Quality bar:** zero-dose children demonstrably included (state the check); per-child deadline logic correct (a dose at age 2y+1d must not count — test one); decile chart shows n; funnel limits computed correctly (Lesson 78's formula) and interpreted with restraint; suppression applied and stated; a 150-word manager summary that answers the brief in plain English with one caveat about area-level inference. Stretch: age-standardise… is meaningless here (single cohort) — instead, stretch by adding dose-1-by-1st-birthday as a second metric and commenting on where drop-off concentrates.
::quiz
Q Replacing the LEFT JOIN (to the doses subquery) with an INNER JOIN would…
- Change nothing; the WHERE clause protects it
* Silently drop every zero-dose child, inflating uptake and destroying the equity analysis
- Double-count doses
- Break the date logic only
! Children with no MMR rows vanish under an inner join — and unvaccinated children are the analytical point. COALESCE(0) + LEFT JOIN is the survival kit; the row-count check proves it worked.
::quiz
Q The funnel plot answers the "furious small practices" complaint because…
- It hides small practices
* Its limits widen for small denominators, so a small practice's low percentage is only flagged if it exceeds what chance produces at that size
- It ranks practices more politely
- Everyone falls inside limits by design
! League tables punish smallness with noise; funnels encode expected variation-by-size, separating "genuinely different" from "small and unlucky" — Lesson 78, now deployed diplomatically.
::ex
Write the R for the decile gradient table WITH suppression, ready to publish: uptake % by imd_decile, eligible n, suppressing any decile where eligible n < 5 (unlikely, but the check is the habit), formatted to 1 dp.
~~~r
gradient <- kids |>
  group_by(imd_decile) |>
  summarise(eligible = n(),
            uptake  = mean(mmr_doses_by_2 >= 2)) |>
  mutate(uptake_pct = round(100 * uptake, 1),
         published  = if_else(eligible < 5, "<5 suppressed",
                              paste0(uptake_pct, "% (n=", eligible, ")"))) |>
  arrange(imd_decile)

gradient |> select(imd_decile, published)
# Then: ggplot(gradient, aes(factor(imd_decile), uptake)) + geom_col(...)
# Caption: "Deciles describe areas of residence, not individual families."
::key
- SQL extracts child-level truth (LEFT JOIN + per-row deadline); R turns it into gradient + funnel + safe outputs.
- The zero-dose check and the age-2y-1d dose test ARE the capstone.
- Fair comparison = funnel, not ranking; publish with suppression and an ecological caveat.
#LESSON 10|Capstone C: the weekly surveillance report (Quarto)
::p
Capstone C builds the artefact that runs a thousand Monday mornings: an **automated weekly surveillance report** — the Module 5-7 skills fused into one product whose defining virtue is that *next week costs nothing*.

**The brief:** "A weekly HTML report on respiratory emergency admissions for the winter period: this week's count with a comparison we can trust (not just 'up 12%!'), the season so far against the spine of expected weeks, an age-band breakdown, and a data-quality footer so we stop getting caught by incomplete feeds. It'll go to the system call every Tuesday — non-analysts read it."

**Architecture** (one `.qmd`, parameterised — Lesson 67):
::code|text|surveillance_report.qmd skeleton
---
title: "Weekly respiratory surveillance"
params:
  week_ending: 2026-02-01        # the ONLY thing that changes weekly
format:
  html:
    embed-resources: true
    toc: true
execute: { echo: false, warning: false }
---
::p
**The five sections to build**, each mapping to lessons you own:
- *Headline*, computed inline (Lesson 66): admissions in the week ending `params$week_ending` — with the comparison done properly: not last-week's delta but position against the **SPC control limits** of the preceding stable weeks (Lesson 77 / NHSRplotthedots). "Within expected variation" is a finding; teach your readers that.
- *Season curve:* weekly counts on a complete date spine (Lesson 32's zero-fill — a missing week must plot as 0 or be flagged, never silently skipped), current week highlighted, incomplete-week shading per Lesson 60's honesty rule.
- *Age bands:* this week vs the season's weekly average, small multiples or a dodged bar (Lessons 59/62); bands from the canonical CASE/case_when pattern so they match la_population's.
- *Data-quality footer* (Lesson 39, automated): rows received vs 4-week average; % missing key fields; latest admission_date seen (staleness detector); each with a plain-English verdict ("feed appears complete").
- *The tripwires* (Lesson 87): assertions that **fail the render** if data smells wrong — because a report that publishes garbage confidently is worse than no report:
::code|r|The render-blocking checks
stopifnot(
  max(adm$admission_date) >= params$week_ending - 6,  # feed fresh enough
  nrow(this_week) > 0,                                # week exists at all
  mean(is.na(adm$age_band)) < 0.05                    # coding intact
)
::p
**Fictional data note:** generate a plausible two-winter weekly series in a setup chunk (`rpois` with a seasonal bump — Lesson 60 showed the trick) so the report runs self-contained; structure it exactly as the playground extract would arrive, so swapping in `dbGetQuery()` later is a one-line change. That separation — *data acquisition vs report logic* — is the design habit that makes reports portable to real warehouses.

**Quality bar:** renders start-to-finish with one command; changing `week_ending` re-targets everything (test two different weeks!); every number in prose is inline code, zero typed statistics; SPC framing used for the headline (no naked two-week deltas); incomplete data visibly flagged; checks demonstrably block a sabotaged render (test by feeding it a truncated dataset — seriously, do this); reads sensibly to a non-analyst (the hardest criterion — have a human try). Stretch: parameterise by LA as well and batch-render three reports with `quarto_render` in a loop (Lesson 67's pattern).
::quiz
Q The headline says "within expected variation" instead of "up 12% on last week" because…
- It sounds calmer
* Two-point comparisons promote noise to news; SPC limits define what this process normally produces, so only genuine signals trigger alarm
- Percentages confuse readers
- Last week's data was missing
! Lesson 77's entire argument, operationalised weekly. Reports that cry wolf on common-cause wiggle train readers to ignore the real signal when it comes.
::quiz
Q Why must the render FAIL (not warn) when the feed looks stale or truncated?
- Failures are easier to log
* A confidently published wrong report causes decisions; a loudly absent report causes a phone call — the second is vastly cheaper
- Quarto cannot show warnings
- To punish the data team
! Fail-loud is the automation covenant (Lesson 87). The Tuesday call survives a missing report; trust does not survive a wrong one.
::ex
Write the data-quality footer chunk: a small tibble of check / value / verdict for the three tripwire metrics, rendered with gt, verdicts computed not typed.
~~~r
dq <- tibble(
  check = c("Rows this week vs 4-wk mean",
            "Missing age band",
            "Latest admission seen"),
  value = c(sprintf("%d vs %.0f", nrow(this_week), mean_4wk),
            sprintf("%.1f%%", 100 * mean(is.na(adm$age_band))),
            as.character(max(adm$admission_date))),
  verdict = c(
    if_else(nrow(this_week) > 0.7 * mean_4wk, "OK", "LOW - investigate"),
    if_else(mean(is.na(adm$age_band)) < 0.05, "OK", "DEGRADED"),
    if_else(max(adm$admission_date) >= params$week_ending - 6,
            "Fresh", "STALE - do not circulate")
  ))

gt(dq) |> tab_header(title = "Data quality this week")
::key
- One parameterised .qmd: headline (SPC-framed), spined season curve, age bands, automated DQ footer.
- Inline numbers everywhere; assertions that block bad renders; incomplete weeks visibly flagged.
- Separate data acquisition from report logic — fictional now, dbGetQuery later, same report.
#LESSON 10|Capstone D: the mini-RAP, both hands (and the rubric)
::p
The final capstone is the whole job in miniature — **an end-to-end reproducible pipeline built twice-minded**: some components AI-assisted under Module 8 discipline, some deliberately unaided, with a written reflection comparing the modes. This is the repo you pin first (Lesson 99); it demonstrates not just skills but *judgement about your own workflow* — the rarest line on a CV.

**The brief — choose one question** (or invent one this shaped): "Which GP practices have the highest emergency admission **rates** (per 1,000 list size) among their 65+ patients, and is high use associated with area deprivation?" — or a question of your own against the playground/open data, provided it demands: an extract, a rate with a real denominator, a join across ≥3 tables, one statistical method from Module 6, and a rendered report.

**Required RAP dress** (Lesson 86's baseline, all of it): `.Rproj` + Lesson 83 structure; Git from the first commit with meaningful messages; README a stranger could run from; numbered scripts 01→04 (or targets, if you're feeling gold-tier); `here()` throughout; testthat for at least your two riskiest functions; assertions between stages; outputs/ fully regenerable; suppression on anything "published".

**The two-hands protocol** — the capstone's signature. Split components explicitly:
::table
Unaided (no AI)|AI-assisted (Module 8 discipline)
The core extract query|Adversarial review OF that query (Lesson 91 prompt)
Definitions & the analysis plan|First-draft README and roxygen comments
Verification of everything|Edge-case test generation for your functions
The final interpretation|Refactoring one ugly-but-working pipeline stage
::p
Log as you go (a plain `AILOG.md`): each AI interaction's purpose, what it produced, **what verification you performed, what it got wrong**. Then write the 300-word reflection: where did assistance genuinely accelerate? Where did it mislead or add friction? What would you delegate next time, and what never? Honest specifics ("it invented a `by_group` argument; ?arrange caught it in 10 seconds") are worth ten platitudes — this document is interview ammunition of the highest grade.

**The rubric** — score yourself, harshly, /20:
::table
Criterion|0|1|2
Correctness|Numbers unverified|Spot-checked|Reconciled + boundary-tested, checks documented
Reproducibility|Runs on your machine, maybe|Fresh session clean run|Stranger-test passed (structure+README+seeded)
Definitions|Implicit|Stated|Stated, justified, edge cases decided (FY! deaths! zeroes!)
Statistical honesty|Point estimates bare|Uncertainty shown|Uncertainty + fairness handled (funnel/CI/standardisation as apt)
Safe outputs|None|Suppression applied|Suppression + secondary-leak check, automated
Git craft|One giant commit|Regular commits|Small commits, messages explain WHY, tidy history
Testing|None|Key functions tested|Tests + inter-stage assertions; a sabotage run fails loudly
Report quality|Numbers dumped|Clear to analysts|Clear to non-analysts; inline stats; styled; alt text
AI discipline|Undisclosed / unverified|Logged|Logged + verified + reflection with real specifics
Reflection|Absent|Present|Insightful: changes your future workflow, quotably
::p
16+ = portfolio-lead ready. 12–15 = one focused revision pass. Below 12 = the gaps are your Lesson 101 plan, which is the system working. When you're done: pin it, write the README's headline chart, and go back to Lesson 100 — you now have the evidence that statement needs.

That's the course. One hundred and five lessons ago you'd never written a query; you now build verified, reproducible, honestly-communicated analytical pipelines with a documented AI workflow. The remaining distance to "professional" is repetition and community — both scheduled in Lesson 101. Off you go: Barsetshire's data isn't going to analyse itself.
::quiz
Q The AILOG + reflection exists primarily because…
- Anthropic requires it
* Documented, verified, critically-evaluated AI use is itself the employable skill — and the log is the evidence you can discuss under interview pressure
- AI output is always wrong
- It pads the repository
! "I use AI with a verification discipline, here's the paper trail, here's what it got wrong" is the 2026 differentiator. Undocumented assistance is a liability; documented judgement is a credential.
::quiz
Q Rate-not-count is mandatory in the brief because…
- Counts are confidential
* Practices differ hugely in list size — raw counts would rank size, not need; per-1,000 rates (right denominators!) enable the comparison
- Rates are easier to compute
- The rubric says so
! Lesson 37's founding principle, now load-bearing: comparisons require denominators. Choosing the RIGHT one (65+ list size, not total) is part of the definitions criterion.
::ex
Write your Stage-boundary assertion block for this capstone: after the extract (03 tables joined), before analysis — covering grain, join fan-out, denominator sanity, and rate bounds.
~~~r
stopifnot(
  # grain: one row per practice after aggregation
  !any(duplicated(practice_rates$practice_code)),
  # fan-out guard: patients didn't multiply through joins
  nrow(extract) == n_distinct(extract$patient_id),
  # denominators: every practice has a positive 65+ list size
  all(practice_rates$list_65plus > 0),
  # rates: humanly possible (per 1,000, 65+ emergency admissions)
  all(practice_rates$rate_per_1000 >= 0),
  all(practice_rates$rate_per_1000 < 1000)
)
# Each failure names its line: the debugging starts WHERE it fails.
::key
- One question, full RAP dress, split deliberately: unaided core, AI-assisted periphery, everything verified.
- AILOG + honest reflection = the credential; the rubric (/20) = your revision plan.
- 105 lessons in: you build verified, reproducible, honest pipelines. Now repeat until fluent.
